The Symmetry Argument: Prior Inability and Prior Power
Maturidi's symmetry argument against prior inability preventing action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if prior inability prevents the act at the time of power, then by symmetry prior power would necessitate the act at the time of inability, leading to an impossibility.

Core Idea
Causal symmetry breaks down: prior inability and prior power produce opposite necessities.

Maturidi presents a logical argument against the notion that prior inability prevents action when power exists. He uses a symmetry argument: if inability prior to the act could block the act when power arrives, then by the same logic, prior power would force the act when inability arrives. Since the latter is impossible (an act cannot occur with inability), the former is also impossible. This clever reductio ad absurdum reveals the flaw in extending causal powers across different states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the structure of the argument.
Notice the 'if...then...' form.
2
Identify the hypothetical
The argument starts with a conditional: if prior inability prevents the act at the time of power.
3
Understand the symmetry
Realize that the argument treats prior inability and prior power as analogous in causation.
The consequence is that prior power would necessitate the act at time of inability.
4
Recognize the impossibility
The consequence is impossible, so the original premise must be impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المسألة سوى ما قدمنا ذكره من الأدلة أنه لو كان العجز المتقدم يمنع الفعل لوقت القدرة ليجب أن تكون القدرة المتقدمة توجب الفعل لوقت العجز، وفي إحالة ذلك إحالة الأول. ولو كان الفعل يقع لفقد القدرة لكان كلما دام دام الفعل؛ إذ أسباب الأشياء لما هي لها، كلما دامت أوجبت دوامها، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالوجود معها.
And in the issue, besides what we have mentioned of the proofs, is that if prior inability prevents the act at the time of power, then prior power would necessitate the act at the time of inability, and in the impossibility of that is the impossibility of the first. And if the act occurs due to the loss of power, then whenever [the loss] persists, the act persists; because the causes of things, as long as they persist, necessitate their persistence, and in that is the necessary assertion of existence with them.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents an additional proof (dalīl) concerning the relationship between inability ('ajz), power (qudra), and the act. It argues by hypothetical: if prior inability prevented the act at the time when power exists, then by symmetry prior power would necessitate the act at the time of inability. Since the latter is impossible (because inability and act cannot coexist), the former must also be impossible. Then a second argument: if the act occurs because of loss of power, then as long as that loss continues, the act should continue. But causes, when they persist, necessitate the persistence of their effects. This would imply that the act always coexists with the loss of power, which is absurd. The passage guards against mistakenly extending causal symmetry across different states.

Key Terms
عجز
قدرة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that prior inability and prior power are symmetrical in causation, so if one blocks, the other must necessitate.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the symmetry leads to an impossibility, so the initial claim about inability blocking must be false. The asymmetry lies in the states: power cannot necessitate act when inability is present.

Checkpoint
What does the passage claim would happen if prior power necessitated the act at the time of inability?
A) The act would occur despite inability.
B) It would be impossible, just as the original claim is impossible.
C) Power would be unnecessary.
D) Inability would be removed.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's symmetry argument shows that extending causal logic across different states leads to absurdity, thus refuting the claim that prior inability can prevent action at the time of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you holding onto an excuse that your past failures or weaknesses prevent you from obeying Allah now? This argument strips away that excuse. What act of obedience have you been postponing because you think your history makes it impossible?
?
If you believe that Allah's power is not limited by prior conditions, how does that reshape your prayer for strength in a recurring sin? Do you truly trust that at this very moment, you have the capacity to choose right, regardless of your past inability?
?
The symmetry argument exposes the absurdity of blaming prior states. Is there a duty or repentance you have been delaying because you feel 'not ready'? What would it mean to act now, as if your past inability has no hold on your present power?

The Persistence Argument: Act Occurs Due to Loss of Power
Maturidi's persistence argument against act being caused by loss of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if the act occurs due to the loss of power, then the act would persist as long as the loss persists, which leads to the absurd conclusion that existence is eternal with the loss of power.

Core Idea
If loss of power causes the act, then the act would never cease, contradicting temporal existence.

Maturidi presents a second argument against the idea that the act occurs because of the loss of power. He argues that if the act is caused by the absence of power, then as long as that absence continues, the act should continue. Since causes necessitate their effects continuously, this would imply that the act always exists together with the loss of power, which is impossible. This argument further undermines the view that power and inability have a straightforward causal relationship with actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the causal reasoning.
2
Identify the causal claim
The passage assumes the act occurs due to loss of power (fqd al-qudra).
3
Apply the principle of causal persistence
Causes necessitate their effects as long as they persist. The loss of power is continuous.
4
Recognize the absurd consequence
The act would be eternal with the loss of power, which is false.
This refutes the initial claim.

The Meaning of Existence in Power and Act
Maturidi questions the meaning of 'existence' to refute the opponent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that power cannot coexist with an act by questioning what 'existence' means.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'existence' determines whether power can coexist with the act.

The opponent argues that power cannot be with an act because God sees the act as existent. But what does 'existent' mean? Maturidi probes three possible meanings, showing that each fails to support the opponent's claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the argument's flow. Pay attention to the three meanings of 'existence' that are examined.
2
Identify the three meanings
The passage considers three interpretations of 'existence': completion of the act, part of the act, or the act itself. Notice how each is addressed.
The opponent's position is cornered by these distinctions.
3
Understand the refutation for 'completion'
If 'existence' means completion, then the act is past. The opponent's claim that 'it is possible for inability to be non-existent' is shown to be false, because inability with a non-existent act is not impossible.
This invalidates the opponent's reasoning.
4
Reflect on the logic
Consider why the other meanings (part or in the act) are not addressed explicitly but are implied to fail. The commentator clarifies that the opponent's initial claim is unfounded regardless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعم أن القدرة محال كونها مع الفعل، لأن الله يراه موجودا، ومحال كون القدرة مع الفعل الموجود. قيل : عنيت بالوجود الفراغ منه أو منه أو هو فيه ؟ فإن قال: الفراغ منه، بان كذبه عند من يعقل، وأبطل قوله : يجوز أن يكون في ذلك بالبدل ـ وهو العجز - معدوما. ولم يجب القول بإحالة العجز مع الفعل المعدوم، وإن كان يراه معدوما، إذ لم يكن العدم منقضيا بل هو مشغول به. ثم يقال له : الله يواليه ويُعاديه مع فعله أو قبله أو بعده؟ فإن قال: قبله، أحاله؛ وإن قال : بعده أبطل قوله : يراه موجودًا، لأنه يحقق وجود فعل العداوة والولاية، ولا عداوة ولا ولاية. وإن قال : في حاله قيل: صار السبب مع المسبب موجودا، ولم ينف كون الفعل ، وأن كان يرى الولاية والعداوة موجودتين، فمثله القدرة.
Then he claimed that it is impossible for power to be with the act, because Allah sees it as existent, and it is impossible for power to be with an existent act. It was said: Did you mean by 'existence' its completion, or something from it, or it being in it? If he says: its completion, then his lie becomes clear to those who understand, and his statement is invalidated: that it is permissible for in that [case] by replacement—i.e., inability—to be non-existent. And the statement that inability is impossible with the non-existent act is not required, even if he sees it as non-existent, since the non-existence is not terminated but rather occupied by it. Then it is said to him: Does Allah cause him to be loyal and hostile with his act, before it, or after it? If he says: before it, it is impossible; if he says: after it, his statement that He sees it as existent is invalidated, because He would be realizing the existence of the act of enmity and loyalty, without enmity or loyalty. And if he says: during its state, it is said: the cause becomes existent with the effect, and it does not negate the act, and even if he sees loyalty and enmity as existent, similarly is power.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that power cannot coexist with an act because God sees the act as existent. The refutation first questions what is meant by 'existence'—whether it is the completion of the act, part of it, or the act itself. If the opponent says 'completion', the refutation shows it is false because inability would then be non-existent, but impossibility of inability with non-existent act is not required. Then a parallel argument is made using divine will regarding loyalty and enmity: when does God cause them? If before the act, impossible; if after, the opponent's claim about God seeing the act as existent fails; if during, cause and effect coexist, analogous to power and act. Thus power can indeed coexist with the act. The passage guards against misinterpreting God's knowledge of existence as precluding causal coevality.

Key Terms

فعل
وجود
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God sees an act as existent, then power cannot also be present with the act.
Correction: The meaning of 'existence' matters: if it means completion, the act is past and power could have been present before; other meanings do not preclude coexistence.

Checkpoint
What is the first meaning of 'existence' that the refutation examines?
A) The act itself
B) Completion of the act
C) Part of the act
D) The potential of the act

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim fails because the meaning of 'existence' is ambiguous; when properly defined, the objection collapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Parallel of Loyalty and Enmity
Maturidi uses loyalty/enmity as an analogy to show coexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses divine will regarding loyalty and enmity to further refute the claim that power cannot coexist with an act.

Core Idea
God's causing loyalty and enmity parallels power and act, showing coexistence is possible.

Maturidi presents a parallel argument: if God causes loyalty and enmity, when does He do so? The same three possibilities (before, after, or during the act) are examined, and the only coherent option entails that cause and effect coexist, just as power and act can.

Guided Path
1
Read the parallel argument
Read the second part of the passage where Maturidi shifts to the example of loyalty and enmity. Notice how it mirrors the earlier analysis.
The structure is the same: three possibilities are offered.
2
Examine the three timings
The three timings are: before the act, after the act, or during the act. See how each is rejected or accepted.
3
Understand the refutation for 'after'
If after, then God would be realizing the existence of the act of enmity/loyalty without any act existing—impossible.
This directly undermines the opponent's view.
4
Connect to power and act
The parallel concludes: just as cause (God's will) and effect (loyalty/enmity) coexist during the act, so power can coexist with the act.
This is the key analogical reasoning.

The Meaning of Existence in Refuting Al-Ka'bi
Maturidi questions the meaning of 'existence' to refute al-Ka'bi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by questioning the ambiguous meaning of 'existence' in the claim that power cannot coexist with action.

Core Idea
The key is to ask what 'existence' of an action really means.

Al-Ka'bi claimed that power cannot coexist with action because God sees the action as existent. Maturidi immediately challenges the ambiguity of the term 'existence'. By asking whether 'existence' means completion, emanation, or engagement, he exposes the flaw in al-Ka'bi's reasoning. This lesson focuses on that initial refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that Maturidi begins by stating al-Ka'bi's claim, then poses a question.
2
Identify the three possible meanings of 'existence'
Maturidi offers three interpretations: completion of the action, emanation from the agent, or engagement in it. List them and see how each leads to a contradiction.
The three meanings are crucial for the refutation.
3
Understand the response to 'completion'
If 'existence' means completion, then power would be after the action, but power is needed during, not after. Al-Ka'bi's alternative of incapacity is also refuted.
You see how logical questioning dismantles the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعم أن القدرة محال كونها مع الفعل، لأن الله يراه موجودا، ومحال كون القدرة مع الفعل الموجود.
قيل : عنيت بالوجود الفراغ منه أو منه أو هو فيه؟ فإن قال: الفراغ منه، بان كذبه عند من يعقل، وأبطل قوله: يجوز أن يكون في ذلك بالبدل - وهو العجز - معدوما. ولم يجب القول بإحالة العجز مع [الفعل] المعدوم، وإن كان يراه معدوما، إذ لم يكن العدم منقضيا بل هو مشغول به. ثم يقال له: الله يواليه ويعاديه مع فعله أو قبله أو بعده؟ فإن قال: قبله، أحاله؛ وإن قال: بعده أبطل قوله: يراه موجودا، لأنه يحقق وجود فعل العداوة والولاية، ولا عداوة ولا ولاية. وإن قال: في حاله قيل: صار السبب مع المسبب موجودا، ولم ينف كون الفعل، وأن كان يرى الولاية والعداوة موجودتين، فمثله القدرة.
وأيضا إنه على أي حال معه يراه يرى القدرة معه؛ على ما يرى إلقاء الشيء وإخراجه مع خروج ذلك وإلقائه، ولم يبطل حق الإلقاء والإخراج بما يرى الشيء على ما يراه فمثله الذي ذكرت. وإذ جاز أن يراه موجودا ومعه الأسباب كلها، ولم يبعد ذلك، فمثله القوة، بل كذلك يجب أن يرى القدرة، كما كذلك يجب أن يرى الفعل مع الأسباب.
وجملته أن للفعل وقت العدم وهو قبله، ووقت الفناء وهو بعده، ووقت الوجود وهو في حاله، ولا محالة يراه الله مع أحواله على ما ذكر لا غير؛ وكذلك الأوقات التي تقع فيها الأفعال والأمكنة، فعلى ذلك الأسباب، فمثله القوة يراها معدومة قبله، فانية بعده، موجودة معه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he claimed that power is impossible to be with action, because God sees it as existent, and it is impossible for power to be with the existent action. It was said: Do you mean by existence the completion of it, or from it, or it being in it? If he says: the completion of it, his falsehood becomes clear to those who reason, and his statement 'it is possible that in that by substitution—which is incapacity—it be nonexistent' is invalidated. And he did not answer the statement that incapacity with the nonexistent [action] is impossible, even if he sees it as nonexistent, since the non-existence is not terminated but rather occupied by it. Then it is said to him: Does God make allegiance and enmity with his action, or before it, or after it? If he says: before it, he makes it impossible; if he says: after it, he invalidates his statement 'He sees it as existent', because he confirms the existence of the act of enmity and allegiance, and there is no enmity nor allegiance. And if he says: at its moment, it is said: the cause together with the effect is existent, and it does not negate the occurrence of action, even if he sees allegiance and enmity as existent, so likewise power. And also, in whatever state with it He sees, He sees power with it; just as He sees the throwing of a thing and its extraction with the emergence of that and its throwing, and He does not invalidate the reality of throwing and extraction by what He sees of the thing as He sees it, so likewise what you mentioned. And since it is permissible that He sees it existent and with it all causes, and that is not improbable, so likewise power, rather it is necessary that He see power, just as it is necessary that He see action with causes. The summary: action has a time of non-existence which is before it, and a time of perishing which is after it, and a time of existence which is at its moment, and inevitably God sees it with its states as mentioned, no other; and likewise the times in which actions occur and places, and likewise causes, so likewise power He sees it nonexistent before it, perishing after it, existent with it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that power cannot coexist with action because God sees the action as existent and power with an existent is impossible. Maturidi responds by first asking what exactly is meant by 'existence' of the action: whether it means the action is completed, or that it is emanating from the agent, or that the agent is engaged in it. If the opponent means 'completed', then he is refuted because power is needed during the action, not after. The opponent also falsely claims that incapacity (as substitute) can be nonexistent with the action. Then Maturidi uses the example of God's seeing of allegiance (wilaya) and enmity (’adawa): if God sees these with the action, then power can also be seen with action; if before or after, contradictions arise. He further illustrates with the example of throwing and extracting: we see the throw and the extracted thing simultaneously, and this does not negate the agent's power. The summary clarifies that action has three times: prior non-existence, after perishing, and present existence. Power is seen with action at its present time, just as causes are seen with their effects.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
فعل (fi'l)
وجود (wujud)
عدم ('adam)
فناء (fana')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power cannot coexist with action because God sees the action as existent.
Correction: The concept of 'existence' is ambiguous; if it means completion, power is after, but if it means being in the action, power coexists.

Checkpoint
What are the three possible meanings of 'existence' that Maturidi asks about?
A) Completion, emanation, engagement
B) Before, during, after
C) Power, incapacity, action
D) Seen, unseen, imagined

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that the claim depends on an ambiguous notion of 'existence', and that understanding what is meant leads to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you fail to do good, do you attribute it to your own weakness or to God's will? Are you using 'incapacity' as an excuse in a way that Maturidi's argument might challenge?
?
How often do you pause to consider the precise meaning of 'existence' when you think about your deeds before God? Is your awareness of God's presence limited to general belief, or does it shape the moment-by-moment consciousness of your actions?

Analogies for Simultaneity of Power and Action
Maturidi uses analogies to show that power coexists with action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses analogies of allegiance/enmity and throwing/extracting to demonstrate that power coexists with action.

Core Idea
If God sees allegiance with action, then power can also be seen with action.

After the initial linguistic refutation, Maturidi provides concrete analogies to illustrate that simultaneity of cause and effect is normal. He first asks about God's seeing of allegiance and enmity, then gives the example of throwing and extracting. Finally, he summarizes the three temporal states of action, confirming that power is seen with action in its present state. This lesson covers these analogies and the summary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: first analogy (allegiance/enmity), second analogy (throwing/extracting), and a summary.
2
Understand the first analogy
Maturidi asks about the timing of God seeing allegiance and enmity. If before or after, there is a contradiction; only at the moment of action does it make sense, implying coexistence.
This shows that cause and effect can be simultaneous.
3
Understand the second analogy
The example of throwing and extracting: we see the throw and the thrown object together, not sequentially. This further supports that power can be seen with action.
4
Learn the summary of three states
Maturidi concludes that action has three times: non-existence before, perishing after, and existence at its moment. Power is seen with action in its existent state.
This provides a clear temporal framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعم أن القدرة محال كونها مع الفعل، لأن الله يراه موجودا، ومحال كون القدرة مع الفعل الموجود.
قيل : عنيت بالوجود الفراغ منه أو منه أو هو فيه؟ فإن قال: الفراغ منه، بان كذبه عند من يعقل، وأبطل قوله: يجوز أن يكون في ذلك بالبدل - وهو العجز - معدوما. ولم يجب القول بإحالة العجز مع [الفعل] المعدوم، وإن كان يراه معدوما، إذ لم يكن العدم منقضيا بل هو مشغول به. ثم يقال له: الله يواليه ويعاديه مع فعله أو قبله أو بعده؟ فإن قال: قبله، أحاله؛ وإن قال: بعده أبطل قوله: يراه موجودا، لأنه يحقق وجود فعل العداوة والولاية، ولا عداوة ولا ولاية. وإن قال: في حاله قيل: صار السبب مع المسبب موجودا، ولم ينف كون الفعل، وأن كان يرى الولاية والعداوة موجودتين، فمثله القدرة.
وأيضا إنه على أي حال معه يراه يرى القدرة معه؛ على ما يرى إلقاء الشيء وإخراجه مع خروج ذلك وإلقائه، ولم يبطل حق الإلقاء والإخراج بما يرى الشيء على ما يراه فمثله الذي ذكرت. وإذ جاز أن يراه موجودا ومعه الأسباب كلها، ولم يبعد ذلك، فمثله القوة، بل كذلك يجب أن يرى القدرة، كما كذلك يجب أن يرى الفعل مع الأسباب.
وجملته أن للفعل وقت العدم وهو قبله، ووقت الفناء وهو بعده، ووقت الوجود وهو في حاله، ولا محالة يراه الله مع أحواله على ما ذكر لا غير؛ وكذلك الأوقات التي تقع فيها الأفعال والأمكنة، فعلى ذلك الأسباب، فمثله القوة يراها معدومة قبله، فانية بعده، موجودة معه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he claimed that power is impossible to be with action, because God sees it as existent, and it is impossible for power to be with the existent action. It was said: Do you mean by existence the completion of it, or from it, or it being in it? If he says: the completion of it, his falsehood becomes clear to those who reason, and his statement 'it is possible that in that by substitution—which is incapacity—it be nonexistent' is invalidated. And he did not answer the statement that incapacity with the nonexistent [action] is impossible, even if he sees it as nonexistent, since the non-existence is not terminated but rather occupied by it. Then it is said to him: Does God make allegiance and enmity with his action, or before it, or after it? If he says: before it, he makes it impossible; if he says: after it, he invalidates his statement 'He sees it as existent', because he confirms the existence of the act of enmity and allegiance, and there is no enmity nor allegiance. And if he says: at its moment, it is said: the cause together with the effect is existent, and it does not negate the occurrence of action, even if he sees allegiance and enmity as existent, so likewise power. And also, in whatever state with it He sees, He sees power with it; just as He sees the throwing of a thing and its extraction with the emergence of that and its throwing, and He does not invalidate the reality of throwing and extraction by what He sees of the thing as He sees it, so likewise what you mentioned. And since it is permissible that He sees it existent and with it all causes, and that is not improbable, so likewise power, rather it is necessary that He see power, just as it is necessary that He see action with causes. The summary: action has a time of non-existence which is before it, and a time of perishing which is after it, and a time of existence which is at its moment, and inevitably God sees it with its states as mentioned, no other; and likewise the times in which actions occur and places, and likewise causes, so likewise power He sees it nonexistent before it, perishing after it, existent with it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that power cannot coexist with action because God sees the action as existent and power with an existent is impossible. Maturidi responds by first asking what exactly is meant by 'existence' of the action: whether it means the action is completed, or that it is emanating from the agent, or that the agent is engaged in it. If the opponent means 'completed', then he is refuted because power is needed during the action, not after. The opponent also falsely claims that incapacity (as substitute) can be nonexistent with the action. Then Maturidi uses the example of God's seeing of allegiance (wilaya) and enmity (’adawa): if God sees these with the action, then power can also be seen with action; if before or after, contradictions arise. He further illustrates with the example of throwing and extracting: we see the throw and the extracted thing simultaneously, and this does not negate the agent's power. The summary clarifies that action has three times: prior non-existence, after perishing, and present existence. Power is seen with action at its present time, just as causes are seen with their effects.

ولاية (wilaya)
عداوة ('adawa)
إلقاء (ilqa')
إخراج (ikhraj)
فناء (fana')
قدرة (qudra)
فعل (fi'l)
وجود (wujud)
عدم ('adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power cannot coexist with action because the action is seen as existent by God.
Correction: Through analogies and temporal analysis, Maturidi shows that power coexists with action in its present state, just as causes coexist with effects.

Checkpoint
What are the three temporal states of action mentioned in the summary?
A) Past, present, future
B) Non-existence, perishing, existence
C) Cause, effect, reason
D) Power, incapacity, action

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates through analogies and a temporal analysis that power necessarily coexists with action in its present state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you truly believe that your power and God's power are simultaneous, or do you secretly think you did it alone? How would living with this simultaneity change your humility? Commit to thanking Allah for one specific action today, acknowledging His power with yours.
?
In the analogy, the cause (like allegiance) and effect (action) are seen together. Think of a sin you struggle with: do you see the cause (desire) as separate from the act, making you feel powerless? How does the lesson’s truth that God sees power with action (not before or after) give you both responsibility and hope? What step will you take to align your will with His?

Invalidation of Command and Prohibition Without Power
Maturidi argues against independent power by showing it invalidates command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if a person is not subject to divine command in their current state, the entire system of command and prohibition collapses.

Core Idea
Divine commands lose meaning if not addressed to a capable agent.

This passage presents a key Maturidi argument against the view that human power (qudra) exists independently of the act. Maturidi shows that if a disbeliever were not commanded to believe nor forbidden from disbelief in his current state, then no command or prohibition would ever apply to him, nullifying divine law. The argument uses a reduction ad absurdum to defend the necessity of linking divine commands to the state of the agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First read the Arabic passage without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
Find where Maturidi states the problematic view and where he draws the conclusion of nullification.
The key phrase is 'فإذا لم يكن...'.
3
Reflect on the theological stakes
Ask yourself: Why does this argument matter for the doctrine of qadar? How does it connect to human responsibility?
This argument defends the idea that commands are tied to the agent's state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جميعا . ثم تأويله قول المسلمين على وجه يعلم كل مسلم أن ذلك لم يخطر بباله، بل لا يحتمله عقل كل أحد لو ألزم بجهد : أن يكون كافر ليس بمنهى عن كفره، وليس بمأمور في حاله. فإذا لم يكن في وقته منهيًا مأموراً لما هو فيه ولا لضده، وهو كذلك في الوقت الثاني والثالث إلى ما لا نهاية له؛ وفي ذلك بطلان الأمر والنهي على التحقيق، لأنه يكون الأمر بالشيء للوقت الثاني والنهي عن ضده، وهو في ذلك ليس بمؤتمر بالأمر ولا مرتكب النهي، لأنه ليس في ذلك؛ وكذا في كل وقت، فيبطل حق الأمر والنهي عن الفعل أبدا، ويرجع إلى غير حال الائتمار والارتكاب، وذلك بعيد.
All together. Then its interpretation is the saying of the Muslims in a way that every Muslim knows that that did not occur to his mind, nay, the intellect of everyone would not bear it if he were compelled with effort: that a disbeliever is not forbidden from his disbelief, nor is he commanded in his state. So if at his time he is neither forbidden nor commanded for what he is in nor for its opposite, and he is likewise in the second and third time ad infinitum; in that is the invalidation of command and prohibition in reality, because the command becomes for the thing for the second time and the prohibition of its opposite, while he in that is not acting upon the command nor committing the prohibition, because he is not in that; and likewise in every time, so the right of command and prohibition regarding the act becomes invalid forever, and it returns to a state other than that of complying and committing, and that is far.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument against the view that human power (qudra) can exist independently of the act for which it is intended. Maturidi begins by describing the interpretation of a certain group of Muslims (likely Mu'tazilites) which no sound intellect can accept: that a disbeliever is neither commanded to believe nor forbidden from disbelief in his current state. If that were the case, then at no time—past, present, or future—would he be subject to command or prohibition regarding his state or its opposite. This would completely nullify the entire system of divine commands and prohibitions, because the command for faith and the prohibition of disbelief would be directed at someone who is not in a position to comply or violate them. The argument concludes that such a view is far from sound reason.

Key Terms
الأمر والنهي
الكفر
الإيمان
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human power (qudra) exists independently of any particular act.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if power existed independently, then divine commands and prohibitions would be nullified, because a person could be commanded without being in a state to comply.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim would be the consequence if a disbeliever is not commanded or forbidden in his state?
A) It would make the disbeliever excused.
B) It would nullify the entire system of command and prohibition.
C) It would prove that God does not want all to believe.
D) It would show that human power is limited.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that divine command and prohibition are only meaningful when addressed to a person in a state where they can comply; otherwise, the entire system collapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes feel that a particular command of Allah (e.g., to pray on time, to avoid a sin) is not relevant to you right now? How does this lesson expose the danger of that feeling, and what will you do to align your heart with the command's immediacy?
?
When you fail to obey a command, do you ever excuse yourself by thinking 'I just couldn't do it'? How does Maturidi's argument remind you that Allah's command implies you have the capacity, and what does that oblige you to do in your next moment of weakness?
?
If you believe that Allah's prohibitions are not addressing you in your current state of sin, doesn't that make His commands meaningless for you? What step will you take today to recognize that you are addressed by every command and prohibition in this very moment?

The Self-Objection on Pharaoh and God's Foreknowledge
Maturidi presents and answers a self-objection about Pharaoh's power and God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that human free will could nullify God's foreknowledge.

Core Idea
Power is for something other than the known non-existent act.

A common objection against divine foreknowledge is that if humans had real power, they could falsify God's knowledge. Maturidi raises this objection himself and shows why it fails, using the example of Pharaoh. This lesson examines his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the self-objection
Read the passage where Maturidi presents the objection about Pharaoh. Notice the conditional: if he could believe, he could nullify God's knowledge.
2
Understand the answer
Maturidi's answer is that power is for something other than the act that is known not to occur. Note the phrase 'القدرة على غير الإيمان الذي هو المعلوم أنه لا يكون'.
Power does not extend to the non-existent act.
3
Notice the parallel objection
Maturidi then raises a similar objection about God's power to create but not to nullify His own knowledge, and dismisses it as analogous.
4
Reflect on the logic
Why does the objection fail? Because power is always limited to what is possible given divine foreknowledge. The act in question is not possible because it is known not to occur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بأمر فرعون إنه لو كان يقدر على الإيمان لكان يقدر على إبطال علم الله، وهذا في فرعون وكل من في علم الله أنه لا يؤمن . فأجاب بأن ذا لا يجب، لأن القدرة على غير الإيمان الذي هو المعلوم أنه لا يكون؛ ولو لزمنا ذلك في القوة ليلزمكم في الأمر. ثم عارض بقدرة الله على إنشاء العالم ليحال، من غير أن يجوز الوصف بالقدرة على إبطال علمه، فمثله الأول. ثم عارض حسينًا بالإطلاق، إنه أطلق بينه وبين الإيمان . أتقول في أنه أطلق إبطال علم الله ؟ ثم قال: الله عالم أن لو كان كيف يكون، فلو كان لم يكن يخرج من علم الله . قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ووجه الاعتبار به ليس على ما قدر، ولكن بما يقول بالأصلح. ومعلوم أن الله لو لم يكن ملكه على ما ملكه لم يكن ليقدر أن يضل من أضله ويمنع من يمنعه عن طاعة رسوله، وكان ذلك أقل للغواية وأقرب إلى الطاعة. فثبت أن القول بالأصلح باطل مضمحل . والثاني أنه إذا أخبر أنه لا يؤمن بالله، وقد علم ذلك، وهو عدوه، وإقدار العدو على تسفيه المقدر وتقويته على نقض ملكه وإبطال ربوبيته خارج عن حد الحكمة ببديهة العقل. مع ما فيه تمكين عدوه لأعظم مئة له عليه بأن يقول : لي عليك كل منة، إذ ملكتني نقض ربوبتك، بما لا يكون رب جاهل، وقويتني على إزالة حكمتك، بما لا يكون حكيم كذوب وقد جعلت لي القدرة على ذلك، وبذلك أمرتني، وقد تعلم أني لو شئت لفعلت؛ فتمت
Then he opposed himself with the matter of Pharaoh: if he were able to believe, he would be able to nullify God's knowledge. This applies to Pharaoh and everyone whom God knows will not believe. So he answered that this does not necessarily follow, because power is for other than the faith which is known not to occur; and if that were necessary for us regarding power, it would be necessary for you regarding command. Then he opposed with God's power to create the world to change, without permitting the description of power to nullify His knowledge, so it is like the first. Then he opposed Husayn by absolute freedom, that he claimed freedom between him and faith. Do you say that he claimed to nullify God's knowledge? Then he said: God knows how it would be if it were, and if it were, it would not leave God's knowledge. {Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said:} And the aspect of consideration is not as he thought, but rather according to what he says about the doctrine of al-aslah (the best). And it is known that if God's dominion were not as He possesses it, He would not be able to mislead whom He misleads and prevent whom He prevents from obedience to His messenger, and that would be less conducive to error and closer to obedience. So it is established that the doctrine of al-aslah is false and null. And the second is that when He informs that he does not believe in God, while He knows that, and he is His enemy, empowering the enemy to mock the Determiner and strengthen him to nullify His dominion and invalidate His lordship is outside the bounds of wisdom by intuitive reason. Along with what it involves of enabling His enemy to have the greatest favor over Him by saying: 'I have every favor upon You, since You gave me the power to nullify Your lordship, which would not be the case for an ignorant Lord, and You strengthened me to remove Your wisdom, which would not be the case for a lying wise one, and You have made me powerful over that, and by that You commanded me, and You know that if I wished I would have done it.' So it is complete.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage contains several objections and answers. First, Maturidi raises a self-objection: if Pharaoh had the power to believe, he could nullify God's knowledge that he will not believe. The answer is that power is for something other than the known non-existent faith, and the same objection can be turned against the opponent's own position on command. Second, an objection about God's power to create the world without being able to nullify His own knowledge is dismissed as similar. Third, an objection from Husayn al-Najjar about absolute freedom of choice is countered: even if freedom is claimed, it does not undermine God's foreknowledge. Then Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (that God must always do what is best for His creatures). He argues that if God's dominion were limited by this principle, He would not be able to mislead or prevent—things He actually does—so the doctrine leads to a contradiction. Finally, he presents an argument from reason: empowering an enemy to nullify divine lordship is absurd; the enemy could then claim a favor over God, implying ignorance or lying, which are impossible for God. Thus the entire position is refuted.

Key Terms
القدرة
علم الله
فرعون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a person can choose freely, they can make God's knowledge false.
Correction: Power is for something other than the known non-existent act; God's knowledge is of what will actually happen, not of what could happen contrary to His knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the objection about Pharaoh does not stand?
A) Because Pharaoh did not actually have power.
B) Because power is for something other than the known non-existent faith.
C) Because God's knowledge cannot be nullified.
D) Because Pharaoh was forced.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that power does not extend to acts that are known not to occur, preserving God's foreknowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin that you know God has forbidden, do you ever secretly think you can 'show God' or change His knowledge of your outcome? How does this lesson confront that pride?
?
This lesson teaches that your power to choose is limited to what God knows is possible. Does that humble you or provoke you? Will you let it drive you to submit rather than rebel?
?
If you truly believed that your every choice is within the scope of God's unchanging knowledge, would you still gamble with disobedience? What will you change today to align your will with His decree?

The Objection of Husayn al-Najjar on Absolute Freedom
Maturidi counters an objection from Husayn al-Najjar about absolute free will and God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's response to Husayn al-Najjar's claim that absolute free will would nullify God's knowledge.

Core Idea
God knows how it would be if it were, and it remains within His knowledge.

A contemporary of Maturidi, Husayn al-Najjar, argued that if humans have absolute freedom, they could nullify God's foreknowledge. Maturidi counters by affirming that God knows all possibilities, even those that never occur. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the exchange
Read the passage where Maturidi presents Husayn's claim of absolute freedom and his own response.
The Arabic word 'أطلق' means 'claimed absolute freedom'.
2
Understand the question
Maturidi asks: does this claim imply nullifying God's knowledge?
3
Analyze the answer
Maturidi says God knows how it would be if it were, and it would not leave His knowledge. This means God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities.
The phrase 'الله عالم أن لو كان كيف يكون' shows God's knowledge of hypotheticals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بأمر فرعون إنه لو كان يقدر على الإيمان لكان يقدر على إبطال علم الله، وهذا في فرعون وكل من في علم الله أنه لا يؤمن . فأجاب بأن ذا لا يجب، لأن القدرة على غير الإيمان الذي هو المعلوم أنه لا يكون؛ ولو لزمنا ذلك في القوة ليلزمكم في الأمر. ثم عارض بقدرة الله على إنشاء العالم ليحال، من غير أن يجوز الوصف بالقدرة على إبطال علمه، فمثله الأول. ثم عارض حسينًا بالإطلاق، إنه أطلق بينه وبين الإيمان . أتقول في أنه أطلق إبطال علم الله ؟ ثم قال: الله عالم أن لو كان كيف يكون، فلو كان لم يكن يخرج من علم الله . قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ووجه الاعتبار به ليس على ما قدر، ولكن بما يقول بالأصلح. ومعلوم أن الله لو لم يكن ملكه على ما ملكه لم يكن ليقدر أن يضل من أضله ويمنع من يمنعه عن طاعة رسوله، وكان ذلك أقل للغواية وأقرب إلى الطاعة. فثبت أن القول بالأصلح باطل مضمحل . والثاني أنه إذا أخبر أنه لا يؤمن بالله، وقد علم ذلك، وهو عدوه، وإقدار العدو على تسفيه المقدر وتقويته على نقض ملكه وإبطال ربوبيته خارج عن حد الحكمة ببديهة العقل. مع ما فيه تمكين عدوه لأعظم مئة له عليه بأن يقول : لي عليك كل منة، إذ ملكتني نقض ربوبتك، بما لا يكون رب جاهل، وقويتني على إزالة حكمتك، بما لا يكون حكيم كذوب وقد جعلت لي القدرة على ذلك، وبذلك أمرتني، وقد تعلم أني لو شئت لفعلت؛ فتمت
Then he opposed himself with the matter of Pharaoh: if he were able to believe, he would be able to nullify God's knowledge. This applies to Pharaoh and everyone whom God knows will not believe. So he answered that this does not necessarily follow, because power is for other than the faith which is known not to occur; and if that were necessary for us regarding power, it would be necessary for you regarding command. Then he opposed with God's power to create the world to change, without permitting the description of power to nullify His knowledge, so it is like the first. Then he opposed Husayn by absolute freedom, that he claimed freedom between him and faith. Do you say that he claimed to nullify God's knowledge? Then he said: God knows how it would be if it were, and if it were, it would not leave God's knowledge. {Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said:} And the aspect of consideration is not as he thought, but rather according to what he says about the doctrine of al-aslah (the best). And it is known that if God's dominion were not as He possesses it, He would not be able to mislead whom He misleads and prevent whom He prevents from obedience to His messenger, and that would be less conducive to error and closer to obedience. So it is established that the doctrine of al-aslah is false and null. And the second is that when He informs that he does not believe in God, while He knows that, and he is His enemy, empowering the enemy to mock the Determiner and strengthen him to nullify His dominion and invalidate His lordship is outside the bounds of wisdom by intuitive reason. Along with what it involves of enabling His enemy to have the greatest favor over Him by saying: 'I have every favor upon You, since You gave me the power to nullify Your lordship, which would not be the case for an ignorant Lord, and You strengthened me to remove Your wisdom, which would not be the case for a lying wise one, and You have made me powerful over that, and by that You commanded me, and You know that if I wished I would have done it.' So it is complete.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage contains several objections and answers. First, Maturidi raises a self-objection: if Pharaoh had the power to believe, he could nullify God's knowledge that he will not believe. The answer is that power is for something other than the known non-existent faith, and the same objection can be turned against the opponent's own position on command. Second, an objection about God's power to create the world without being able to nullify His own knowledge is dismissed as similar. Third, an objection from Husayn al-Najjar about absolute freedom of choice is countered: even if freedom is claimed, it does not undermine God's foreknowledge. Then Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (that God must always do what is best for His creatures). He argues that if God's dominion were limited by this principle, He would not be able to mislead or prevent—things He actually does—so the doctrine leads to a contradiction. Finally, he presents an argument from reason: empowering an enemy to nullify divine lordship is absurd; the enemy could then claim a favor over God, implying ignorance or lying, which are impossible for God. Thus the entire position is refuted.

Key Terms
الإطلاق
علم الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absolute free will means God does not know future choices.
Correction: God knows all possibilities, including hypothetical choices that are never made.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi respond to Husayn's claim of absolute freedom?
A) He agrees that freedom nullifies God's knowledge.
B) He says God does not know future free acts.
C) He says God knows how it would be if it were, and it remains within His knowledge.
D) He dismisses the claim as irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities, preserving both divine foreknowledge and human free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice that turns out badly, do you ever comfort yourself by thinking 'God didn't see that coming'? How does Maturidi's argument that God knows even the hypothetical paths you did not take challenge that thought?
?
If you truly believed that God knows every possible version of your life — every path you could have taken — how would that change the way you view your past mistakes? Would you still feel the same regret or shame?
?
Husayn al-Najjar's claim of absolute freedom seems appealing, but Maturidi warns it could imply nullifying God's knowledge. In what areas of your life do you act as if your choices are completely independent of God's knowledge, and how can you realign that with this lesson?

Refutation of al-Aslah and the Absurdity of Empowering the Enemy
Maturidi refutes the doctrine of al-aslah and argues that empowering the enemy is irrational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two-part refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah and the absurdity of giving power to God's enemy.

Core Idea
The doctrine of al-aslah is false; empowering the enemy is irrational.

Maturidi builds on the previous objections to directly refute the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God must always do what is best for creatures. He argues that this principle would limit God's power to mislead and prevent, which He does. He then presents a second independent argument: empowering an enemy like Pharaoh to nullify divine attributes is absurd. This lesson explores both arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Read the refutation of al-aslah. Maturidi states that if God's dominion were not as it is, He would not be able to mislead or prevent, which He does.
The phrase 'لو لم يكن ملكه على ما ملكه' is key.
2
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the doctrine of al-aslah is false and null ('باطل مضمحل').
3
Read the second argument
Read the argument about empowering the enemy. Maturidi says it is outside the bounds of wisdom to empower an enemy to nullify divine lordship.
4
Examine the enemy's speech
The enemy could claim a favor over God, saying God gave him power to nullify His lordship, which would imply ignorance or lying in God.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

?

?
The enemy in the passage claims a favor over God because he was empowered. Do you ever act as if God owes you something, or that you have done Him a favor by your obedience? Reflect on His absolute lordship and your complete dependence. How can you cultivate genuine humility in gratitude?

Objection about Preference Between Opposites and Its Answer
Imam Maturidi responds to an objection about why one chooses one of two opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's response to the objection that choice involves doing whatever one wishes, and the necessity of choosing what is most appropriate.

Core Idea
Choice is not doing whatever one wants; it is selecting what is most appropriate.

Imam Maturidi addresses a common objection about human choice: if someone has power over both faith and disbelief, why do they choose one over the other? The objector claims that if they could only do one, they would be compelled, but choice is established. The Imam clarifies that choice does not mean arbitrary will, but selecting what is most appropriate. He then extends the argument to God, showing that the objection is invalid on both theological views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the objection and response.
2
Identify the objection and the Imam's critique
Notice how the objector frames the issue: if someone can do both, why do they choose one? The Imam says the objector has deviated from the real question.
The real question is about the reason for preference, not ability.
3
Understand the condition of choice
The Imam states: 'ليس شرط الاختيار أن يفعل ما شاء، ولكن يختار الأولى به أن يفعل.' Choice is not about doing whatever one wishes, but about choosing what is most appropriate for the agent.
This is the key distinction.
4
Consider the parallel objection about God
The objector raises a similar point about God. The Imam explains why it fails on both views: either God's actions are from His essence and thus not subject to 'why', or they are for benefit and not questioned.
5
Reflect on reliance on God
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله', reminding us that all power is from God.
This underscores the theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالذي قدر على الإيمان والكفر، فلِمَ فَعَل أحدهما دون الأخر؟ فزعم أن ذا محال، لأنه لو كان لا يأتي إلا بواحد كان يكون مضطرًا، وقد ثبت الاختيار. ثم عارض بمثله في الله.
نقول : قد حاد عن جواب السؤال، إذ هو في أنه كيف اختار ذا على ضده؟ وليس شرط الاختيار أن يفعل ما شاء، ولكن يختار الأولى به أن يفعل فإذا فعل ما لا يعرف لماذا فعل ثبت أن لغيره في فعله تدبيرا، على ذلك خرج فعله. والله الموفق.
ومعارضته بالله سبحانه محال على القولين: على قولنا بأنه خالق بذاته، فالقول به كالقول بأنه لم قدر وعلم؟ وعلى قوله : إن ذلك أصلح في الدين، ولا يُسأل من ذلك وصف فعله. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected to himself with the one who has power over faith and disbelief: why did he do one and not the other? He claimed that this is impossible, because if he only came with one, he would be compelled, and choice has been established. Then he objected similarly concerning God. We say: He has deviated from answering the question, for it is about how he chose this over its opposite. The condition of choice is not that he does whatever he wishes, but he chooses what is most appropriate for him to do. So if he does something and one does not know why he did it, it is established that someone else has a plan in his action; upon that his action proceeds. And God is the grantor of success. His objection concerning God, exalted be He, is impossible on both views: on our view that He is creator by His essence, so to say that is like saying why He has power and knowledge? And on his view that that is more beneficial for religion, and He is not questioned about that which describes His action. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection about why a human chooses one opposite (e.g., faith) over another (disbelief) when both are within his power. The objector claims that if he could only do one, he would be compelled, but since choice is established, the question is why he chose this and not that. The Imam responds that the questioner has missed the point: the issue is not about ability but about the reason for the preference. The condition of choice is not doing whatever one wants, but choosing what is most appropriate (الأولى) for the agent. If an action is done for no known reason, that implies someone else is directing the action, which contradicts human free will. Then the Imam addresses a parallel objection about God: it is impossible because either we affirm that God is creator by His essence (and thus His actions are not subject to 'why' as they are from His essence), or we accept the opponent's view that God's actions are for the benefit of religion and He is not questioned about them. The passage ends with a reliance on divine power.

Key Terms
اختيار
ضد
اضطرار
أولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Choice means the ability to do anything one wishes without reason.
Correction: Imam Maturidi clarifies that choice involves selecting what is most appropriate for the agent, not doing whatever one wishes.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, what is the condition of choice?
A) To do whatever one wishes.
B) To do what is most appropriate for oneself.
C) To have complete compulsion.
D) To have no reason for action.

Key Takeaway
The condition of choice is to choose what is most appropriate, not to do whatever one wishes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you made recently where you could have chosen either of two opposites. Did you truly seek what was most appropriate, or did you simply do what you wanted? What would change in your life if you made that distinction a daily practice?
?
This lesson says that if you do something without knowing why, it shows that someone else is directing your action. Is there any area of your life where you act without a clear reason—perhaps out of habit, impulse, or pressure? Are you willing to stop and ask yourself why, even if the answer is uncomfortable?
?
When you hear about God's absolute power and wisdom, do you sometimes feel frustrated that His reasons are hidden from you? How does the Imam's response—that God's actions are not subject to 'why' in the same way— challenge your heart to trust Him more deeply, even when His decree seems incomprehensible? What will you do this week to turn that frustration into humble submission?

Refuting the Objection: Does One Capacity Imply Approval of Both?
Refutation of the objection and analogy of sword and dirham

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why giving a single capacity for both obedience and sin does not imply divine approval of sin, based on the nature of divine giving as favor and God's knowledge of its use.

Core Idea
Capacity is a neutral tool; divine giving is favor, not endorsement.

Imam Maturidi addresses a subtle objection: if one and the same capacity can be used for both faith and disbelief, why not say that God actively gives strength (التقوية) for both? The objector uses an analogy of a sword that can kill a guardian or protect, and a dirham that can buy lawful or unlawful things, to argue that the giver cannot be said to give for the evil use. The Imam clarifies that divine giving is a favor (امتنان), not a commission, and God knows the actual use, which the servant chooses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its context
Read the Arabic and translation, noting that this is an objection raised by opponents against the Maturidi position on capacity (qadar).
The objection claims that if one capacity is enough, then God must be seen as giving strength for both.
2
Identify the analogy used by the objector
The objector uses the analogy of a sword and a dirham: just as a sword can be used for lawful killing or murder, and a dirham for buying permissible or forbidden items, the giver of the sword or dirham is not said to give for the evil use.
The objector argues that similarly, God's giving capacity for both would imply approval of disbelief.
3
Understand Imam Maturidi's refutation
The Imam responds by noting that God knows how the capacity will be used. In the witnessed world, a human may be described as giving strength for both, but this does not apply to God because His giving is a favor, not a consequence of the servant's action.
The key phrase: 'it is in it not by it' (فيه لا بها) indicates the capacity is a tool, not the cause of the action.
4
Reflect on the concept of favor (امتنان)
The Imam states that the letter of giving (حرف الإعطاء) is not placed because it is a type of favor. This means the capacity is a gift, not a statement of support for its misuse.
Divine favor does not change the moral status of the action; it merely enables the servant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نحمد الله، قد أغنانا الله عن نحو هذا السؤال، لكن أحببت أن أذكر هنا مقداره فيما لا يُرضى به سؤالاً لضعفه، ليعلموا به قدره في المرضى به. والله الموفق.
ثم زعم أنه إذ صلحت قوة واحدة للإيمان وضده لمَ لا صلح القول بالتقوية عليهما؟ فدفع ذا بالأمر والنهي. وعارض بالسيف والدرهم : وإن احتمل استعماله في قتل الولي وإنفاقه في شرى الخمر لم يجز القول بالإعطاء لذلك.
نقول : تمام السؤال [وجوابه] أن الله إذ علم أنه فيم يستعمل. وفي مثله في الشاهد يوصف بالتقوية عليه، والله لم يوصف به، فمثله في الخلق. مع ما يقال بالأول، لكنه طلب منه واختار ذلك، فيه لا بها. ولا يوضع في ذلك حرف الإعطاء، لأنه نوع امتنان. ولا...
And we praise God, God has sufficed us from this kind of question, but I wished to mention here its extent in what is not approved as a question due to its weakness, so that they may know its value in what is disliked. And God is the grantor of success. Then he claimed that if one power suffices for faith and its opposite, why is the statement of giving strength (التقوية) for both not valid? He repelled this with the command and prohibition. And he objected with the sword and the dirham: even if their use is possible for killing the guardian and spending on buying wine, it is not permissible to say that the giving is for that. We say: The complete question [and its answer] is that God, since He knows how it will be used. And in a similar case in the witnessed world, one is described as strengthening both, but God is not described with that, so His likeness is in creation. Along with what is said at first, but he requested it and chose that, it is in it not by it. And the letter of giving is not placed in that, because it is a type of favor. And not...
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage begins with a prefatory remark that God has sufficed from such questions, but the Imam nevertheless includes it to show its weakness. Then it presents an objection: if one power is sufficient for both faith and disbelief, why not say that God gives strength (التقوية) for both? The objector tries to refute this by citing divine commands and prohibitions, and by analogy with a sword and a dirham: even though a sword can be used to kill a guardian and a dirham can be spent on wine, one does not say that the giving (i.e., providing the instrument) is for those purposes. The Imam's answer is that God knows how the capacity will be used. In the witnessed world, a human may be described as giving strength for both, but God is not described that way; His case is like that of creation. Moreover, the action comes from the servant's request and choice (الطلب والاختيار), not from the mere capacity. The giving of capacity is a favor (امتنان), not an endorsement of misuse. The text ends abruptly.

Key Terms
تقوية
قوة
سيف
درهم
امتنان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that giving the capacity for both opposites implies divine approval of the sinful use.
Correction: The Imam shows that divine giving is a favor (امتنان) and God knows the actual use, which is chosen by the servant. The capacity is neutral, and the giving is not an endorsement.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the opponent use to argue against giving capacity for both opposites?
A) A sword and a dirham.
B) A horse and a camel.
C) A king and a servant.
D) Light and darkness.

Key Takeaway
The capacity for opposites is a neutral favor from God; its use is determined by the servant's choice, not by the giving itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a capacity—like intelligence, wealth, or influence—from Allah, do you subconsciously assume His approval of whatever you choose to do with it? How does this lesson's truth—that the giving is a neutral favor, not endorsement—break that assumption and call you to accountability?
?
Is there a capacity in your life right now that you are using in a way that contradicts Allah's commands? How does the fact that God knows exactly how you will use it (even if you hide it from yourself) make you reconsider your choices today?
?
The objector argued that since Allah gave the capacity for both good and evil, He must approve both. You have seen the flaw. Now search your own heart: where else do you behave as if Allah's provision implies His pleasure with your actions? What will you change after this lesson?

Maturidi's Refutation of the Devoid-of-Action Claim
Maturidi refutes the claim that an entity can be devoid of action only initially.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that an entity can always be free from action, contrary to the opponent's restricted view.

Core Idea
Existence does not necessitate continuous action; freedom from action is always rationally possible.

Maturidi engages with an opponent who claims that a thing can be free from action only at the moment it comes into existence. Here he refutes that claim and criticizes the opponent's reasoning. This passage illustrates Maturidi's approach to rational theology and his critique of flawed arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the full literal translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the opponent's statement: that it is permissible for an entity to be devoid of action only at the moment of its existence, but never afterward.
Note that the opponent allows initial freedom but denies it later.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi states clearly that the opponent is wrong: such freedom is always permissible, not just initially. This is a direct rebuttal.
4
Reflect on the critique of rationality
Maturidi points out that the opponent's claim to rationality is flawed; true reason would not restrict freedom as the opponent does. He ends by dismissing a further discussion due to confusion.
Notice how Maturidi uses the opponent's own concept of rationality against him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في الصحيح السليم: إنه يجوز أن يخلو عن الفعل وقت كونه، ثم لم يجز أبدا. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: } وما يقوله خطأ، بل يجوز ذلك. ثم زعم أن ذلك معقول، وهو عقل من حق العقل [عنده] الخروج عما خاله عقلاً. ثم تكلم في العلم بما لم أظن أحدا تأمله إلا عرف أن الحيرة دفعته إليه، فتركته لقلّة نفعه.
He said in the sound and correct: It is permissible that it be devoid of action at the time of its existence, then it never becomes permissible. The Sheikh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: What he says is wrong; rather, that is permissible. Then he claimed that that is rational, and it is rationality that the right of reason [in his view] is to depart from what reason deemed contradictory. Then he spoke about knowledge such that I do not think anyone who contemplates it would not know that bewilderment drove him to it, so I left it for its lack of benefit.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection from an unnamed opponent: it is permissible for a thing to be free from acting at the moment it comes into existence, but after that moment it is never permissible for it to be free from action. Sheikh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi refutes this, stating that it is always permissible for it to be free from action—the restriction is invalid. He then comments that the opponent claims his own view is rational, but that rationality, in his judgment, consists in abandoning what reason actually holds. Finally, the Sheikh notes that the opponent entered into a discussion of knowledge that clearly shows his confusion, and for that reason it is not worth examining further.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that an entity can always be free from action, rejecting the opponent's restriction to the initial moment.

Choosing Belief over Disbelief: The Nature of Choice
Maturidi answers why one chooses belief over disbelief despite having power for both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that choice is based on suitability, not unlimited power, and why the analogy with God fails.

Core Idea
Choice means selecting what is most suitable, not having power over everything.

This passage addresses a key objection: if humans have power over both belief and disbelief, why do they choose one? Maturidi clarifies the nature of choice and defends divine attributes from a flawed analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the opponent's objection and Maturidi's reply starting with 'نقول'.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage contains two objections: first about human choice, then an analogy with God. Find how the opponent formulates them.
3
Analyze Maturidi's reply on choice
Maturidi says the condition (شرط) of choice is not doing whatever one wishes, but choosing the more appropriate action (الأولى به أن يفعل). Find this phrase and understand its significance.
This is the core of his argument.
4
Understand why the God-objection is invalid
Maturidi shows the objection fails on two views: on his own view (God is Creator by essence) and on the opponent's view (God's actions are for the best). Trace both rebuttals.
The phrase 'محال على القولين' marks the two-part refutation.
5
Reflect on human responsibility
Consider how Maturidi's definition of choice preserves human responsibility without implying compulsion, and how it differs from a view that requires equal ability for all options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالذي قدر على الإيمان والكفر، فلم فعل أحدهما دون الأخر؟ فزعم أن ذا محال، لأنه لو كان لا يأتي إلا بواحد كان يكون مضطرًا، وقد ثبت الاختيار. ثم عارض بمثله في الله. نقول: قد حاد عن جواب السؤال، إذ هو في أنه كيف اختار ذا على ضده؟ وليس شرط الاختيار أن يفعل ما شاء، ولكن يختار الأولى به أن يفعل فإذا فعل ما لا يعرف لماذا فعل ثبت أن لغيره في فعله تدبيرا، على ذلك خرج فعله. والله الموفق. ومعارضته بالله سبحانه محال على القولين: على قولنا بأنه خالق بذاته، فالقول به كالقول بأنه لم قدر وعلم؟ وعلى قوله: إن ذلك أصلح في الدين، ولا يُسأل من ذلك وصف فعله. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected to himself concerning the one who has power over belief and disbelief: why did he do one and not the other? He claimed that this is impossible, because if he only ever does one, he would be compelled, while choice has been established. Then he made a similar objection concerning God. We say: He deviated from answering the question, for it is about how he chose this over its opposite. The condition of choice is not that he does whatever he wishes, but that he chooses the one more appropriate for him to do. If he does something without knowing why he did it, it is established that another has control in his action—upon that his action proceeds. And Allah is the Grantor of success. His objection concerning God, exalted, is impossible on both views: on our view that He is the Creator by His essence, so to say that is like saying why did He not have power and knowledge? And on his view that that is more beneficial for religion, and He is not asked about that—His action is described as such. There is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents a self-objection: if a person has power over both belief and disbelief, why does he choose one over the other? The opponent argues that if only one occurs, the person would be compelled, contradicting choice. He then extends this to God. Maturidi's reply (نقول) states that the opponent missed the real question: the issue is how the agent chooses one option over its opposite. Choice does not require the ability to do everything one wishes; rather, the agent selects what is more suitable. If an action is done without known reason, it implies external control. As for the analogy with God, it fails because on Maturidi's view God is Creator by essence (not subject to external reasons), and on the opponent's own view, God's actions are for the best in religion and not open to interrogation.

Key Terms
اختيار
اختيار الأولى
خالق بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think choice requires equal ability to do either option.
Correction: Maturidi shows choice is based on suitability (الأولى به أن يفعل), not capacity to do everything one wishes.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the condition of choice?
A) To do whatever one wishes.
B) To choose the more appropriate action.
C) To have equal ability for all options.
D) To know the outcome of each option.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that choice is about selecting what is most appropriate, not about having power over all options, and that objections against divine action based on human choice are invalid.

Maturidi's Refutation of a Single Power for Both Belief and Disbelief
Maturidi refutes that a single power suffices for both good and evil actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that a single divine power (qudra) is not indifferent between belief and disbelief, but is created for a specific outcome known to Allah.

Core Idea
Divine power is not neutral; it is created for a single effect known to Allah.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi responds to an objection that if a single power can produce both belief and its opposite, then God would be enabling both. He rejects this by clarifying that the power is created for one specific effect and that evil actions are chosen by the servant, not caused by the power. This sharpens the Sunni doctrine of qadar (divine decree) without placing evil in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the dense dialectical style typical of Maturidi.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and initial response
Find where the opponent states that a single power suffices for both opposites, and note Maturidi's first rebuttal using command and prohibition.
The opponent uses 'why not enabling on both?'
3
Analyze the full answer: two parts
Maturidi gives a two-part answer: (1) Allah knows how the power will be used, so He does not 'enable' in the human sense; (2) the servant chooses the action, not the power. Recognize the distinction between 'through him' and 'through it'.
The phrase 'فيه لا بها' is crucial.
4
Understand the sword analogy and its failure
The opponent analogizes with a sword that can kill or be used lawfully. Maturidi argues the analogy fails because power (qudra) must produce an effect, unlike a sword that can remain unused.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that power only admits one of the two opposites and cannot be devoid of effect, so the repelling argument is invalid.
This reinforces the specificity of divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعم أنه إذ صلحت قوة واحدة للإيمان وضده لمَ لا صلح القول بالتقوية عليهما؟ فدفع ذا بالأمر والنهي. وعارض بالسيف والدرهم: وإن احتمل استعماله في قتل الولى وإنفاقه في شرى الخمر لم يجز القول بالإعطاء لذلك. نقول: تمام السؤال [وجوابه] أن الله إذ علم أنه فيم يستعمل. وفي مثله في الشاهد يوصف بالتقوية عليه، والله لم يوصف به، فمثله في الخلق. مع ما يقال بالأول، لكنه طلب منه واختار ذلك، فيه لا بها. ولا يوضع في ذلك حرف الإعطاء، لأنه نوع امتنان. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وما عارض فاسد، لا حتماله أن لا يستعمل في الوجهين، فلم يكن الدفع لوجه ذلك. والقوة لا تحتمل إلا أحدهما، ولا يجوز أن يخلو عن وقوع أحدهما بها، وقد علم بذلك، فلا يحتمل القول بالدفع لغير ذلك.
Then he claimed that when a single power suffices for belief and its opposite, why should the statement of enabling upon both not be valid? He repelled this by command and prohibition. And he objected with the sword and the dirham: even if its use is possible for killing a guardian and spending on buying wine, it is not permissible to speak of granting for that. We say: The complete question [and its answer] is that Allah knows what it will be used for. In a similar case in the witnessed world, one is described as enabling upon it, but Allah is not described with that, so its likeness is in creation. Along with what was said initially, but he (the servant) requested from Him and chose that—the act is through him, not through it. And the letter of granting is not placed in that, because it is a type of favor. There is no power except through Allah. And what he opposed is corrupt, because of the possibility that it is not used in both ways, so the repelling was not for that reason. And the power only admits one of them, and it is not permissible that it be devoid of the occurrence of one of them through it, and that is known, so the statement of repelling for other than that is not admissible.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
The opponent argues: if a single power (qudra) suffices for both belief and disbelief, why can't we say that God enables the servant for both? He tries to repel this by referring to divine commands and prohibitions, and analogizes with a sword or a dirham that can be used for good or evil: granting them does not imply permission for evil use. Maturidi's answer has two parts. First, the complete answer is that God knows how the power will be used; in the visible world, we describe a person as 'enabling' another when the latter does the action, but God is not described as 'enabling' in the same sense because His knowledge is decisive. Second, the servant requests and chooses the action; the act is attributed to him (فيه لا بها—through him, not through the power). The word 'granting' (إعطاء) is not appropriate because it implies favor, not causal determination. Maturidi then adds that the opponent's analogy with the sword is flawed because the power (qudra) cannot be used for both; it is created for one specific outcome, and it is impossible for it to remain without any effect.

Key Terms
قوة واحدة
تقوية
أمر ونهي
إعطاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a power can produce either of two opposites, it is indifferent, and God would be enabling both.
Correction: Maturidi argues that power is created for a specific effect known to Allah, and the servant's choice determines the outcome, not the power itself.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the analogy between divine power and a sword that can be used for good or evil?
A) Because a sword has free will.
B) Because divine power is created for one specific effect.
C) Because God does not grant anything.
D) Because the sword analogy is about physical objects.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that divine power is specific and purposeful, not indifferent, safeguarding Allah from being the source of evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'Allah gave me this ability, so He is ultimately responsible'? The opponent argued that granting a sword does not mean permission to kill. Allah has given you many 'swords'—status, influence, relationships. Have you ever used any of them in a way that violates His commands? How does this lesson hold you accountable without giving you an excuse to blame Allah?
