⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray 'Allahumma qinna 'ala ta'atik', do you truly feel your utter dependence on Him, or do you recite it as a routine while relying on your own strength? Pause and identify one area of obedience where you have been trusting your own willpower. Today, before acting in that area, pause and say that dua with full awareness that without His help, you cannot do it.
?
The unpraised dua 'Allahumma khall baynana wa bayna ta'atik' implies that you think you can obey on your own if left to yourself. Is there a sin or neglect in your life that you justify by saying 'I'm just weak, I can't help it'? That excuse assumes you need to be left free rather than helped. Confess this to Allah and ask specifically for His help to overcome it.
?
Maturidi shows that even when your own power is depleted, the action still occurs by Allah's power. Have you ever felt spiritually exhausted, unable to perform a good deed? In that moment, did you remember that Allah's power is at work? Reflect on a time when you felt powerless. How can you, starting today, actively rely on His power rather than giving up?

The Provision (Rizq) Is Guaranteed by God Alone
Maturidi argues that no one can prevent God from providing what He has guaranteed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that no creature can prevent Allah from providing the sustenance He has guaranteed, and the theological implications of that claim.

Core Idea
Allah alone guarantees provision; no one can thwart His promise.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a common question about provision (rizq). He critiques an improper way of asking and instead lays out the correct understanding based on the Qur'anic guarantee. He then presents a logical disjunction: either someone can prevent Allah from fulfilling His guarantee (which leads to absurdity) or not (which affirms Allah's sole power). This lesson walks through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow.
Notice the rhetorical structure: Maturidi starts with a critique, then states the proper view, then presents two alternatives.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two mutually exclusive possibilities regarding someone's power over Allah's provision. Find them and note how Maturidi phrases them.
The first possibility is introduced with 'فإما أن يكون' (so either it is), and the second with 'أو لا يكون' (or it is not).
3
Understand the implications of each
For each possibility, Maturidi explains the theological consequence. The first leads to a grave error; the second affirms the truth. Trace the reasoning.
Pay attention to the conditional: if someone could prevent Allah, then Allah would be under that person's power—an impossibility.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
After presenting the two options, Maturidi concludes that no one can have power over another's provision from that angle. Ask yourself: what does this mean for reliance on Allah?
This reinforces tawakkul (trust in Allah) and negates any notion of independent causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تكلم في سؤال الرزق بوجه لا يُرضى به سؤالاً؛ بل الوجه في ذلك أن الله تعالى إذ ضَمِن الرزق بقوله: ﴿وما من دابة في الأرض إلا على الله رزقها ﴾ [هود، ٦/١١]، كان ذلك يُملك بملكه أو بما يُطعمه : (أ) فإما أن يكون لأحد قدرة في منع الله عن وفاء ما ضمن من الوجه الذي ضمن حتى يلحقه الخلف في الوعد والعجز عن وفاء شيء ضمنه، فيكون الله في فعله تحت قدرة غيره، وبغيره يقدر على إنجاز الوعد ووفاء العهد، وهذا أمر عظيم؛ أو لا يكون، فيبطل أن يكون أحد يُرزق بما هو في الحقيقة رزق غيره من ذلك الوجه أو يقدر عليه. ولو كان ذلك فيها القدرة معه لكانت هذه الوحشة تلحقه؛ إذ ما علم أنه من ذلك الوجه يطلب رزقه .
Then he spoke about the question of provision (al-rizq) in a way that is not satisfactory as a question. Rather, the proper way is that Allah the Exalted, since He guaranteed provision by His saying: 'There is no creature on earth but that upon Allah is its provision' [Hud 11:6], that is possessed by His possession or by what He feeds it. So either someone has the power to prevent Allah from fulfilling what He has guaranteed in the way He guaranteed, such that a breach of promise and inability to fulfill something guaranteed would attach to Him—then Allah would be, in His action, under the power of another, and by another He is able to fulfill the promise and keep the covenant, and this is a grave matter. Or it is not so, then it is invalid that anyone is provided with what is in reality the provision of another in that way or has power over it. And if that were the case—that there is power with it—then this alienation would attach to Him, because it is known that He seeks His provision from that way.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on provision (rizq) and the proper way to understand it. Maturidi criticizes a certain question about provision as unsatisfactory. He then explains the correct view: Allah has guaranteed provision for every creature, as per Quran 11:6. He considers two possibilities: (1) Someone has the power to prevent Allah from fulfilling His guarantee, which would imply that Allah is under that person's power and unable to keep His promise—this is an impossibility. (2) No one has such power, so it is invalid that anyone can be provided with what rightfully belongs to another, or that anyone can have power over it. If someone claimed that a person has power over his own provision alongside Allah, that would imply that Allah is alienated from His own action. Thus, all provision comes from Allah alone, and no creature can hinder it.

ضمان الرزق

Misconception: Some might think that one person's provision can be taken away or given to another arbitrarily, implying that creatures have control over provision.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if any creature had such power, it would imply Allah's inability to fulfill His promise, which is impossible. Therefore, all provision is solely from Allah and cannot be diverted.

B) That person would be a partner with Allah.

Key Takeaway
Allah alone guarantees provision, and no creature can hinder His promise; the logical consequence is that all provision is from Him alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you strive for your livelihood, is your ultimate reliance on your own skills and network, or on the Guarantor? This lesson declares that any creature claiming power over provision would be a grave lie. So ask yourself: In the quiet of your heart, whom do you trust to secure your future?
?
If Allah alone determines your rizq, why do you ever resort to dishonest means to earn it? Is it because you fear His guarantee is insufficient? This lesson calls that fear a 'great matter' (أمر عظيم) that would imply Allah is under the control of others. Confront your own contradictions: Are you living as if Allah's promise is true?

The Three Modes of Iṭlāq and Takhlīyah
Maturidi defines the three modes of iṭlāq and takhlīyah and distinguishes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct modes of the terms 'itlāq' and 'takhlīyah' as defined by Imam Maturidi, and how they apply differently to good and evil actions.

Core Idea
Itlāq and takhlīyah have three distinct meanings, not just one.

Imam Maturidi begins by clarifying that the terms 'itlāq' and 'takhlīyah' are not simple synonyms. They can refer to removal of restriction, commanding, or mere permission. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine actions and human responsibility. The passage then applies these modes to good and evil, and supports the distinction with Quranic and linguistic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm. Then read the literal translation. Notice the three modes listed at the start.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three modes
Find the three modes: raising hardship/prohibition, commanding, and permitting. Understand each one.
3
Note the condition for evil
See that for evil actions, these modes only apply conditionally: that the person is not coerced or compelled.
This guards against misuse of the terms.
4
Examine the evidence
Look at the Quranic verse and the contrast between two supplications. How do they support the distinction?
The verse shows release after prohibition; the supplications show different implications.
5
Reflect on the power point
Think about the statement that the act occurs at the extinction of power, yet itlāq and takhlīyah are not removed. What does this tell us about power and action?
This connects to broader kalām discussions on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من الإطلاق والتخلية فهو كلام يتوجه أوجها ثلاثة: رفع العسر والمنع، أو الأمر به، أو الإباحة . وذلك كله في الخير مطلق وفي الشر لا إلا مقيداً : إنه لم يُقسر ولم يُجبَر. وإذا كان كذلك فمعارضته بالذي ذكر فاسد . وما أجاب عنا بالمنع فحق. قال الله تعالى في قوله: فخلوا سبيلهم بعد ذكر المنع ، وما يحمد من قول الناس: «اللهم قونا على طاعتك»، ولا يحمد : «اللهم خلّ بيننا وبين طاعتك»؛ ثبت أن لأحدهما حالاً ليس للاخر. وكذلك هو يقول بالفعل وقت فناء القدرة ولا قدرة معه، ولا يقول بارتفاع الإطلاق والتخلية وقت الفعل لتعلم بذلك بعده فيها قدر . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And what is mentioned of 'itlāq' (release) and 'takhlīyah' (leaving free) is a statement that faces three modes: removal of hardship and prohibition, or commanding it, or permitting it. And all of that in good is absolute, and in evil it is only conditional: that he is not coerced nor compelled. And if it is so, then opposing it with what was mentioned is corrupt. And what he answered us with the prohibition is true. Allah Almighty said in His statement: 'So let them go their way' after mentioning prohibition, and it is not praiseworthy from people's saying: 'O Allah, strengthen us for Your obedience,' and it is not praiseworthy: 'O Allah, leave us and Your obedience'; it is established that one has a state that the other does not. And likewise he says that the act occurs at the time of the extinction of power and there is no power with it, and he does not say that 'itlāq' and 'takhlīyah' are removed at the time of the act, so that you may learn by that subsequently in it a power. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the terms 'itlāq' (release) and 'takhlīyah' (leaving free). The author states that these terms have three possible meanings when applied to an action: (1) removal of hardship and prohibition (i.e., making an action permissible by lifting a previous restriction), (2) commanding that action, or (3) simply permitting it (without command). These apply absolutely in the case of good actions, but in the case of evil actions they apply only with a condition—namely, that the person is not coerced or compelled (i.e., they do not imply license to do evil freely). The author then argues that any objection to this based on what was previously mentioned is invalid, and that the answer given by the opponent (the prohibition) is correct. He cites the Quranic verse 'So let them go their way' (Q 9:5) after mentioning prohibition, showing that the release comes after a prior restriction. He also contrasts two supplications: 'O Allah, strengthen us for Your obedience' (praiseworthy) vs. 'O Allah, leave us and Your obedience' (not praiseworthy), establishing that there is a state for one that is not for the other. Finally, he notes that the act occurs at the moment of the extinction of power (i.e., when the agent has no power), but one does not say that 'itlāq' and 'takhlīyah' are removed at that moment, so that you may learn that power exists subsequently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That iṭlāq and takhlīyah are completely synonymous and always imply simple permissibility.
Correction: The passage shows they have three distinct modes, and their application depends on context: they can mean removal of restriction, command, or permission. Moreover, for evil acts they are conditional on lack of coercion.

Checkpoint

D) Command, prohibition, permission

Key Takeaway
Itlāq and takhlīyah are not simple synonyms; they have three distinct modes that apply differently to good and evil, as supported by Quranic and linguistic evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says that in evil, iṭlāq and takhlīyah are conditional on not being coerced. Do you ever blame your circumstances, your upbringing, or your environment for your sins, as if you were compelled? The Impossibility of Preventing Divine Sustenance
Maturidi argues against the notion that anyone can prevent God from fulfilling His guarantee of sustenance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that no one can prevent God from fulfilling His guarantee of sustenance, and why this preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
No one can block God's provision; to claim otherwise diminishes His power.

Maturidi addresses a common question about sustenance. He argues that since Allah has guaranteed provision for every creature (Quran 11:6), it is impossible for anyone to prevent that guarantee. If such prevention were possible, Allah would be under another's power—a view he deems grave. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to the dichotomous structure (either...or).
The passage is dense; take your time with the translation.
2
Identify the dichotomous argument
Notice the two options presented: (a) someone has power to prevent God from fulfilling His guarantee, leading to God being under another's power; (b) no one has such power, making it impossible to take another's provision.
Focus on why option (a) is considered a 'grave matter'.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this argument upholds God's transcendence and power. Ask yourself: What does this teach about the nature of divine guarantee?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تكلم في سؤال الرزق بوجه لا يُرضى به سؤالاً ؛ بل الوجه في ذلك أن الله تعالى إذ ضَمِن الرزق بقوله: ﴿وما من دابة في الأرض إلا على الله رزقها ﴾ [هود، ٦/١١]، كان ذلك يُملك بملكه أو بما يُطعمه : (أ) فإما أن يكون لأحد قدرة في منع الله عن وفاء ما ضمن من الوجه الذي ضمن حتى يلحقه الخلف في الوعد والعجز عن وفاء شيء ضمنه، فيكون الله في فعله تحت قدرة غيره، وبغيره يقدر على إنجاز الوعد ووفاء العهد، وهذا أمر عظيم؛ بـ) أو لا يكون، فيبطل أن يكون أحد يُرزق بما هو في الحقيقة رزق غيره من ذلك الوجه أو يقدر عليه. ولو كان ذلك فيها القدرة معه لكانت هذه الوحشة تلحقه؛ إذ ما علم أنه من ذلك الوجه يطلب رزقه .
Then he spoke about the question of sustenance in a manner not satisfactory as a question; rather the correct way is that when Allah Almighty guaranteed sustenance by His statement: 'And there is no creature on earth except that upon Allah is its provision' (Q 11:6), that is possessed by His possession or by what He feeds. (a) Either someone has the power to prevent Allah from fulfilling what He guaranteed in the manner He guaranteed, such that He would be subject to failure in promise and inability to fulfill something He guaranteed, so Allah in His act would be under the power of another, and by another He would be able to fulfill the promise and keep the covenant—and this is a grave matter; (b) or it is not, then it is invalid that anyone is sustained by what is in reality the sustenance of another in that manner or has power over it. And if that were the case that there is power with it, this distress would afflict Him; since he does not know that he seeks his sustenance in that manner.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of divine guarantee of sustenance. The author criticizes a certain way of asking about sustenance and presents the correct perspective: Allah has guaranteed provision for all creatures (Q 11:6). This guarantee is fulfilled either by His ownership or by His feeding. The author then presents a dichotomy: (a) either someone has the power to prevent Allah from fulfilling His guarantee—in which case Allah would be under that person's power, unable to keep His promise, which is an appalling idea—(b) or no one has such power, in which case it is impossible for anyone to be sustained by what is actually the sustenance of another (i.e., taking someone else's provision) or to have power over that. If there were power with the guarantee, it would lead to distress for the guaranteed, because he does not know whether he will get his provision from that specific source.

ضمان
قدرة
وعد
عهد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That humans can prevent God from providing sustenance to someone, implying God's guarantee can be blocked.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such prevention would entail God being under another's power, which is impossible. Thus, no creature can prevent God's fulfillment of His guarantee.

Key Takeaway

The Meaning of Power in Taqwa
Maturidi distinguishes two types of power to resolve the dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between two meanings of 'power' (qudra) and how it resolves al-Warrāq's dilemma about taqwa and divine watchfulness.

Core Idea
Taqwa is possible through the power of means, not power concurrent with sin.

This passage addresses a tricky question: did the prophets ever avoid sin out of God-consciousness while being capable of sinning? The initial dilemma forces a choice between denying the prophets' taqwa or affirming it before the act. Maturidi cuts through by distinguishing two senses of 'power' and shows that the prophets' taqwa lies in using the means God gave them.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial question and answers
Read the opening question and the two possible answers (yes/no) with their implications for the prophets' taqwa. Notice the dilemma.
The question assumes a single concept of power.
2
Identify the two meanings of power
Maturidi distinguishes: (1) power as causes/states that occur inevitably unless wasted, and (2) power concurrent with the act. Focus on the difference.
Only the first meaning allows for taqwa.
3
Understand the counter-objection
The opponent counters with a similar question about God watching a prophet leaving a sin. See how Maturidi's answer already covers this.
The counter shows the opponent misunderstands the first meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: سئل الوراق فقال : يقال لهم : هل اتقى أحد معصية الله وهو قادر لمراقبته الله ؟ فإن قالوا: لا، أعظموا القول في وصف الأنبياء أنهم لم يفعلوا ذلك. وإن قالوا: نعم، لزمهم القول بها قبل الفعل. نقول له، وبالله التوفيق: إن عنيت بالقدرة الأسباب التي هي أحوال القدرة التي تعرض لا محالة لولا التضييع من العبد فبلى؛ وكل الأنبياء كذلك كانوا، وكذلك الأخيار. وإن أردت به القدرة التي هي مع الفعل أحلت السؤال وصرت كمن يقول: هل راقب الله أحد في إبقاء المعاصي وهو فاعل لها، وذلك مما لا معنى له. وهو يعارضك فيقول: هل راقب الله نبي من الأنبياء في إبقاء معصية علمها منه أو أخبر عنه؟ فمهما أجاب من شيء فمثله الأول. ثم يقال : هل تفضل الله على أحد من أوليائه بمنع قدرة عدواته؟ فإن قال: نعم، قال: إن الله لم يعط أولياءه قدرة معاصيه، فعليه في أعدائه أيضا أنه لم يعطهم قوة طاعته، وفي ذلك ما أنكر آنفًا. وإن قال: لا، زعم أنه أعطى أولياءه قوة عدواته؛ ومن قولهم: إنه لم يعط أعداءه قوة العداوة، فالآن صار إلى أن أعطى أولياءه قوتها، وذلك عظيم. ثم يقال: هل أعطى الله ولياً قوى على تلك الطاعة حين الطاعة؟ فإن قال: لا، فالوحشة في طاعة لم يقو عليها ليست بدونها في اجتناب معصية لم يقو عليه، بل قوى على ترك المعصية. وعندهم لم يقو على الطاعة، وهذا أوحش. ثم يقال: هل والى الله ولي أو عاداه عدو بفعل قوى عليه؟ فإن قال: نعم، أقر بالقوة مع الفعل وإن قال: لا، زعم أن العدواة والولاية بما لا يقوى عليه، وذلك بعيد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He said: Al-Warrāq was asked, and he said: It is said to them: Did anyone avoid disobedience of God—while being capable—out of watchfulness of God? If they say: No, they magnify the statement in describing the prophets that they did not do that. And if they say: Yes, then they must say it before the act. We say to him, and by God is success: If you mean by power the causes which are states of power that occur inevitably were it not for the waste from the servant, then yes; and all the prophets were like that, as well as the righteous. And if you mean by it the power that is with the act, you have changed the question and become like one who says: Did God watch anyone in leaving sins while he is doing them? That has no meaning. And he counters you saying: Did God watch any prophet in leaving a sin that He knew from him or He informed about him? Whatever he answers, the like of it is the first. Then it is said: Did God favor any of His friends by preventing the power of His enemies? If he says: Yes, he said: God did not give His friends the power to disobey Him, so likewise it follows that He did not give His enemies the strength to obey Him, and in that is what he denied earlier. And if he says: No, he claims that He gave His friends the power of His enemies; and it is from their statement that He did not give His enemies the power of enmity, so now he has come to the point that He gave His friends the power of enmity, and that is grave. Then it is said: Did God give a friend strength for that obedience at the time of obedience? If he says: No, then the distress in an obedience he was not given strength for is not less than the distress in avoiding a sin he was not given strength for; rather he was given strength to leave the sin. And according to them, he was not given strength for obedience, and this is more distressing. Then it is said: Did God befriend a friend or make an enemy because of an act he was given strength for? If he says: Yes, he affirms power with the act; and if he says: No, he claims that friendship and enmity are based on what one has no power over, and that is far-fetched. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of a specific argument. It begins with a question posed to 'al-Warrāq' about whether anyone ever avoided sin out of God-consciousness while being capable of sinning. The interlocutor's position is that if they say no, it implies the prophets did not have such taqwa (bad); if they say yes, it would imply that they possessed that consciousness before the act (a strange requirement). The author responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'power': (1) the causes/states of power that would inevitably occur if the servant does not waste them—in this sense, yes, the prophets and righteous have taqwa; (2) power that is concurrent with the act—this would make the question meaningless, like asking if God watched someone while he was actively sinning. Then a series of further objections and answers are presented: (a) whether God favored His friends by preventing the power of His enemies (if yes, that would mean He gave friends power to sin but not enemies power to obey—inconsistent; if no, then friends have power to be enemies—grave). (b) whether God gave a friend strength for obedience at the time of obedience (if no, then the distress of being unable to obey is worse than the distress of being unable to avoid sin; according to them, he cannot obey, which is worse). (c) whether God's friendship/enmity is based on an act one had power over (if yes, that affirms power with the act; if no, it implies friendship/enmity based on something beyond one's power—far-fetched). The passage concludes with 'There is no power except with God.'

Key Terms
مراقبة
قدرة
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'power' is a single, unambiguous concept in this context.
Correction: The passage shows 'power' can mean either the means to act or the ability concurrent with the act, and only the first allows for taqwa.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the consequence if one interprets 'power' as concurrent with the act?
A) It affirms the prophets' taqwa
B) It makes the question meaningless
C) It implies God is unjust
D) It forces a choice between two bad options

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's distinction between two types of power resolves the apparent dilemma about taqwa and divine watchfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you avoid a sin, do you attribute your success to your own willpower, or do you recognize that your ability itself (the means) is a gift from God that could be taken away at any moment? How might that change the way you pray for steadfastness?
?
The prophets had the same means as you, yet they never wasted them. Where in your life today are you 'wasting' the means God gave you—like prayer, reflection, or good company—that would help you avoid sin? Be specific.
?
If you truly believed that every moment of obedience is a result of God's watchfulness and the means He provides, what would you ask Him for tonight in your prayer to strengthen your taqwa?

God's Favor and the Power of Enemies
Maturidi presents two more dialectical arguments about divine favor and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dialectical refutation of the claim that God favored His friends by preventing the power of His enemies, and the implications for the power to obey or disobey.

Core Idea
If God prevents enemies' power, He must also prevent friends' power to sin—an inconsistency.

The argument continues with a new twist: does God favor His friends by preventing His enemies from having power over them? The answer leads to contradictions about who has power to do good or evil. Maturidi then introduces a further question about whether God gives a friend strength for obedience at the moment of obedience, revealing more inconsistencies in the opponent's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the first question about favor
The question asks whether God favored His friends by preventing the power of His enemies. Consider the two possible answers.
The answer 'yes' implies God did not give enemies power to obey Him.
2
Analyze the consequences of 'yes'
If God prevented enemies' power, then He also did not give friends power to sin, which is consistent; but the opponent's position previously assumed something else.
The contradiction is that the opponent denied that God would prevent power.
3
Analyze the consequences of 'no'
If God did not prevent enemies' power, then He gave friends power to be enemies—a grave claim.
This also contradicts the opponent's view.
4
Examine the second question about strength for obedience
The question asks if God gave a friend strength for obedience at the time it is performed. The answer 'no' leads to a comparison of distress.
Maturidi argues that being unable to obey is more distressing than being unable to avoid sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: سئل الوراق فقال : يقال لهم : هل اتقى أحد معصية الله وهو قادر لمراقبته الله ؟ فإن قالوا: لا، أعظموا القول في وصف الأنبياء أنهم لم يفعلوا ذلك. وإن قالوا: نعم، لزمهم القول بها قبل الفعل. نقول له، وبالله التوفيق: إن عنيت بالقدرة الأسباب التي هي أحوال القدرة التي تعرض لا محالة لولا التضييع من العبد فبلى؛ وكل الأنبياء كذلك كانوا، وكذلك الأخيار. وإن أردت به القدرة التي هي مع الفعل أحلت السؤال وصرت كمن يقول: هل راقب الله أحد في إبقاء المعاصي وهو فاعل لها، وذلك مما لا معنى له. وهو يعارضك فيقول: هل راقب الله نبي من الأنبياء في إبقاء معصية علمها منه أو أخبر عنه؟ فمهما أجاب من شيء فمثله الأول. ثم يقال : هل تفضل الله على أحد من أوليائه بمنع قدرة عدواته؟ فإن قال: نعم، قال: إن الله لم يعط أولياءه قدرة معاصيه، فعليه في أعدائه أيضا أنه لم يعطهم قوة طاعته، وفي ذلك ما أنكر آنفًا. وإن قال: لا، زعم أنه أعطى أولياءه قوة عدواته؛ ومن قولهم: إنه لم يعط أعداءه قوة العداوة، فالآن صار إلى أن أعطى أولياءه قوتها، وذلك عظيم. ثم يقال: هل أعطى الله ولياً قوى على تلك الطاعة حين الطاعة؟ فإن قال: لا، فالوحشة في طاعة لم يقو عليها ليست بدونها في اجتناب معصية لم يقو عليه، بل قوى على ترك المعصية. وعندهم لم يقو على الطاعة، وهذا أوحش. ثم يقال: هل والى الله ولي أو عاداه عدو بفعل قوى عليه؟ فإن قال: نعم، أقر بالقوة مع الفعل وإن قال: لا، زعم أن العدواة والولاية بما لا يقوى عليه، وذلك بعيد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He said: Al-Warrāq was asked, and he said: It is said to them: Did anyone avoid disobedience of God—while being capable—out of watchfulness of God? If they say: No, they magnify the statement in describing the prophets that they did not do that. And if they say: Yes, then they must say it before the act. We say to him, and by God is success: If you mean by power the causes which are states of power that occur inevitably were it not for the waste from the servant, then yes; and all the prophets were like that, as well as the righteous. And if you mean by it the power that is with the act, you have changed the question and become like one who says: Did God watch anyone in leaving sins while he is doing them? That has no meaning. And he counters you saying: Did God watch any prophet in leaving a sin that He knew from him or He informed about him? Whatever he answers, the like of it is the first. Then it is said: Did God favor any of His friends by preventing the power of His enemies? If he says: Yes, he said: God did not give His friends the power to disobey Him, so likewise it follows that He did not give His enemies the strength to obey Him, and in that is what he denied earlier. And if he says: No, he claims that He gave His friends the power of His enemies; and it is from their statement that He did not give His enemies the power of enmity, so now he has come to the point that He gave His friends the power of enmity, and that is grave. Then it is said: Did God give a friend strength for that obedience at the time of obedience? If he says: No, then the distress in an obedience he was not given strength for is not less than the distress in avoiding a sin he was not given strength for; rather he was given strength to leave the sin. And according to them, he was not given strength for obedience, and this is more distressing. Then it is said: Did God befriend a friend or make an enemy because of an act he was given strength for? If he says: Yes, he affirms power with the act; and if he says: No, he claims that friendship and enmity are based on what one has no power over, and that is far-fetched. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of a specific argument. It begins with a question posed to 'al-Warrāq' about whether anyone ever avoided sin out of God-consciousness while being capable of sinning. The interlocutor's position is that if they say no, it implies the prophets did not have such taqwa (bad); if they say yes, it would imply that they possessed that consciousness before the act (a strange requirement). The author responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'power': (1) the causes/states of power that would inevitably occur if the servant does not waste them—in this sense, yes, the prophets and righteous have taqwa; (2) power that is concurrent with the act—this would make the question meaningless, like asking if God watched someone while he was actively sinning. Then a series of further objections and answers are presented: (a) whether God favored His friends by preventing the power of His enemies (if yes, that would mean He gave friends power to sin but not enemies power to obey—inconsistent; if no, then friends have power to be enemies—grave). (b) whether God gave a friend strength for obedience at the time of obedience (if no, then the distress of being unable to obey is worse than the distress of being unable to avoid sin; according to them, he cannot obey, which is worse). (c) whether God's friendship/enmity is based on an act one had power over (if yes, that affirms power with the act; if no, it implies friendship/enmity based on something beyond one's power—far-fetched). The passage concludes with 'There is no power except with God.'

Key Terms
ولاية
عداوة
قوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's favor only refers to external blessings, not to the power to act.
Correction: The passage shows that divine favor is linked to the granting or withholding of power to obey or disobey.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if someone says God favored His friends by preventing the power of His enemies, what follows?
A) God gave His enemies power to obey
B) God did not give His enemies power to obey
C) God gave His friends power to sin
D) God is unjust

Key Takeaway
The opponent's position leads to contradictions about who has power to obey or disobey, revealing flaws in the original argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, have you ever felt that God gave you the strength to avoid a sin but not the strength to perform a good deed? How does this passage challenge or affirm that feeling? What is one specific way you can ask God for the power to obey today?
?
The argument here exposes an inconsistency in thinking about God's favor. Is there an area where you subtly believe that God has favored you with the ability to avoid certain sins, but you neglect the need to seek His help for positive acts of worship? Commit to one concrete action to seek strength for obedience.
?
If you truly believed that every good action you perform is only possible because God grants you the power at that moment, how would your prayer life change? Will you start your next prayer by acknowledging your utter dependence on Him for even the intention to obey?

Friendship, Enmity, and Power with the Act
Maturidi concludes the dialectic with a final question and a theological affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's final argument that God's friendship or enmity is based on acts one has power over, and the concluding affirmation that all power is from God.

Core Idea
Friendship and enmity are based on acts we have power to perform.

The final dialectical exchange asks whether God's friendship or enmity is based on an act one was given strength for. This leads to the conclusion that power must be with the act, and the session ends with the profound reminder: 'There is no power except with God.' This lesson ties together the earlier distinctions and underscores a key tenet of Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the final question
The question asks whether God's friendship or enmity is based on an act one had power over. Consider the two answers.
2
Analyze the 'yes' answer
If yes, it affirms that power is with the act, consistent with Maturidi's earlier distinction.
3
Analyze the 'no' answer
If no, it claims that friendship/enmity is based on something beyond one's power, which is far-fetched.
This contradicts the idea of moral responsibility.
4
Reflect on the concluding phrase
The phrase 'There is no power except with God' is a common Islamic affirmation that all power belongs to God, serving as a theological summary.

Two Types of Ability: Resolving the Objection on Fearing God
Maturidi distinguishes two senses of ability to answer a determinist objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between ability as causes and ability with the act, and how it resolves the determinist objection regarding fear of God.

Core Idea
Ability must be distinguished as either the means or the act itself; confusing them leads to contradictions.

This passage presents Maturidi's response to a determinist challenge: if people fear God only after committing sin, then the prophets would not have feared. Maturidi shows that the problem lies in a flawed understanding of ability (qudra). He distinguishes two senses of ability and demonstrates that the determinist objection collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the dialectical structure: a question, two possible answers, a distinction, a counter-objection, and further arguments.
The passage uses conditional reasoning ('if...then...') throughout.
2
Identify the two senses of ability
Find where Maturidi introduces the distinction: 'If you mean by ability the causes...' and 'If you mean by it the ability that is with the act...'. Understand how each sense changes the answer.
The first sense refers to potential ability, the second to actual ability.
3
Follow the counter-arguments
Trace how Maturidi turns the opponent's own reasoning against them, especially the counter-question about God watching a prophet. Notice the pattern of forcing the opponent into absurdity.
The arguments about friends and enemies reinforce the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: سئل الوراق فقال: يقال لهم: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله وهو قادر لمراقبته الله؟ فإن قالوا: لا، أعظموا القول في وصف الأنبياء أنهم لم يفعلوا ذلك. وإن قالوا: نعم، لزمهم القول بها قبل الفعل. نقول له، وبالله التوفيق: إن عنيت بالقدرة الأسباب التي هي أحوال القدرة التي تعرض لا محالة لولا التضييع من العبد فبلى؛ وكل الأنبياء كذلك كانوا، وكذلك الأخيار. وإن أردت به القدرة التي هي مع الفعل أحلت السؤال وصرت كمن يقول: هل راقب الله أحد في إبقاء المعاصي وهو فاعل لها، وذلك مما لا معنى له. وهو يعارضك فيقول: هل راقب الله نبيا من الأنبياء في إبقاء معصية علمها منه أو أخبر عنه؟ فمهما أجاب من شيء فمثله الأول. ثم يقال: هل تفضل الله على أحد من أوليائه بمنع قدرة عدواته؟ فإن قال: نعم، قال: إن الله لم يعط أولياءه قدرة معاصيه، فعليه في أعدائه أيضا أنه لم يعطهم قوة طاعته، وفي ذلك ما أنكر آنفا. وإن قال: لا، زعم أنه أعطى أولياءه قوة عدواته؛ ومن قولهم: إنه لم يعط أعداءه قوة العداوة، فالآن صار إلى أن أعطى أولياءه قوتها، وذلك عظيم. ثم يقال: هل أعطى الله وليا قوى على تلك الطاعة حين الطاعة؟ فإن قال: لا، فالوحشة في طاعة لم يقو عليها ليست بدونها في اجتناب معصية لم يقو عليه، بل قوى على ترك المعصية. وعندهم لم يقو على الطاعة، وهذا أوحش. ثم يقال: هل والى الله وليا أو عاداه عدوا بفعل قوى عليه؟ فإن قال: نعم، أقر بالقوة مع الفعل وإن قال: لا، زعم أن العداوة والولاية بما لا يقوى عليه، وذلك بعيد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He said: al-Warraq was asked, and he said: It is said to them: Did anyone fear God's disobedience while being able, due to his watchfulness of God? If they say: No, they make a grave statement in describing the prophets that they did not do that. And if they say: Yes, then they must affirm it before the act. We say to him, and with God is success: If you mean by ability the causes which are the states of ability that necessarily occur unless the servant wastes them, then yes; and all the prophets were like that, and likewise the righteous. And if you mean by it the ability that is with the act, you have made the question impossible and become like one who says: Did God watch anyone in committing sins while being the doer of them? And that is meaningless. And he counters you saying: Did God watch a prophet among the prophets in committing a sin that He knew from him or informed about him? So whatever he answers with something, the first is similar to it. Then it is said: Did God favor any of His friends by preventing the ability of his enemies? If he says: Yes, he said: God did not give His friends the ability of His sins, so upon him regarding His enemies as well that He did not give them the power of His obedience, and in that is what was denied earlier. And if he says: No, he claims that He gave His friends the power of His enmity; and from their statement: He did not give His enemies the power of enmity, so now he has come to giving His friends its power, and that is great. Then it is said: Did God give a friend strength for that obedience at the time of obedience? If he says: No, then the dread in an obedience he was not strong for is not less than it in avoiding a sin he was not strong for; rather, he was strong for abandoning the sin. And according to them, he was not strong for obedience, and this is more dreadful. Then it is said: Did God befriend a friend or show enmity to an enemy through an act he was strong for? If he says: Yes, he affirmed the power with the act; and if he says: No, he claims that enmity and friendship are based on something he is not strong for, and that is far-fetched. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a determinist objection. It begins with a question posed to al-Warraq: whether anyone has ever feared God's disobedience while being able to do it, due to their watchfulness of God. If the objector says no, then they are claiming the prophets did not fear, which is a grave statement. If yes, then they must affirm that fear existed before the act (contradicting their claim that fear only occurs after). The author responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'ability' (qudra): first, ability as the causes (asbab), which are states that would necessarily occur unless the servant wastes them; in that sense, yes, prophets feared. Second, ability that is co-extensive with the act; that makes the question nonsensical, as it would mean asking if God watched someone while God Himself was the doer of the sins. Then the author presents a counter-objection: the opponent might ask whether God watched a prophet in committing a sin that God knew or informed about. Whatever answer the author gives, the same applies to the initial question. Further arguments are given regarding God granting ability to His friends and enemies, ending with the conclusion that the only consistent position is to affirm ability with the act in some sense, otherwise absurdities follow. The passage ends with the formula 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'.

Key Terms
قدرة
أسباب
مراقبة
تضييع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ability is a simple, univocal concept that applies the same way in all contexts.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'ability' (qudra) has two distinct meanings: potential ability (causes) and actual ability (co-extensive with the act). Confusing them leads to false conclusions.

Checkpoint
What distinction does Maturidi make in answering the question about fearing God?
A) between ability before the act and after the act
B) between ability as causes and ability with the act
C) between human ability and divine ability
D) between physical ability and spiritual ability

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's two-sense distinction of ability resolves the apparent contradiction in the determinist objection about fearing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you tend to blame a lack of ability or your own waste of God-given means? How does this lesson's distinction change how you view your own responsibility?
?
Consider a time you feared disobeying God. Did that fear come from recognizing your ability to sin, or from recognizing that God had given you the means to avoid it? What does that reveal about your reliance on Him?
?
If God's granting ability is conditional on His wisdom, how does that affect your trust in His justice when you see others sinning while seemingly 'able'? Will you now pray for them differently?

Who is More Praiseworthy: The Prophet or Iblis?
Maturidi answers which of two figures is more praiseworthy using the same distinction of ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the distinction between two types of ability to answer a paradoxical question about moral praiseworthiness.

Core Idea
Moral praiseworthiness depends on the type of ability, not just the action.

Maturidi continues his dialectic by posing a provocative comparison: a prophet who would sin if able versus Iblis who would obey if able. The answer hinges on what we mean by 'ability'. This passage refines the earlier distinction and applies it to a concrete case to show the absurdity of deterministic premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two questions posed and the two-part answer. Pay attention to the final phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله'.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two meanings of ability
The respondent distinguishes between 'the causes' (الأسباب) and 'the power that is with the act' (القوة التي معها الفعل). Understand how each leads to a different judgment of praiseworthiness.
The first meaning makes the prophet more praiseworthy; the second makes the question impossible.
3
Examine the parallel question about obedience
The second question mirrors the first: who is more obedient, one who obeys if befriended or one who disobeys if shown enmity? See how the same logic applies symmetrically.
This shows the consistency of the author's argument.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why Maturidi brings up this comparison. It serves to expose the absurdity of the deterministic view that ability determines action. If ability is taken as a power that necessitates action, the question becomes meaningless.
This connects to the broader theological debate on free will and predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال: من أحمد: من لو قدر على المعصية عصى وهو النبي، أو من لو قدر على الطاعة أطاع وهو إبليس؟ قيل: إن عنيت الأسباب فالأول، وإن عنيت القوة التي معها الفعل أحلت، ومثله عليك في العلم والخبر. ثم يقال له: من أطوع الله: من لو والاه الله أطاعه، أو من لو عاداه عصاه؟ فبأي شيء يجيب في ذلك فهو له في الأول جواب. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He said: Who is more praiseworthy: one who if he were able to sin would sin, and he is the prophet, or one who if he were able to obey would obey, and he is Iblis? It was said: If you mean the causes, then the first; and if you mean the power that is with the act, then you have made it impossible, and the same applies to you regarding knowledge and information. Then it is said to him: Who is more obedient to God: one who if God befriended him he would obey, or one who if God showed enmity to him he would disobey? So whatever he answers with in that, then it is for him the same answer as the first. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage continues the same dialectic. The questioner asks who is more praiseworthy (ahmad): a prophet who would sin if able, or Iblis who would obey if able. The author's response again applies the distinction: if by ability you mean the causes (asbab), then the prophet is more praiseworthy because he has the causes but refrains; if you mean the power concomitant with the act, the question becomes impossible (ahalta). Then a parallel question: who is more obedient to God: one who would obey if God befriends him, or one who would disobey if God shows enmity? The answer is symmetric. Again the point is that the deterministic premise leads to absurdities.

Key Terms

أطوع
ولاية
عداوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That moral praiseworthiness depends solely on the action itself, without considering the type of ability one has.
Correction: The passage shows that whether someone is more praiseworthy depends on which meaning of 'ability' is used. If ability means causes, the prophet is more praiseworthy; if it means power that necessitates the act, the question is absurd.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, who is more praiseworthy when ability is understood as the causes?
A) The prophet
B) Iblis
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the question of who is more praiseworthy cannot be answered without specifying which meaning of 'ability' is intended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own sins, do you attribute them to your 'ability' in the sense of having the causes to sin, or to a power that leaves you no choice? How does your answer affect your responsibility before God?
?

?
The passage ends with 'There is no power except with God.' Do you truly believe that in moments of moral weakness, or do you assume you have independent power? How can you cultivate a deeper dependence on God's help in resisting sin?

Inability as Excuse: When It Holds and When It Does Not
Maturidi refutes the claim that inability is always an excuse by distinguishing prevention from waste.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Maturidi, a claim of inability is a valid excuse only when the inability is genuinely prevented and not due to the servant's own waste (tadyi').

Core Idea
Inability excuses only when not self-inflicted.

This lesson focuses on Maturidi's initial refutation of the determinist's claim that 'I couldn't do it' is the ultimate excuse. He argues that such an excuse is valid only in cases where the inability is a genuine prevention from outside, not when the servant himself has wasted his capacity or prevented its occurrence through his own action. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine justice and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the opening sentence to see what the determinist argues: that the best excuse is inability, and it applies to the unseen world just as in the witnessed world.
Note the phrase 'فمثله في الغائب' suggesting analogy between seen and unseen.
2
Observe Maturidi's response
Notice how he immediately distinguishes: the excuse works only in what prevents ability from him, not in what he wasted completely (ما ضيعها باتا). This is the core distinction.
3
Understand the concept of waste (tadyi')
Reflect on what it means to 'waste' ability. It implies the servant had the means to act but neglected them, thus forfeiting the excuse.
Compare with the example of ignorance in the next lesson.
4
Apply to the unseen world
Ask yourself: How does this distinction apply to religious obligations? Maturidi argues that the same principle holds in matters of faith and action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال آخر: إنه لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم من أن يقول لو قيل له: لم لا فعلت كذا؟ فيقول: لأني لم أقدر عليه، فمثله في الغائب. قيل: هذا يكون عذرا فيما يمنع عنه القدرة، لا فيها ضيعها باتا، وما منع حدوث القدرة. وكذلك أيضا في الشاهد لا عذر أوسع من أن يقول: لم أعلم أمرك ولا نهيك ولا علمت أن فعلي يغضبك. فإن لم يكن عذرا بما أعطى ما لو لم يترك طلبه ليبلغه فمثله في القوة وبعد، فإنه لا عذر على ذلك أيضا أعظم من أن يقول: لأنك أخبرت أني لا أفعل وكذلك علمت، فقلت: لو فعلت لصيرتك جاهلا كاذبا، فلم أفعل لهذا؛ وأن يقول أيضا: لي عليك أعظم المنة، لأنك أقدرتني عليه وجعلت أمر ربوبيتك في يدي وأقدرتني على نقضه، فلي عليك أعظم المنن، وعندك أكثر الأيادي. فمهما أجاب من شيء فذلك أعظم منه جوابا له. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And another said: There is no excuse for the servant in the witnessed world greater than that he says, if it was said to him: Why did you not do such-and-such? He says: Because I was not able to do it. So the like of that applies to the unseen. It was said: This is an excuse in what prevents ability from him, not in what he wasted completely, and what does not prevent the occurrence of ability. Likewise also in the witnessed world, there is no excuse broader than that he says: I did not know your command nor your prohibition, nor did I know that my act angers you. If then it is not an excuse because he was given what, if he had not neglected seeking it, it would have reached him, then similarly regarding power and after that, there is no excuse also greater than that he says: Because You informed that I will not do, and likewise You knew, so I said: If I did, I would make You ignorant and lying, so I did not do for this; and he also says: I have the greater favor upon You, because You empowered me over it and placed the matter of Your lordship in my hand and empowered me to violate it, so I have the greatest favors upon You, and with You are more favors. So whatever he answers with something, then that is a greater answer against him. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage refutes the argument that inability is an absolute excuse. The opponent claims that in the witnessed world, the best excuse is 'I couldn't do it', and similarly in the unseen (i.e., regarding divine commands). The author responds that this excuse works only when the inability is not due to the servant's own negligence (tadyi') and when it is a prevention that does not allow new ability to occur. Then he gives another example: ignorance of the command is an excuse only if the servant did not neglect seeking knowledge. If he had the means to know, it is not an excuse. Then a further argument: the servant might say, 'Because You informed that I will not do, so I refrained to avoid making You a liar,' which turns the blame back on God. This is a reductio ad absurdum showing the deterministic position leads to claiming that God owes thanks to the servant. The final conclusion is that any answer the determinist gives is weaker than this counter-argument.

Key Terms
عذر
شاهد
غائب
تضييع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any claim of inability is a valid excuse for failing to fulfill an obligation.
Correction: Inability is only an excuse if the inability was not caused by the servant's own negligence or waste of capacity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, when is the excuse of 'I was unable' valid?
A) Always, because it is the best excuse
B) Only when the ability is truly prevented from the servant, not when he wasted it
C) Only in the witnessed world, not the unseen
D) Never, because the servant always has ability

Key Takeaway
The excuse of inability is conditional: it excuses only when the inability is not due to the servant's own waste (tadyi').

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I can't' regarding an obligation—like praying on time, seeking knowledge, or avoiding sin—are you genuinely prevented, or have you allowed laziness, procrastination, or worldly distractions to waste the ability Allah gave you? What would it cost you to be honest about that right now?
?
Think of one area in your life where you have repeatedly used the excuse of inability. Have you ever actively sought to remove that obstacle? If not, what does your silence reveal about your true prioritization of Allah's commands?
?
If Allah were to ask you on the Day of Judgment about a duty you neglected, and you said 'I couldn't,' would He accept that excuse? Based on this lesson, how would you know? What concrete step can you take today to ensure you are not wasting the ability He has already given you?

Further Excuses: Ignorance and Divine Foreknowledge Do Not Absolve Negligence
Maturidi refutes two more deterministic excuses: ignorance of the command and reliance on divine foreknowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the excuses of ignorance and the claim that divine foreknowledge of our actions exempts us from responsibility.

Core Idea
Knowing God knows you won't do something is no excuse to not do it.

Maturidi continues his refutation by addressing two more deterministic arguments. First, the excuse of ignorance: if a person does not know the command, is that a valid excuse? He answers that it is only if the person did not neglect seeking knowledge. Secondly, the determinist might argue that since God knows and informs that a person will not do an act, the person refrains to avoid making God a liar. Maturidi shows this leads to the absurd conclusion that the servant has a favor over God.

Guided Path
1
Read the ignorance example
Notice how Maturidi presents the second example: the excuse 'I did not know' is similarly conditional. He says it is not an excuse if the person was given the means to know but neglected seeking it.
The phrase 'بما أعطى ما لو لم يترك طلبه ليبلغه' indicates that the means were available.
2
Analyze the deterministic argument about foreknowledge
The determinist says: 'You informed that I will not do, and You knew, so I refrained to avoid making You ignorant and lying.' Understand this argument: it tries to shift blame to God's knowledge.
3
See the reductio ad absurdum
The determinist then adds another argument: 'I have the greater favor upon You because You empowered me and placed Your lordship in my hand.' This reduces to claiming that God owes the servant thanks for being able to disobey. Maturidi shows the absurdity.
The conclusion: any answer the determinist gives is weaker than this counterargument.
4
Summarize the lesson
Reflect on how Maturidi systematically shows that deterministic excuses ultimately blame God and are invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So whatever he answers with something, then that is a greater answer against him. And there is no power except with God.

The opponent claims that in the witnessed world, the best excuse is 'I couldn't do it', and similarly in the unseen (i.e., regarding divine commands). Correcting the Question About God Willing Evil
Maturidi corrects a Mu'tazilite question about God willing evil and provides a logical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'Does God intend to be insulted?' is improper and how Maturidi reframes it to affirm God's wisdom and will.

Core Idea
The proper question is about God's intent for the moral status of actions, not direct intent for evil.

Maturidi addresses a Mu'tazilite objection about divine will and evil actions. He first corrects a misinterpretation of a Quranic verse, then sharpens the theological question. This lesson walks through his correction and the underlying argument about divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a citation, a correction, a rephrased question, and an argument.
Pay attention to the line 'أيريد الله أن يُشتَم؟' — the key error.
2
Identify the opponent's error
The opponent asks whether God intends to be insulted. Maturidi says this is wrong. Why? Because it anthropomorphizes God's intention as directly related to the insult.
Compare with the corrected formulation.
3
Understand the corrected formulation
Maturidi says the proper question is: 'Does God intend that the act of insult be ugly and displeasing?' This refocuses on the moral quality of the action, not the action's occurrence.
This aligns with divine wisdom (hakim).
4
Follow the argument about divine wisdom
The Shaykh argues that if one denies that God wills evil actions, then either God is not wise (if He wills falsehood) or one is compelled to affirm will, otherwise there is falsehood. This shows the necessity of affirming divine will for all occurrences.
This is a logical dilemma.
5
Conclusion and broader implication
Maturidi concludes that the prohibition and anger are not from the same aspect as will; this suffices for the topic of creation of actions.
This clarifies the distinction between moral and ontological will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في قوله: {وما تشاؤن إلا أن يشاء الله} الآية: إن الاستقامة كانت بها، وذلك فاسد، لأن الله قد شاء فلا يكون؛ فلا يجوز فيما يكون أن يكون بها إلا على قولنا إنه يكون لا محالة إذا شاء الله. ثم قال: أيريد الله أن يُشتَم؟ وقد أخطأ في السؤال، بل حقه أن يقال: أيريد الله أن يكون فعل الشتم ممن يشتمه قبيحا مسخوطا؟ ثم قال: معاذ الله، لأنه نهى عنه وغضب عليه، ولا يفعل الحكيم ذلك. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: قيل: أحكيم من لا يشاء ذلك: مما لو كان الذي شاء كاذبا سفيها؟ فإن قال: نعم، بان جهله بالحكيم؛ وإن قال: لا، أَلزِمَ القول بالمشيئة، إذ في فوت ذلك كذبه وسفهه. ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن النهي ليس من الوجه الذي ذكرنا، وكذلك الغضب. وهذا النوع مما قد ذكرنا منه هو الكافي في باب خلق الأفعال.
And he said concerning His saying: {And you do not will unless God wills} the verse: 'That uprightness occurs through it [the human will],' and that is corrupt, because God has willed but it does not occur; so it is not permissible that what occurs occurs through it except on our saying that it indeed occurs inevitably when God wills. Then he said: 'Does God intend to be insulted?' And he erred in the question; rather its proper phrasing is: 'Does God intend that the act of insult from the one who insults Him be ugly and displeasing?' Then he said: 'God forbid, because He forbade it and is angry at it, and the Wise does not do that.' The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: It is said: Is it wise for one who does not will that: such that if what he willed were false and foolish? If he says yes, his ignorance of the Wise becomes clear; and if he says no, he is compelled to affirm will, because in its absence would be his falsehood and foolishness. And there is no power except with God. Moreover, the prohibition is not from the aspect we mentioned, and likewise the anger. And this type is sufficient from what we have already mentioned in the chapter on the creation of actions.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite argument about scriptural verses on will. The opponent claims that uprightness (istiqāmah) occurs through the human will, meaning human will is the effective agent. Maturidi rejects this: if God wills something but it does not occur (as in the case of disobedience), then uprightness cannot be solely through human will; rather, it occurs inevitably when God wills. Then Maturidi corrects the opponent's formulation of a question: 'Does God intend to be insulted?' is wrong because it anthropomorphizes God. The correct question is: 'Does God intend that the act of insult from the insulter be ugly and displeasing?' The opponent then exclaims that God would not do that because He forbids it. The Shaykh (Maturidi himself or a commentator) then argues that if one denies that God wills evil acts, then either God is not wise (if He wills what is false/foolish) or the opponent is compelled to affirm will for everything that occurs, because otherwise the absence of will would imply falsehood and foolishness. Thus the objection fails. Maturidi adds that the prohibition and anger are not from the same aspect as the will to create; this suffices for the topic.

Key Terms
مشيئة (mashī'ah)
إرادة (irādah)
حكيم (ḥakīm)
فعل الشتم (fi'l al-shatm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That asking 'Does God intend to be insulted?' is a valid theological question.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that the correct question is: 'Does God intend that the act of insult be ugly and displeasing?' because God's will relates to the moral status of actions, not directly to being insulted.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the opponent erred in asking 'Does God intend to be insulted?'
A) Because insult is a human action, not a divine action
B) Because the correct question is about God intending the existence of the act as ugly and displeasing
C) Because God never intends anything related to evil
D) Because the question implies God is subject to insult

Key Takeaway
Proper theological inquiry requires precise formulation; God's will encompasses all events but is distinguished from His moral commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or witness evil, do you ever fall into the trap of questioning God's wisdom because you cannot see the good in it? How does this lesson remind you that God's will for the existence of evil is distinct from His approval? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that God hates the sin but still allows it for a greater purpose? Will you trust His wisdom even when you don't understand?
?
Have you ever found yourself asking about God in an anthropomorphic way, as the Mu'tazilite did when he asked 'Does God intend to be insulted?'? In what areas of your life do you need to purify your theology and ask the right questions about God's attributes? What specific steps can you take to learn the proper language for discussing divine actions?
?
The logical dilemma here forces us to acknowledge that God wills all that occurs. This includes actions you regret or sins you commit. Do you truly accept that your every act occurs by God's will, yet you are responsible? How does this tension between divine decree and human accountability affect your repentance and your reliance on God's mercy? Will you commit today to humbly accept both truths without rationalizing away either?

Divine Power and the Prevention of Evil
Maturidi answers an objection about divine power and prevention of evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's ability to prevent evil does not obligate Him to do so, and that His not preventing is due to His will, not impotence.

Core Idea
Power does not compel action; will determines whether power is exercised.

Imam Maturidi addresses a common objection: if God can prevent evil, why doesn't He? The objection seems to imply that God lacks power if He does not prevent. Maturidi's response clarifies the relationship between divine power and will, showing that God's not preventing is an act of wisdom and free choice, not weakness. This lesson examines that response and its proof from the nature of compelled faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the argument. Note the exchange between the objector and Maturidi.
The passage is a reply to an objection; try to identify each part.
2
Identify the objection and the core response
Find where the objector says 'if He is able to prevent but does not, then He is not prevented' (i.e., He lacks power). Then note Maturidi's counter: power without will does not indicate inability.
The response hinges on the distinction between qudrah (power) and iradah (will).
3
Analyze the proof by analogy
Maturidi gives the example of compelling people to Islam: if God forced them, they would not be genuine Muslims. This shows that not preventing evil is consistent with power and wisdom.
The proof uses a shahid (witnessed) example to clarify the ghayb (unseen).
4
Reflect on the key takeaway
Consider how this response safeguards both God's power and human moral responsibility. The lesson shows that evil existing in the world does not contradict God's perfect power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب لما عورض من الزيادة في ملكه ما لا يريده بالرضا والمحبة، وقد بينا التفريق في ذلك بما هو فعله. ثم قال: إذا قدر على المنع فلم يمنع فليس بممنوع. فيقال له: لو قدر وهو لا يريده ليمنع، فدل كونه بلا إرادة أنه لم يقدر. ومما يبين ذلك أنه لو قهرهم على الإسلام لم يكونوا مسلمين قهرا، يبين أنه لم يكن يقدر على ذلك، وذلك حق الغلبة والقهر في الشاهد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And he answered when he was objected to regarding the increase in His kingdom of what He does not will, by love and approval, and we have explained the distinction in that by what is His action. Then he said: 'If He is able to prevent but does not prevent, then He is not prevented.' So it is said to him: If He is able while He does not will it, would He prevent? For His being without will indicates that He did not have power. And among what clarifies that is that if He had compelled them to Islam, they would not have been Muslims by compulsion; it clarifies that He was not able to do that, and that is the reality of overcoming and compulsion in the witnessed world. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about increase in God's kingdom. The opponent had used love and approval to answer the issue of un-willed acts being an increase in His kingdom. Maturidi first notes the distinction has already been explained. Then the opponent says: if God is able to prevent evil but does not, then He is not prevented (i.e., He lacks power). The response is: if He is able and does not will to prevent, that does not indicate lack of power; rather, if He had no will, He would not have power. The proof is that if God had compelled people to be Muslims by force, they would not truly be Muslims; that shows that God's not preventing is due to His will to grant free choice, not impotence. This aligns with the witnessed reality of force and compulsion.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudrah)
منع (man')
قهر (qahr)
إرادة (irādah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's ability to prevent evil means He must prevent it; otherwise He lacks power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that power does not compel action; God's will determines whether He prevents. Not preventing is an act of wisdom, not impotence.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute the claim that if God could prevent evil but does not, then He is not powerful?
A) He says God is not able to prevent evil
B) He says God's not preventing is due to His will, not lack of power
C) He says the objection only applies to human kings
D) He says God prevents evil in another world

Key Takeaway
Divine power and will are distinct: God's not preventing evil is a deliberate act of wisdom, not a sign of impotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider the example of forced belief: can you honestly say your submission to God is genuine and not coerced by circumstances? Are there areas where you only obey out of fear or habit rather than sincere will? What would it take to transform your actions into true submission?
?
If God's power is always accompanied by His will, are you relying on your own will to change your life, or are you surrendering to His will, trusting that His power works through His decree? What is one area where you need to submit your will to His today?

Answering the Objection of God's Leaving Sinners
Maturidi answers the objection about God's leaving sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's decision to leave sinners without compulsion does not contradict His will, and that it falls under the same reasoning as the earlier discussion about increase in the kingdom.

Core Idea
Leaving is an act of will, not its absence.

Here Maturidi addresses a subtle objection: if God does not compel sinners to obey, does that not mean He is adding something to His kingdom that He does not will? Maturidi's answer is concise but profound: the act of leaving ('tark') itself is willed by God. Therefore, the earlier explanation about increase in the kingdom applies.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is raised and then immediately answered.
The objection is implied, not stated explicitly in this passage.
2
Understand the logic of 'tark'
Consider what 'leaving' means in this context. Does it imply indifference? Maturidi says no—leaving is a deliberate act that is willed by God.
3
Connect to the earlier argument
Maturidi says 'so what we mentioned attaches.' Think about how the earlier reasoning about increase in kingdom applies here too.
This shows consistency in Maturidi's theology.

Source Text

Maturidi's answer: the act of leaving (tark) does not contradict God's will; it is a deliberate withholding of compulsion. Therefore, the earlier response about increase (i.e., that un-willed acts are willed for existence in a secondary sense) applies here as well. The phrase 'so what we mentioned attaches' means the previous reasoning covers this case.

Why the Analogy with Kings Fails
Maturidi refutes the analogy between God and human kings regarding inability to prevent un-willed acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key reasons why the analogy between God and human kings is invalid in matters of divine power and will.

Core Idea
Human kings lack the power and authority to prevent all un-willed acts; God is not like that.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses an objection that draws a parallel between God and earthly kings: if a king does not prevent a crime, it seems he is weak. The same is argued about God and evil. Maturidi dismantles this analogy with two clear reasons, showing the fundamental differences in power and authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage presents two reasons why the analogy is mistaken. Find each one and notice how Maturidi introduces them.
The reasons are introduced with 'أحدهما' and 'الثاني'.
3
Understand the first reason
Maturidi argues that if a human king could prevent all un-willed acts, he would. His failure to do so shows his inability. This contrasts with God, who has all power.
4
Understand the second reason
Earthly kings have no authority over the internal acts of others. Even if they could prevent some actions, they do not own the actions of their subjects. God's sovereignty is absolute.
5
Reflect on the final statement
The passage ends with 'And there is no power except with God', reminding us that all true power belongs to Allah alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارض بمثله في الشاهد، وهو خطأ لوجهين: أحدهما أن ملكنا لا يقدر على المنع، وإلا كان يمنع عن كل شيء لم يرده. والثاني أن ذلك ليس في ملكه ولا سلطانه، لما ليس لملك الأرض على أفعال غيره ملك ولا سلطان. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And he objected by something similar in the witnessed world, and it is mistaken for two reasons: One is that our king is not able to prevent, otherwise he would prevent everything he does not will. The second is that that [act] is not in his kingdom nor his authority, because the king of the earth has no kingdom or authority over the actions of others. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection that draws an analogy between God and human kings: if a human king does not prevent a crime, it suggests he lacks power, so by analogy, God's not preventing evil implies lack of power. Maturidi points out two errors. First, human kings are indeed unable to prevent all unwanted actions; if they could, they would. Second, the actions of subjects are not within the king's authority or kingdom—earthly kings have no sovereignty over the internal acts or choices of others. Therefore the analogy fails. God's not preventing is due to His will and wisdom, not impotence.

Key Terms
شاهد (shāhid)
ملك (malik)
منع (man')
سلطان (sultān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy between God and a human king is valid because both can prevent unwanted actions if they are powerful.
Correction: Maturidi shows that human kings are inherently limited: they cannot prevent all unwanted acts and they have no authority over the actions of others. Therefore the analogy fails.

Checkpoint

A) Human kings are not able to prevent all unwanted acts, and they have no authority over others' actions
B) Human kings do not want to prevent evil, and they have different laws
C) Human kings are often unjust, and their power is limited
D) Human kings have no will, and their actions are coerced

Key Takeaway
The analogy between God and human kings collapses because human kings are neither omnipotent nor sovereign over the actions of others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

You trust Allah's power with your lips, but do you truly believe that everything He allows has a wisdom, even if you cannot see it? What is one area of your life where you struggle to accept His decree, and what step will you take to surrender that to Him today?
?
If you truly internalized that 'there is no power except with Allah,' how would your reliance on people, your anxiety about the future, and your fear of evil change? Name one specific habit you will change starting today to live this truth.
