Refuting al-Ka'bi on God's Decree of Disbelief
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi on God's decree of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's argument that God cannot decree disbelief because His decree is truth and disbelief is false.

Core Idea
God's decree is true even when it decrees falsehood, like a judge's true decree of tyranny.

Al-Ka'bi raised a subtle objection: if God's decree is absolute truth, how can He decree something false like disbelief? Maturidi responds by exposing a confusion in the meaning of 'decree' and uses a powerful analogy to show that decreeing evil does not make the decree evil. Let's walk through his refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ka'bi's claim and Maturidi's initial objection
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi first criticizes al-Ka'bi for concealing the true scope of qada (decree).
Al-Ka'bi's claim is that God does not decree disbelief at all.
2
Analyze the analogy of the judge
Maturidi compares God's decree of disbelief to a judge's decree of an act of tyranny. The decree is true even though the act is unjust. Understand the logic: the decree is a true description of the act.
The analogy shows that the value of the decree (true/false) is separate from the value of the decreed thing (good/evil).
3
Reflect on the hadith
Al-Ka'bi himself cites a hadith about being content with God's decree. Maturidi uses this as further evidence that God indeed decrees all things, including trials.
The hadith implies that there is a decree to be content with, so disbelief must be part of that decree.
4
Summarize the refutation
Putting it all together: Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by clarifying that 'decree' is a comprehensive term, and that decreeing falsehood does not make the decree false.
This lesson establishes that God's decree is just and true regardless of the nature of what is decreed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكعبي زعم أن الله لا يقضى الكفر، ثم فستر وجوه القضاء وجعله في بعض فسر. فإنكاره في الجملة على احتمال ذلك في أحد الوجوه خطأ. ثم احتج بأن الكفر متفاوت وباطل، وقضاء الله حق وصواب. كمن لا يعلم أن القضاء بالباطل أنه باطل وبالمتفاوت أنه متفاوت عدل وحق، وكذا قضاء الحكام بأفعال الجور والظلم أنها جور غير باطل ولا متفاوت حتى كاد يعرفها الصبيان. فمن جهل ذلك ثم ادعى حدود الكلام، فحق الكلام عليه أن يعرف ما الكلام. ولا قوة إلا بالله. واحتج بما روى عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: قال تعالى جده: من لم يرض بقضائي ولم يصبر على بلائي فليتخذ ربا سوائي.
And al-Ka'bi claimed that God does not decree disbelief, then he concealed the aspects of qada and restricted it to some [meanings] and misinterpreted. So his denial in general, given the possibility of that [meaning] in one of the aspects, is an error. Then he argued that disbelief is varied and false, while God's decree is truth and correctness. Like one who does not know that decreeing falsehood is falsehood, and decreeing what is varied is varied—it is justice and truth. Likewise, the decree of judges concerning acts of tyranny and oppression: they are tyranny, not false [in being tyranny] and not varied [in being tyranny]—even children nearly know that. So whoever is ignorant of that and then claims [knowledge of] the limits of speech (kalam), then the right of speech upon him is that he know what speech is. And there is no power except with God. And he argued with what was narrated from the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, that he said: 'God Almighty said: Whoever is not content with My decree and does not bear My trial patiently, let him seek a lord other than Me.'
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the argument of al-Ka'bi, who claimed that God does not decree disbelief. Maturidi first points out that al-Ka'bi concealed the various meanings of qada and limited it to just some, misinterpreting it. His outright denial is an error because there is a legitimate aspect of qada that can include all things, even disbelief. Then al-Ka'bi's argument is presented: he argues that disbelief is varied and false, whereas God's decree is true and correct, so God cannot decree falsehood. Maturidi refutes this by analogy: a judge who decrees an act of tyranny is not decreeing falsehood in the sense that the act is not a false act of tyranny—it is truly an act of tyranny. Similarly, God's decree of disbelief does not make His decree false; rather, He decrees disbelief as disbelief, which is a true description of the act. The author then rebukes al-Ka'bi for claiming expertise in kalam (theological speech) while being ignorant of such basic distinctions; he says such a person should first learn what speech is. Finally, al-Ka'bi cites a hadith (divine saying) that warns against discontent with God's decree, which Maturidi presumably uses to support that God does indeed decree all things, including trials. The passage thus refutes al-Ka'bi's position by showing that the meaning of 'decree' includes all events, and that God's decree is just regardless of the nature of the decreed thing.

Key Terms
الكعبي (al-Ka'bi)
قضاء (qada)
كفر (disbelief)
حق وصواب (truth and correctness)
كلام (kalam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because God's decree is absolute truth, He cannot decree false or evil things.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that decreeing something false does not make the decree itself false; it is a true decree of that false thing, just as a judge's decree of tyranny is true.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to refute al-Ka'bi's argument?
A) A king decreeing a law
B) A judge decreeing an act of tyranny
C) A teacher grading a student's work
D) A farmer planting seeds

Key Takeaway
God's decree is true and just even when it decrees falsehood or evil, because the decree itself is a true statement about the nature of the decreed thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you truly content with God's decree when it brings you hardship or trial? What would it mean for you to 'seek a lord other than Him' if you are not content?
?
When you face something that seems unjust or evil in your life, do you accuse God of being unjust, or do you trust that His decree is truth and correctness, as Maturidi argues? How can you cultivate that trust in your heart starting today?
?
Have you ever questioned God's wisdom in decreeing disbelief for some people? How does this lesson challenge you to accept that God's decree is true and just, even when you don't understand it? Will you commit to not questioning His decree?

Maturidi's Refutation of al-Ka'bi on Divine Decree of Disbelief
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's denial of divine decree of disbelief via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that Allah's decree of disbelief is not rendered false by the falseness of disbelief itself.

Core Idea
A decree about falsehood is not itself false; divine decree is independent of its object.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi responds to the Mu'tazili scholar al-Ka'bi, who argued that since disbelief is false and evil, Allah cannot decree it. Maturidi dismantles this argument by distinguishing between the decree itself and what is decreed, using a powerful analogy from judicial rulings. Understanding this refutation clarifies a key Maturidi doctrine on divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim
Notice that al-Ka'bi denies that Allah decrees disbelief, and his reason that decree must be true and good.
Al-Ka'bi's position is a classic Mu'tazili view.
3
Examine Maturidi's analogy
The analogy of a judge's decree on injustice shows that the decree itself can be just even if the subject is unjust.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that those ignorant of this distinction should not claim expertise in kalam.
This underscores the importance of precise definitions in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آراء الكعبي في القضاء والقدر وبيان فسادها. والكعبي زعم أن الله لا يقضي الكفر، ثم فسر وجوه القضاء وجعله في بعض فسر. فإنكاره في الجملة على احتمال ذلك في أحد الوجوه خطأ. ثم احتج بأن الكفر متفاوت وباطل، وقضاء الله حق وصواب. كمن لا يعلم أن القضاء بالباطل أنه باطل وبالمتفاوت أنه متفاوت عدل وحق، وكذا قضاء الحكام بأفعال الجور والظلم أنها جور غير باطل ولا متفاوت حتى كاد يعرفها الصبيان. فمن جهل ذلك ثم ادعى حدود الكلام، فحق الكلام عليه أن يعرف ما الكلام. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Al-Ka'bi claimed that Allah does not decree disbelief, then he interpreted the aspects of decree and placed it in some interpretation. So his denial in general, on the possibility of that in one of the aspects, is an error. Then he argued that disbelief is disparate and false, while Allah's decree is truth and correct. This is like one who does not know that the decree concerning falsehood is falsehood and concerning the disparate is disparate, justice and truth, and likewise the decree of judges concerning acts of injustice and oppression is injustice, not false and not disparate, to the point that children almost know it. So whoever is ignorant of that and then claims the limits of kalam, then the right of kalam upon him is that he knows what kalam is. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Ka'bi's (a Mu'tazili) view that Allah does not decree disbelief. Maturidi argues that al-Ka'bi's denial is erroneous because he misinterpreted the meaning of decree. Al-Ka'bi claimed that decrees must be true and good, while disbelief is false and bad, so Allah cannot decree it. Maturidi counters by pointing out that a decree is not the same as what is decreed; a decree of falsehood is still a decree, just as a judge's decree concerning an unjust act is itself just and true in the sense of being a correct ruling. The analogy shows that the quality of the decree is independent of the quality of the object. The passage concludes that those who are ignorant of this basic distinction should not claim to be masters of kalam.

كفر
باطل
حق
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That decreeing something makes it identical in quality to the thing decreed.
Correction: The decree itself can be just even if the decreed thing is evil; the decree's truth is independent of the decreed object.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider al-Ka'bi's argument that Allah cannot decree kufr because kufr is false and Allah's decree is true to be invalid?
A) Because Allah can decree anything He wills without restriction.
B) Because the decree of falsehood is not itself false; the decree's truth is independent of the nature of the decreed.
C) Because kufr is not actually false; it is a test from Allah.
D) Because al-Ka'bi misinterpreted the Quranic verses about decree.

Key Takeaway
Allah's decree of disbelief is a true and just decree, even though disbelief itself is false; the decree's quality is independent of the decreed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson shows that a decree can be true even if the thing decreed is false. Is there an area of your life where you have confused the quality of a divine decree with the quality of what is decreed? What would change if you separated them?
?
Al-Ka'bi's error stemmed from judging Allah's decree by human standards. In what ways might you be unconsciously imposing your own sense of 'good and bad' onto Allah's actions? What step can you take tonight to align your heart with His decree?

Being Pleased with Divine Decree: The Evil of Disbelief
Al-Faqih explains being pleased with decree by citing the hadith and defining its meaning regarding disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that being pleased with Allah's decree regarding disbelief means recognizing its inherent evil and just punishment, and that disbelief is the servant's act, not Allah's decree.

Core Idea
Pleasure with decree means accepting the justice of judgment, not approving of sin.

This lesson unpacks a hadith qudsi that seems to demand unconditional acceptance of Allah's decree. Al-Faqih clarifies that the decree in question concerns the evil of disbelief itself, not the act of sin. He explains that true pleasure with the decree is the recognition that disbelief is evil and justly punished, and that this decree is distinct from the servant's act.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and al-Faqih's statement
Begin by reading the hadith qudsi and al-Faqih's initial comment that this is like the first. This sets the context.
2
Identify what 'pleased with decree' means
Focus on al-Faqih's explanation: it means knowing that disbelief is contemptible, ugly, evil, and necessitates punishment unless repentance occurs.
3
Understand the distinction between decree and act
Notice that al-Faqih clarifies that disbelief is the act of the servant, not Allah's decree. The decree is the just determination of the act's outcome.
This is a key theological point against those who say God decrees evil acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بما روى عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : قال تعالى جده : من لم يرض بقضائي ولم يصبر على بلائي فليتخذ ربا سوائي. قال الفقيه رحمه الله : هذا مثل الأول، وإن الرضا بقضائه أن تعلم بأن الكفر مضمحل قبيح، وأنه شر وفساد، وأنه يوجب مقت صاحبه وتعذيبه إلا أن يتوب؛ فمن لم يرض بهذا فهو كافر، فيكون على ما جاء به الخبر على أن الكفر والقبح هو فعل العبد، ومحال أن يكون قضاءه، فثبت أن قضاء الله هو ما ذكرت مما عليه حقيقة الفعل. ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن حقيقة الخبر في الأمراض والمصائب. ألا يرى أن التخليد في النار من قضائه عند المعتزلة، وكذلك الخذلان والإضلال ونحو ذلك. فليرض الكعبي لنفسه ذلك، وإلا طلب ربا سواه. والمعتزلة يقولون : ليس الله القضاء بالأمراض والمصائب في الذين لا ذنب لهم إلا بالعوض، فإذا هم لا يرضون بها حتى يُعطوا عليها العوض، وذلك معنى ما روي «فليتّخذ ربا سوائي».
And he argued with what was narrated from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, that he said: Allah, exalted is His majesty, said: 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree and does not bear patiently with My trial, let him take a lord other than Me.' Al-Faqih, may Allah have mercy on him, said: This is like the first. And that being pleased with His decree is that you know that disbelief is contemptible[?] and ugly, and that it is evil and corruption, and that it necessitates wrath upon its doer and punishment unless he repents. So whoever is not pleased with this is a disbeliever. Thus it is according to what the report came that disbelief and ugliness are the act of the servant, and impossible that they be His decree. So it is established that Allah's decree is what I mentioned concerning what the reality of the act is. And there is no power except with Allah. Indeed, the reality of the report concerns illnesses and calamities. Do you not see that eternal damnation in the Fire is from His decree according to the Mu'tazila, and likewise abandonment and misguidance and the like? So let al-Ka'bi be pleased with that for himself, or else let him seek a lord other than me. And the Mu'tazila say: Allah does not decree illnesses and calamities upon those who have no sin except with compensation, so they are not pleased with them unless they are given compensation for them. That is the meaning of what was narrated: 'Let him take a lord other than me.'
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage interprets the hadith qudsi: 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree... let him take a lord other than Me.' Al-Faqih explains that 'being pleased with the decree' means recognizing that disbelief is inherently evil and deserves punishment; therefore, one who is not pleased with this reality is actually rejecting Allah's judgment. The decree itself is not the act of disbelief but the just determination that the act will occur. The passage then contrasts the Mu'tazili view that calamities are decreed with compensation, implying that they are not truly pleased with such decrees unless compensated. The argument is that al-Ka'bi and the Mu'tazila are inconsistent: they claim to reject decreeing evil, yet they accept God's decree of eternal punishment for sinners, and they only accept calamities conditionally.

Key Terms
رضا بالقضاء
كفر
مقت
تعذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that being pleased with divine decree means approving of sin or evil because it is decreed.
Correction: Al-Faqih clarifies that being pleased with the decree means recognizing the evil of disbelief and accepting that it justly deserves punishment, not approving of the act itself.

Checkpoint
According to al-Faqih, what does it mean to be pleased with Allah's decree concerning disbelief?
A) To accept that disbelief is good because Allah decreed it.
B) To know that disbelief is evil and that it deserves punishment, and to affirm that this decree is just.
C) To passively submit to disbelief without complaining.
D) To believe that Allah does not actually decree disbelief.

Key Takeaway
Being pleased with Allah's decree regarding disbelief means recognizing its evil and just punishment, not approving of the act itself, which is the servant's responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone reject Islam, do you feel a sense of pity that undermines Allah's decree that disbelief is worthy of punishment? How can you train your heart to both love the person and fully accept the justice of Allah's judgment?
?
Think of a trial you are currently facing. Are you secretly complaining against Allah's decree, wishing it were different? What step can you take today to embrace patience and say, 'I am pleased with Your decree'?
?
Are there any sins in your life that you have normalized to the point that you no longer see them as truly ugly? How does Al-Faqih's definition of being pleased with decree challenge you to change your perspective and repent?

The Hadith Applied to Calamities and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Al-Faqih applies the hadith to calamities and refutes Mu'tazila conditional acceptance of divine decrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Faqih applies the hadith to physical calamities and refutes the Mu'tazila position that God only decrees suffering with compensation.

Core Idea
The hadith demands acceptance of all decrees, including calamities, without conditions.

In the second part of the passage, al-Faqih redirects the discussion from disbelief to physical calamities. He argues that the hadith's real meaning concerns illnesses and disasters, and then launches a refutation of the Mu'tazila, who claim that God only decrees suffering with compensation. He points out their inconsistency: they accept eternal punishment as God's decree but reject calamities without compensation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Start from 'Indeed, the reality of the report concerns illnesses and calamities' and read to the end.
2
Understand the shift to calamities
Note that al-Faqih now says the hadith is about illnesses and calamities, not just disbelief. This broadens the application.
3
Analyze the refutation of Mu'tazila
Al-Faqih points out that the Mu'tazila (represented by al-Ka'bi) accept God's decree of eternal punishment but only accept calamities if compensated. This inconsistency is mocked.
The sarcastic remark 'so let him seek a lord other than me' mirrors the hadith.

Are you willing to repent and cultivate true rida?

Clarifying the Decree of Disbelief
Maturidi clarifies ambiguity in the verse by distinguishing the aspects of decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's method for resolving ambiguity in the verse 'We created everything by measure' regarding divine decree of disbelief.

Core Idea
Not all aspects of decree are equal; context determines appropriateness.

Maturidi addresses a common theological challenge: if God creates everything by measure, does that make disbelief His good creation? He shows that the term 'measure' can refer to different aspects, and the proper response is to first clarify the questioner's intent. This passage teaches a principle of precision in theological discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp Maturidi's argument.
Notice the Arabic word 'qadar' appears in two different contexts.
2
Identify the two aspects of 'qadar'
Maturidi distinguishes between what is 'appropriate' (yanbaghi) and what is not. Find where he shifts from the general 'everything by measure' to the specific case of disbelief.
3
Understand why disbelief is 'not appropriate'
Maturidi says disbelief itself is not from what is appropriate—evil acts are not good. But the measure of disbelief from God is appropriate because it is a just and wise determination.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Learn the recommended response
When asked whether God decreed disbelief, Maturidi says you must first ask about the intended meaning of 'decree' or 'measure'. This prevents misunderstandings.
This is the method of 'istikhbar' (inquiry).
5
Reflect on the principle
Think about how this approach prevents oversimplified yes/no answers and preserves divine transcendence while acknowledging human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في قوله : إنا كل شيء خلقناه بقدر ﴾ [القمر، ٤٩/٥٤]: «والقدر مما ينبغي والكفر مما لا ينبغي؛ وإنما القول بقدر منه، فـ [هذا] من الوجه الذي ذكرنا، ومن ذلك الوجه مما ينبغي». وبعد، فإنه ينبغي أن يكون قدره قبيحا سمجاً. ثم قال : [إن] سألك : هل قضى الله الكفر وقدر [ه]؟ يجب أن تستخبره عن المراد.
He said concerning His saying: 'Indeed, We have created everything by a measure' [54:49]: 'And the measure is from what is appropriate, and disbelief is from what is not appropriate. Rather, the statement is about a measure of it, so this is from the aspect we mentioned, and from that aspect, it is from what is appropriate.' Then after that, it is appropriate that His measure be ugly and repulsive. Then he said: 'If he asks you: Did Allah decree disbelief and measure it? You must inquire from him about the intended meaning.'
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage addresses the apparent contradiction in the Quranic verse 'We created everything by measure' when applied to disbelief. Maturidi explains that the word 'measure' (qadar) has different aspects. In one aspect, everything is created by God's measure, but disbelief itself as an evil act is not 'appropriate' in the sense of being good. However, the decree of disbelief from God's perspective is appropriate because it is a just determination. The passage advises that if someone asks whether God decreed and measured disbelief, one should first clarify what they mean by 'decree' and 'measure' – whether they refer to God's knowledge or His creative act etc. This shows the importance of precision in theological questions.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'everything by measure' means God creates evil acts as evil without any distinction.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that while everything is measured, the 'measure' in relation to evil acts is considered inappropriate from the aspect of the act itself, but appropriate from the aspect of God's just determination. Thus, the verse does not imply that God approves of evil.

Checkpoint

C) First ask the questioner what they mean by 'decree.'
D) Recite the verse 'We created everything by measure.'

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that when asked about divine decree of evil, one must first clarify the intended meaning of 'decree' to avoid misunderstanding.

The First Reason: God's Decree Follows Human Choice
Maturidi states the first of three reasons against excusing sin by predestination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine predestination does not excuse human sin according to Maturidi's first principle: God's decree is based on His foreknowledge of human choice.

Core Idea
God's decree confirms human choice; it does not compel it.

Maturidi begins his argument by stating that no one can excuse themselves by saying their sin was predestined. The first reason is that God's decree is based on His knowledge that the servant would freely choose that action. Thus, the decree does not create responsibility; it follows the servant's own preference.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the passage announces three reasons but only gives the first in this excerpt.
The passage is incomplete; we only have the first reason.
2
Identify the logical flow
Look at how Maturidi connects God's decree to human choice: 'Because He knew that it would be chosen and preferred.' This is the key link.
3
Reflect on the implication
If God's decree is based on prior knowledge of our choice, then the decree does not cause the sin. Instead, it is a confirmation of what we freely choose. This means we cannot blame fate.
The other two reasons would reinforce this, but they are missing from the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : فيقال: إذ وجب ذا فجميع ما أجبت قبل الاستخبار عنه إغفال ولا قوة إلا بالله. والأصل في القضاء والقدر والتخليق والإرادة أن لا عذر لأحد بذلك لأوجه ثلاثة. أحدها أن الله تعالى [لِمَ] قضى وخلق وما ذُكر ؟ لما علم أن ذلك يُختار ويُؤثر. وبما أراد وخلق وقضى يصلون إليه ويبلغون ما آثروه. فلم يكن لهم الاحتجاج، بما هو آثر الأشياء
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: It is said: Since this is necessary, then all that you answered before asking is negligence, and there is no power except with Allah. And the principle concerning decree, predestination, creation, and will is that no one has an excuse due to that for three reasons. One of them: Allah, exalted, decreed, created, and what was mentioned? Because He knew that it would be chosen and preferred. And by His willing, creating, and decreeing, they attain to it and reach what they preferred. So they cannot argue, because it is the most preferred of things.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents the foundational principle that divine predestination does not excuse human sin. Maturidi gives the first of three reasons: God's decree and creation of an act are based on His foreknowledge that the servant would freely choose and prefer that act. Therefore, God's decree does not compel the act; it merely confirms the choice the servant will make. The servant cannot use God's decree as an excuse because it is contingent on his own preference. The passage is cut off before listing the other two reasons, but it establishes that human responsibility remains intact despite divine predestination.

Correction: Maturidi argues that God's decree is based on His foreknowledge of human choice, so the decree follows the choice, not the other way around. Humans are still accountable for their own choices.

Key Takeaway

Definition of Satisfaction with Allah's Decree
Maturidi defines rida bi qada and refutes Mu'tazila on qada and compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of satisfaction with Allah's decree as taught by Imam Maturidi, and how it refutes the Mu'tazila's position on compensation.

Core Idea
Satisfaction with Allah's decree means recognizing evil as evil, not approving it.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid explains the true meaning of rida bi qada. It clarifies that being satisfied with Allah's decree is not about condoning evil, but about knowing that evil comes from the servant's act and that Allah's decree is just. The passage also addresses the Mu'tazila's erroneous view that compensation is needed for calamities.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of rida
The jurist defines rida as knowing that disbelief is evil, perishable, and merits punishment unless repented. Note that the evil is ascribed to the servant's act, not to Allah's decree.
2
Examine the refutation of Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila claim that Allah only decrees illnesses with compensation. The passage argues that this implies dissatisfaction with Allah's decree, contradicting the hadith.
Look for the phrase 'فليتخذ ربا سوائي'.
3
Synthesize the lesson
Understand that true satisfaction with qada includes accepting even calamities without demanding compensation, and recognizing that evil belongs to the servant's choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : هذا مثل الأول، وإن الرضا بقضائه أن تعلم بأن الكفر مضمحل يكون هو قبيح، وأنه شر وفساد، وأنه يوجب مقت صاحبه وتعذيبه إلا أن يتوب؛ فمن لم يرض بهذا فهو كافر، فيكون على ما جاء به الخبر على أن الكفر والقبح هو فعل العبد، ومحال أن قضاءه، فثبت أن قضاء الله هو ما ذكرتُ مما عليه حقيقة الفعل. ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أن حقيقية الخبر في الأمراض والمصائب . ألا يرى أن التخليد في النار من قضائه عند المعتزلة، وكذلك الخذلان والإضلال ونحو ذلك. فليرض الكعبي لنفسه ذلك، وإلا طلب ربا سواه. والمعتزلة يقولون : ليس الله القضاء بالأمراض والمصائب في الذين لا ذنب لهم إلا بالعوض، فإذا هم لا يرضون بها حتى يُعطوا عليها العوض، وذلك معنى ما روي «فليتّخذ ربا سوائي ». وقال : علينا الرضا بقضاء الله . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وقد بينا كيف يرضى به وما عليه في ذلك أيضاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The jurist (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This is similar to the first. And verily, satisfaction with His decree is that you know that disbelief is perishable and evil, and that it is evil and corruption, and that it necessitates the displeasure of its doer and his punishment unless he repents. So whoever is not satisfied with this is a disbeliever, thus it is according to what the report came: that disbelief and evil are the act of the servant, and it is impossible that [؟] His decree. So it is established that the decree of Allah is what I mentioned of the reality of the act. There is no power except with Allah. Also that the reality of the report concerning illnesses and calamities. Do you not see that eternal abiding in the Fire is from His decree according to the Mu'tazila, as well as abandonment and misguidance and the like. So let al-Ka'bi be satisfied with that for himself, otherwise let him seek a Lord other than Him. And the Mu'tazila say: Allah does not decree illnesses and calamities upon those who have no sin except with compensation, so they are not satisfied with them until they are given compensation for them. And that is the meaning of what is narrated: 'Let him take a Lord other than me.' And he said: Upon us is satisfaction with the decree of Allah. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We have explained how one is satisfied with it and what [the ruling] is concerning that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a combined statement and commentary on the meaning of satisfaction (rida) with Allah's decree (qada). The 'jurist' (al-faqih) defines rida bi qada: it is to know that disbelief (kufr) is inherently evil and deserving of punishment unless repented. He then links this to the report (al-khabar) which attributes disbelief to the servant's act, not Allah's decree in a way that makes Allah the doer of evil. The phrase 'wa muhal an qada'ahu' is unclear but likely means 'it is impossible that it is His decree' (i.e., that He decrees evil as an act of injustice). The decree of Allah is rather what the author mentioned concerning the reality of the act. Then the passage addresses the Mu'tazila, who claim that Allah's decree of illnesses and calamities on the innocent is only with compensation, implying that they are not satisfied with such decree unless compensated. This is contrasted with the prophetic narration 'Let him take a Lord other than me,' condemning those who reject Allah's decree. Finally, the Shaykh (Imam Maturidi) briefly notes that he has already explained how one is to be satisfied with the decree and its ruling.

Correction: Satisfaction means recognizing evil as evil and accepting that it is from the servant's act, while affirming that Allah's decree is just.

Maturidi on Qadr: Comprehensive Decree and Moral Distinction
Maturidi explains the verse 'We created everything with qadr' and distinguishes between qadr's scope and moral value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's nuanced interpretation of the verse 'We created everything with qadr' and how qadr relates to moral categories.

Core Idea
Qadr is comprehensive, but not everything decreed is morally good.

This lesson examines a dense passage from Imam al-Maturidi's tafsir on the Quranic verse about qadr (divine decree). Maturidi clarifies that while qadr encompasses all things, it does not imply that everything decreed is morally good. Disbelief, for example, is not seemly, yet it falls within the scope of qadr. Let's unpack this subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Notice the structure: the verse reference, then the explanation, then a concluding statement.
2
Identify the two aspects of qadr
Maturidi distinguishes between 'qadr from what is seemly' and the fact that disbelief is not seemly. He then says the verse speaks of qadr 'from it' (minhu) – that is, from the aspect of creation/decree. Understand the dual perspective.
The phrase 'minhu' is crucial: qadr is being discussed from one particular angle.
3
Interpret the concluding sentence about ugliness
Maturidi ends by saying 'it is necessary that His qadr be ugly and foul.' Reflect on why he says this despite earlier calling qadr seemly. This is a paradox that points to the distinction between the decree itself and the thing decreed.
Consider: the ugliness refers to the content of some decrees (e.g., disbelief), not the act of decreeing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في قوله : إنا كل شيء خلقناه بقدر ﴾ [القمر، ٤٩/٥٤]: «والقدر مما ينبغي والكفر مما لا ينبغي ؛ وإنما القول بقدر منه ، فهذا من الوجه الذي ذكرنا ، ومن ذلك الوجه مما ينبغي». وبعد، فإنه ينبغي أن يكون قدره قبيحا سمجاً .
He said concerning His saying: 'Indeed, We have created everything with qadr' (54:49): 'And qadr is from what is seemly, and disbelief is from what is not seemly; but the saying is only [about] qadr from it. So this is from the aspect we mentioned, and from that aspect it is from what is seemly.' After that, it is necessary that His qadr be ugly and foul.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a brief exegesis of the Qur'anic verse 'Inna kulla shay'in khalaqnahu bi-qadr' (We created everything with measure/decree). Maturidi explains that the word 'qadr' here refers to what is fitting or seemly (ma yanbaghi). Disbelief (kufr) is not seemly; however, the verse speaks of qadr in one aspect—the aspect of divine creation and decree from which everything proceeds. From that aspect, even what is not seemly in itself (like disbelief) is still included in the comprehensive qadr. Yet, after clarifying this, Maturidi states that from another perspective, it is necessary that His qadr (i.e., what He decrees) be considered ugly and foul? This appears paradoxical but likely means that the decree of disbelief, though part of the all-encompassing qadr, is not itself good—it is decreed but blameworthy. The precise point is that qadr applies to everything, but the moral quality of the decreed thing is separate.

Key Terms
القدر
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse implies that everything decreed is good because it is all from qadr.
Correction: Maturidi shows that qadr is comprehensive but moral judgments depend on the thing itself. Disbelief is decreed but not seemly; the decree itself (as an act of God) is good, but the decreed thing can be evil.

Checkpoint
In Maturidi's interpretation of the verse 'Inna kulla shay'in khalaqnahu bi-qadr', what does 'qadr' refer to?
A) Only good things
B) Everything, but from the aspect of what is seemly
C) Only decrees that are pleasing to God
D) The measure of divine mercy

Key Takeaway
Maturidi reveals that qadr covers all created things, but the moral value of what is decreed is not determined by the decree itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything you do is decreed by God, yet your disbelief or sin is morally ugly from God's perspective, how does that change the way you view your own wrongdoing? Will you continue to excuse yourself by saying 'it was my qadr'?
?
When you commit a sin, do you feel the weight of it as something that God decreed but also as something that is inherently foul? How can you hold both truths together without falling into despair or arrogance?
?
Maturidi says that qadr as divine act is always seemly, but the content of some decrees is ugly. If God decrees something ugly in your life (like a trial), are you able to see the decree as good while lamenting the hardship? How can you trust God's decree without numbing your heart to pain?

Three Reasons Why Qada Offers No Excuse
Maturidi enumerates three arguments that qada and qadr provide no excuse for sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational arguments Maturidi presents to refute the misconception that divine decree and predestination can serve as an excuse for sin.

Core Idea
Divine decree does not excuse sin because it does not compel; humans act by choice.

Maturidi anticipates a common objection: if Allah decrees everything, can sinners claim they had no choice? He responds by establishing a foundational principle and then offering three distinct reasons that dismantle this excuse. These reasons focus on human choice, the absence of compulsion, and the psychological reality of human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi begins by stressing the need to clarify the questioner's intent.
2
Identify the three reasons
The passage presents three explicit reasons why qada offers no excuse. Find each one and note how it refutes the idea of compulsion.
The reasons are marked by 'أحدها', 'والثاني', 'والثالث'.
3
Examine the logical implications
After the third reason, Maturidi launches a reductio ad absurdum: if qada were an excuse, many absurd consequences would follow. Reflect on why he does this.
This strengthens the argument by showing the conclusion is necessary.
4
Connect to personal responsibility
Consider how the three reasons together affirm that humans are morally responsible agents. How does each reason individually contribute to this?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : [إن] سألك : هل قضى الله الكفر وقدر [ه ]؟ يجب أن تستخبره عن المراد. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : فيقال: إذ وجب ذا فجميع ما أجبت قبل الاستخبار عنه إغفال ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل في القضاء والقدر والتخليق والإرادة أن لا عذر لأحد بذلك لأوجه ثلاثة. أحدها أن الله تعالى [لِمَ] قضى وخلق وما ذُكر ؟ لما علم أن ذلك يُختار ويُؤثر. وبما أراد وخلق وقضى يصلون إليه ويبلغون ما آثروه. فلم يكن لهم الاحتجاج، بما هو آثر الأشياء عندهم وأخيرها ؛ على ما لم يكن لهم ذلك بالعلم والكتاب والإخبار ؛ إذ كانت بالتي تكون منهم مختارين مؤثرين. وبالله نستعين . والثاني أن جميع ما كان لم يحملهم على ما هم فعلوه ولم يدفعهم إليه ولا اضطرهم، بل هم على ما هم عليه لو لم يكن شيء من ذلك، ويتوهم كونه بلا ما ذكرت ؛ وقد مكنوا من مضادات ما عملوا، فما ذلك - إذ لم يضطرهم ولم يحوّل عنهم حقيقة بما علم كل منهم أنه مختار مؤثر فاعل ممكن من الترك - إلا كخلق سائر الجواهر والأعراض والأوقات والأمكنة التي فيها تقع الأفعال. وإن لم يحتمل كون شيء من ذلك عذرا لهم أو حجة لم يكن ما نحن فيه حجة أو عذرًا . والله الموفق. والثالث أنه لم يخطر شيء من ذلك ببالهم ، ولا كان عند أنفسهم وقت الفعل أنهم يفعلون لشيء من ذلك، فالاحتجاج بما ليس لذلك الفعل عند المحتج باطل؛ وكذلك العذر بما لم يكن عند نفسه بالذي يفعل مكان لذلك باطل مضمحل . ولو كان لهم بذلك احتجاج لكان لهم بالإخبار وبالعلم والتقوية ونحو ذلك احتجاج على أنه لو كان هذا اعتذاراً لكان لهم بما جهلوا الأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد، وبما جهلوا موقع مأثمهم بالمحل الذي وقعت، ولكان لهم بما لا يضر الله ولا يوهن سلطانه ولا ينقص ملكه عذر. ولو كان لهم بذلك عذر لكان بما خلقهم على العلم بما يكون منهم عذر . ولو كان لهم في ذلك احتجاج لكان بما هو أوضح لهم من ذلك كله، وهو الذي يكون مثله وقت الفعل متصورا في الوهم من نحو
Then he said: If he asks you: Did Allah decree and measure disbelief? You must inquire about the intended meaning. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is said: Since this is necessary, then all that you answered before inquiring is neglectful, and there is no power except with Allah. The foundation concerning decree and determination and creation and will is that no one has an excuse by that, for three reasons. First: Allah Almighty decreed and created and what was mentioned? Because He knew that it would be chosen and preferred. And by what He willed, created, and decreed, they arrive at it and attain what they have preferred. So they have no argument, since it is the most preferred and best of things to them; although they do not have that by knowledge, scripture, or report, because it is by that which occurs from them as choosers and preferrers. And with Allah we seek help. Second: All that was did not compel them to do what they did, nor push them to it, nor compel them; rather they are as they are even if there were none of that, and one might imagine it being without what I mentioned. And they were enabled to do the opposites of what they did. So that—since it did not compel them nor change from them the reality, based on each of them knowing that he is a chooser, preferrer, doer, capable of refraining—is only like the creation of other substances, accidents, times, and places in which actions occur. And if it is not possible for any of that to be an excuse or argument for them, then what we are discussing is not an argument or excuse. And Allah is the grantor of success. Third: None of that occurred to their minds, nor was it in their own selves at the time of the action that they were doing because of any of that. So arguing by what is not for that action is false in the arguer's case; similarly, excusing by what was not in oneself for that action is false and perishable. And if they had an argument by that, they would have an argument by the report, by knowledge, by strengthening, and the like, because if this were an excuse, they would have an excuse by what they were ignorant of concerning the command, prohibition, promise, and threat, and by what they were ignorant of concerning the location of their sin in the place where it occurred. And they would have an excuse by what does not harm Allah nor weaken His authority nor diminish His kingdom. And if they had an excuse by that, they would have an excuse by His creating them with knowledge of what would be from them. And if they had an argument in that, they would have an argument by what is clearer to them than all of that, which is what is similar to it at the time of action, conceived in estimation from...
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-response followed by a triple proof that divine decree and predestination do not provide a valid excuse for sin. The objection is: if asked whether Allah decreed and measured disbelief, one must first inquire what the questioner means. The response begins by criticizing those who answer such questions without clarifying intent. Then Maturidi states the foundational principle: no one can use Allah's decree, creation, or will as an excuse for their actions. He provides three reasons. First reason: Allah decreed and created because He knew that people would choose and prefer those actions. Since people choose what they prefer most, their own choice negates the excuse. Second reason: Allah's decree and creation do not compel people to act; they remain capable of opposites. The decree is like the creation of the universe—it does not force actions. If other created things are not excuses, then qada is not either. Third reason: At the moment of action, people do not think of qada; their motives are selfish. Claiming qada as an excuse is thus false. Maturidi then elaborates: if qada were an excuse, then ignorance of commands would also be an excuse, leading to absurd conclusions. Also, if qada were an excuse, then Allah's foreknowledge would be an excuse, but it is not. The passage concludes by stating that the strongest evidence is what is evident to them at the time of action—their own voluntary choice.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
لا عذر
الاختيار
الاضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine predestination compels humans to sin, removing moral responsibility.
Correction: Maturidi refutes this by showing that decree does not coerce; humans act by choice, as evidenced by their ability to do otherwise and their lack of awareness of qada during action.

Checkpoint
According to the third reason given by Maturidi, why does qada not serve as an excuse for sin?
A) Because qada is not mentioned in the Quran
B) Because at the time of the action, sinners do not think about qada; they act from their own motives
C) Because qada only applies to good deeds
D) Because sinners are unaware of qada's existence

Key Takeaway
Divine decree does not negate human moral responsibility because it does not compel, and humans act by their own choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever secretly blame Allah's decree as 'destiny'? Read Maturidi's three reasons again. Which one exposes your excuse most? This week, commit to catching that thought and replacing it with the truth that you are the chooser.
?
If you truly believed that no excuse remains after this lesson, how would your repentance tonight be different? Would you confess with deeper remorse and resolve to change? Write one sin you often excuse and a concrete step to avoid it tomorrow.
?
Maturidi says that at the moment of sin, the thought of qada is absent from your mind. Think of your last major sin: were you thinking about destiny at that moment, or about your desire? If the latter, will you now admit that your own desire was the cause—and own the responsibility fully?

The Second Proof: Humans Act by Choice, Not Compulsion
Imam al-Maturidi presents the second proof that qadar is not an excuse because humans act freely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that God's decree does not compel human actions, and how al-Maturidi argues that humans have genuine choice and responsibility despite qadar.

Core Idea
Divine decree provides context, not compulsion — humans choose freely.

Imam al-Maturidi presents his second argument against using qadar as an excuse for sin. He carefully distinguishes between the decree as a contextual framework and as a compulsion. The passage insists that humans retain the ability to choose the opposite of what they do, proving that qadar is not a deterministic force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Focus on the flow of the argument from beginning to end.
Notice the key verbs: 'did not compel', 'did not impel', 'did not force'.
2
Identify the denial of compulsion
The passage opens by denying three types of coercion: carrying (haml), impelling (daf'), and forcing (idtarar). Understand that these are all forms of compulsion that are absent.
This sets up the main point: humans are not forced.
3
Consider the ability to do opposites
The author says humans have been enabled to do the opposites of what they actually did. This implies they could have chosen otherwise, proving free choice.
This is a key evidence that qadar does not negate human agency.
4
Understand the analogy to other created things
The decree is compared to the creation of substances, accidents, times, and places. These are contexts for actions, not causes that compel.
Just as these are not excuses, the decree itself is not an excuse.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
If those contextual factors are not valid excuses, then the decree is not a valid excuse either. The argument upholds human responsibility.
Reflect on how this refutes the fatalist mindset.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن جميع ما كان لم يحملهم على ما هم فعلوه ولم يدفعهم إليه ولا اضطرهم، بل هم على ما هم عليه لو لم يكن شيء من ذلك، ويتوهم كونه بلا ما ذكرت؛ وقد مكنوا من مضادات ما عملوا، فما ذلك - إذ لم يضطرهم ولم يحوّل عنهم حقيقة بما علم كل منهم أنه مختار مؤثر فاعل ممكن من الترك - إلا كخلق سائر الجواهر والأعراض والأوقات والأمكنة التي فيها تقع الأفعال. وإن لم يحتمل كون شيء من ذلك عذرا لهم أو حجة لم يكن ما نحن فيه حجة أو عذرًا. والله الموفق.
And the second (proof) is that all that [was decreed] did not compel them to do what they did, nor impel them nor force them; rather they are as they are even if none of that existed, and it is imagined to be without what you mentioned. And they have been given the ability to do the opposites of what they did. So that [decree] – since it did not force them and did not change their reality as every one of them knows that he is a chooser, preferrer, doer, capable of refraining – is nothing but like the creation of other substances, accidents, times and places in which actions occur. And if it is not possible that any of that be an excuse or argument for them, then what we are in (i.e., the decree) would not be an argument or excuse. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a theological proof that God's decree (qadar) does not compel human actions. Imam al-Maturidi presents the second argument (following an earlier first proof) that humans cannot use qadar as an excuse because they are not forced. He states that the decree did not carry them (حمَلهم), push them (دفعهم), or compel them (اضطرهم). Rather, they remain as they are even if the decree were absent, and they even have the ability to do the opposite of what they did. The decree, since it does not force or change their nature, is like the creation of other contingent things (substances, accidents, times, places) that form the context of actions but do not determine them. If those contextual factors are not valid excuses, then the decree itself is not a valid excuse. The argument emphasizes human freedom and responsibility.

Key Terms
qadar
jabar
ikhtiyar
mukhtar
fā'il mumkin

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this negates divine decree entirely, but it only denies that decree compels actions against human choice.
Correction: The passage does not deny qadar; it denies that qadar coerces humans. It affirms both divine foreknowledge and human free will.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what does the divine decree resemble?
A) A direct cause of human actions
B) The creation of other contingent things like substances and accidents
C) An excuse for sinful behavior
D) A force that compels obedience

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's second proof shows that qadar is not an excuse because humans have the ability to choose otherwise and are not coerced by the decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you find yourself thinking 'it was my qadar' to avoid responsibility? This lesson says you have the ability to do the opposite. Reflect on one specific sin you have made that excuse for. Will you now take ownership?
?
You know that you are a chooser. How would your daily actions change if you truly believed that divine decree does not compel you? What would you stop blaming on fate?
?
Al-Maturidi says you could have refrained. Think of a moment you convinced yourself you had no choice. Right now, commit to one small action today where you exercise your God-given ability to choose differently.

Divine Decree
beginner
5 min
The Third Proof: Qadar Not in Mind at Time of Action
Proof that qadar is not in mind at time of act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qadar cannot be used as an excuse for sin according to Imam al-Maturidi, specifically because it is not consciously considered during the act.

Core Idea
Qadar is not a conscious motive at the time of sin, so it cannot be an excuse.

Imam al-Maturidi presents a powerful psychological argument: when people commit sins, they do not have divine decree on their minds. So how can they later claim that qadar forced them? In this passage, he develops this reasoning and shows that if qadar were a valid excuse, many other things would be too—but they are not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow.
The passage begins with a clear statement and then introduces many 'if' clauses.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence that states qadar does not come to mind during the act. This is the premise for the entire argument.
It is the first sentence: 'none of that came to their minds'.
3
Trace the reductio ad absurdum
The author lists many hypothetical excuses that would be valid if qadar were an excuse. List them: ignorance of command/prohibition, etc.
Notice how each hypothetical builds on the previous one.
4
Understand the conclusion
The final sentence says if none of those are valid excuses, then the first (qadar) is also not valid. This is the logical climax.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this argument is psychologically compelling: it points out that people do not act because of qadar; they act out of desire or ignorance.
The argument does not deny qadar's existence, only its use as an excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أنه لم يخطر شيء من ذلك ببالهم، ولا كان عند أنفسهم وقت الفعل أنهم يفعلون لشيء من ذلك، فالاحتجاج بما ليس لذلك الفعل عند المحتج باطل؛ وكذلك العذر بما لم يكن عند نفسه بالذي يفعل مكان لذلك باطل مضمحل. ولو كان لهم بذلك احتجاج لكان لهم بالإخبار وبالعلم والتقوية ونحو ذلك احتجاج على أنه لو كان هذا اعتذاراً لكان لهم بما جهلوا الأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد، وبما جهلوا موقع مأثمهم بالمحل الذي وقعت، ولكان لهم بما لا يضر الله ولا يوهن سلطانه ولا ينقص ملكه عذر. ولو كان لهم بذلك عذر لكان بما خلقهم على العلم بما يكون منهم عذر. ولو كان لهم في ذلك احتجاج لكان بما هو أوضح لهم من ذلك كله، وهو الذي يكون مثله وقت الفعل متصورا في الوهم من نحو الكرم والجود والغنى عن تعذيبهم، وبما هو عفو غفور، وبما ليس له في طاعتهم نفع ولا عليه في معصيتهم ضرر، فإذا لم يكن الاحتجاج بشيء من ذلك لم يكن في الأول.
And the third (proof) is that none of that came to their minds, nor was it within themselves at the time of the act that they were doing [it] because of any of that. So arguing by what is not for that act in the view of the arguer is invalid; likewise, the excuse by what was not within himself as the one who acts in place of that is invalid and perishable. And if they had an argument by that, they would have an argument by (divine) information, knowledge, enabling, and the like, upon the basis that if this were an excuse, they would have an excuse by their ignorance of the command, prohibition, promise, and threat, and by their ignorance of the location of their sin in the place where it occurred, and they would have an excuse by what does not harm God nor weaken His authority nor diminish His kingdom. And if they had an excuse by that, they would have an excuse by His creating them with knowledge of what would be from them. And if they had an argument in that, they would have an argument by what is clearer to them than all that, namely what is like it at the time of the act imagined in the mind, such as generosity, bounty, independence from punishing them, and by His being forgiving and merciful, and by the fact that He has no benefit in their obedience nor harm in their disobedience. So when argument by none of that is [valid], it is not [valid] in the first case (i.e., using qadar).
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that qadar is not a valid excuse for sin. Imam al-Maturidi states that at the moment of committing the act, the sinner does not have qadar in mind; they act without thinking of divine decree. Therefore, using qadar as an excuse after the fact is invalid. He then extends the argument by reductio ad absurdum: if qadar were a valid excuse, then many other things would also be valid excuses—such as ignorance of divine commands, the fact that God does not need our obedience, or God's attributes of forgiveness. But none of these are accepted as excuses. Hence, qadar cannot be an excuse either.

Key Terms
qadar
ihtijāj
ʿudhr

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that humans are ever aware of God's decree.
Correction: The argument only states that at the moment of action, the sinner does not consciously consider qadar as their motive; it does not deny general awareness of qadar.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof claim about the sinner's state of mind during the act?
A) They are fully aware of qadar and choose to sin
B) They do not think of qadar at the time of the act
C) They are forced by qadar against their will
D) They use qadar as a conscious excuse

Key Takeaway
Qadar cannot be an excuse for sin because it is not a conscious motive at the time of action; using it as an excuse is an invalid afterthought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you excused a sin by telling yourself 'it was my fate' or 'Allah decreed it.' Was that thought actually present in your mind as you sinned, or did it come only afterward? Will you now reject that excuse and instead take full responsibility for your choice?
?
If qadar is not a valid excuse, then you must face your sins without blaming destiny. What sin have you been avoiding repentance for because you felt it was 'written'? Will you repent from it today, acknowledging that you chose it freely?
?
This passage shows that even God's forgiveness and independence are not excuses for sin. How does that challenge the comfort you might take in 'Allah is forgiving' while continuing in sin? What specific step will you take this week to break free from that false comfort?

Imam al-Maturidi's Defense of Human Free Will Against Objections
Imam al-Maturidi answers an objection about divine and human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi responds to the objection that his arguments remove human actions from God's power, affirming both divine creation and human choice.

Core Idea
Human free will is as certain as sensory perception, yet our actions reveal a divine dimension beyond our control.

Imam al-Maturidi anticipates a crucial objection: if God creates all actions, how can humans be responsible? He answers by first establishing that every person knows intuitively that they are free agents. Then he points out that our actions often contradict our own intentions and estimations, showing a divine dimension. This passage is central to understanding the Maturidi doctrine of kasb (acquisition).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the flow. Notice the questioner's concern and the immediate response.
The objection is a natural one for anyone grappling with divine omnipotence.
2
Understand the foundational principle (al-aṣl)
Imam Maturidi states that every human knows they are a free agent (fā'il mumkin) and chooser. This is presented as self-evident knowledge, like direct perception.
This is the anchor that prevents determinism.
3
Examine the three aspects that show actions are not entirely ours
The passage lists ways an action goes against the doer's own rational estimates, knowledge, and intentions. This reveals a power beyond the human.
These aspects almost become sensory (ḥissī) and intuitive (ʿiyānī).
4
Conclude with the reconciliation
The conclusion: actions are from God in some respects and from humans in others. Denying either is to contradict reason and sense.
This is the essence of the Maturidi position on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: كيف لا دل ذا على أن ليس من الله ما تذكرون؟ قلنا: لما مضت الأدلة في تحقيق جميع ما بينا من الله عز وجل. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والأصل في هذا أن كلا يعلم أنه فاعل ممكن مما يفعله، مؤثر له غيره، مما لو منع عنه لعظم ذلك عليه واشتد، وأنه اختار على ضده؛ فلا سبيل إلى دفع حقيقة ذلك، إذ يعلم كل ذلك نفسه، ولما صار ذلك لأهله كالعيان والحس الذي لا يتخيل إليه على الغلط من فيه. ثم يجد كل واحد فعله خارجا على غير الذي يقدّره عقله من الحسن والقبح، وعلى غير الذي يبلغه علمه من التقدير بالمكان والزمان، وعلى ما لا تقصده نفسه من التعب والألم ولا يستعمل قدرته في مثله، على ما ليس عنده في قدرته نقصان فثبت أن أفعالهم من هذه الوجوه التي كادت تصير حسية عيانية ليست لهم، فمن رام تحقيقها عنهم من هذه الوجوه أو نفيها عنهم من الوجوه المتقدمة فهو يكابر عقله ويعاند حسه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
If it is said: How does this not indicate that what you mention is not from God? We say: Because the proofs have already passed in verifying all that we have explained from God, Mighty and Majestic. And there is no power except with God. The principle in this is that everyone knows that he is a free agent (fā'il mumkin) of what he does, a chooser of it over other things, such that if he were prevented from it, that would be great and severe upon him, and that he chose it over its opposite. So there is no way to repel this reality, since every one knows that about himself, and since that has become for its people like direct perception (ʿiyān) and sense (ḥiss) which one cannot imagine being wrong about. Then each one finds his action occurring contrary to what his intellect estimates as good or evil, and contrary to what his knowledge attains of estimation by place and time, and contrary to what his soul intends of fatigue and pain, and he does not use his power in its like, while there is no deficiency in his power. So it is established that their actions, from these aspects that almost become sensory and intuitive, are not theirs. So whoever seeks to affirm them from these aspects or deny them from the previous aspects is disputing his own intellect and opposing his own sense. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. An imaginary objector asks: 'How does all this prove that actions are not from God?' The answer: The previous proofs already established that actions are from God in a way that does not undermine human responsibility. The key principle (al-aṣl) is that every human knows intuitively that they are a free agent (fā'il mumkin) who chooses actions; if prevented, it would be severe. This knowledge is like direct perception. However, upon reflection, a person finds that their actions often go against their own rational judgments of good and evil, against their own estimated timing/place, and against their own intentions of ease—yet their power is not deficient. This shows that actions, from these aspects, are not entirely their own. To deny this is to contradict reason and sense. Thus, the reality is that actions are from God (as Creator) and from humans (as acquirers).

Key Terms
fā'il mumkin
ikhtiyār
ʿiyān
ḥiss

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this implies complete determinism, negating human responsibility.
Correction: The passage affirms human free will as self-evident (al-aṣl) while acknowledging divine involvement in aspects beyond human control, consistent with the Maturidi doctrine of kasb.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the fundamental principle (al-aṣl) regarding human action?
A) Humans are compelled by God's decree
B) Every person knows they are a free agent
C) Actions are entirely from humans
D) Actions are illusions

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi view balances divine creation and human choice by grounding human freedom in self-evident knowledge and grounding divine involvement in aspects of actions that exceed human control.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a conscious choice, do you ever feel that the exact outcome—where, when, and how it unfolds—is not entirely up to you? How does that awareness affect your humility before God?
?
Think of a moment when you intended to avoid difficulty but it still came to you. Did you recognize that as a sign of Allah's control beyond your planning? What would it mean to live in constant consciousness of that?
?
The passage says denying this dual reality (human choice and divine involvement) is 'disputing your own intellect.' Are there areas in your life where you act as if your choices alone determine everything, ignoring Allah's hand? Will you repent from that attitude today?

Refuting the Objection on Divine Attributes
Maturidi answers an objection against divine agency using prior proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that divine attributes like generosity and forgiveness negate divine agency in human actions.

Core Idea
Divine attributes cannot invalidate earlier proofs of divine agency.

Maturidi begins by stating that attributes like generosity, bounty, and forgiveness do not serve as evidence in the argument about human actions. He then presents an objection and answers it by referring to prior proofs that have already established divine agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objector questions why the mentioned attributes don't indicate that actions are not from Allah.
2
Identify Maturidi's answer
Notice that he replies by referencing earlier proofs that already establish the actions are from Allah.
The phrase 'ليما مضت الأدلة' is key.
3
Reflect on the role of prior proofs
Consider how this answer shows that theological arguments build on established foundations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكرم والجود والغنى عن تعذيبهم، وبما هو عفو غفور، وبما ليس له في طاعتهم نفع ولا عليه في معصيتهم ضرر، فإذا لم يكن الاحتجاج بشيء من ذلك لم يكن في الأول. فإن قيل : كيف لا دل ذا على أن ليس من الله ما تذكرون؟ قلنا : ليما مضت الأدلة في تحقيق جميع ما بينا من الله عز وجل. ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل في هذا أن كلا يعلم أنه فاعل ممكن مما يفعله، مؤثر له غيره، مما لو منع عنه لعظم ذلك [عليه] واشتد، وأنه اختار على ضده؛ فلا سبيل إلى دفع حقيقة ذلك، إذ يعلم كل ذلك نفسه، ولما صار ذلك لأهله كالعيان والحس الذي لا يتخيل إليه على الغلط من فيه . ثم يجد كل واحد فعله خارجا على غير الذي يقدّره عقله من الحسن والقبح، وعلى غير الذي يبلغه علمه من التقدير بالمكان والزمان، وعلى ما لا تقصده نفسه من التعب والألم ولا تستعمل قدرته في مثله، على ما ليس عنده في قدرته نقصان فثبت أن أفعالهم من هذه الوجوه التي كادت تصير حسية عيانية ليست لهم، فمن رام تحقيقها عنهم من هذه الوجوه أو نفيها عنهم من الوجوه المتقدمة فهو يكابر عقله ويعاند حسه . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Generosity, bounty, self-sufficiency from punishing them, and that He is Pardoning, Forgiving, and that He has no benefit in their obedience nor harm in their disobedience, so if there is no argument by any of that, then it is not in the first. If it is said: How does this not indicate that what you mention is not from Allah? We say: Because the proofs have already passed in establishing all that we have clarified from Allah Almighty. And there is no power except with Allah. The principle in this is that everyone knows that he is a possible agent of what he does, choosing it over other than it, so that if he were prevented from it, that would be momentous for him and severe, and that he chose it over its opposite; so there is no way to repudiate the reality of that, since he knows all that in himself, and because that has become for its people like direct perception and sense which cannot be imagined to be subject to error by anyone. Then each person finds his action occurring contrary to what his intellect appraises of good and evil, and contrary to what his knowledge reaches of estimation in place and time, and in a way that his soul does not intend of toil and pain, and he does not use his power in its like, in a way that there is no deficiency in his power. So it is established that their actions from these aspects which almost become sensible and perceptual are not theirs. Whoever attempts to attribute them to them from these aspects or to deny them from them from the preceding aspects is defying his intellect and opposing his sense. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage begins with a statement: the divine attributes of generosity, bounty, self-sufficiency from punishment, forgiveness, and the fact that He gains nothing from obedience and loses nothing from disobedience cannot be used as an argument to deny punishment or affirm reward. The text then presents an objection from a hypothetical questioner: 'How does this not indicate that what you mention (i.e., that actions are from Allah) is not from Allah?' The answer is that the proofs have already established that those actions are indeed from Allah. The author then lays out a foundational principle: every person knows intuitively that he is a possible agent of his actions, choosing one alternative over another. This self-knowledge is as clear as sensory perception and cannot be mistaken. Then he observes that each person finds his actions turning out differently from what his own intellect judges as good or evil, and differently from his estimation of place and time, and often involves unintended toil and pain—despite his power not being deficient. Therefore, from these sensory-like aspects, the actions are not truly his. Anyone who tries to attribute them to the human agent from these aspects, or to deny them from the preceding aspects (i.e., the aspect of divine attribution), is contradicting his own intellect and sense.

Key Terms
الاحتجاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine forgiveness implies that Allah does not create actions that deserve punishment.
Correction: Maturidi argues that prior proofs establish divine agency, and these attributes do not contradict that.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is Maturidi's response when someone asks why the divine attributes do not prove that actions are not from Allah?
A) Because the attributes are metaphorical
B) Because prior proofs have already established the actions are from Allah
C) Because humans are free to do as they wish
D) Because the questioner is ignorant

Key Takeaway
Maturidi asserts that prior proofs confirm divine agency, so the objection based on divine attributes fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself using Allah's mercy as a reason to avoid accepting that He is in full control of your deeds? How does Maturidi's response confront that tendency in you?
?
This passage shows that emotional appeals to divine attributes cannot overrule rational proofs. In your own faith, do you sometimes prefer comforting thoughts about Allah's forgiveness over the harder truths of His absolute sovereignty? What would it cost you to submit fully to the proofs?
?
When you think of an action you regret, do you instinctively rely on Allah's pardon to escape the gravity of His decree? Maturidi says the established proofs stand. Will you let the truth of His agency shape your repentance today, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Proving Human Limitation in Action
Maturidi argues that human actions are not truly theirs based on self-knowledge and discrepancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human actions are not truly their own, based on self-awareness and the discrepancy between intention and outcome.

Core Idea
Our actions often defy our own judgments and intentions, proving they are not our creations.

After refuting the objection, Maturidi lays out a foundational principle: every person intuitively knows they are a possible agent. But then he shows how actions frequently contradict their own assessments of good and bad, place and time, and involve unintended toil. From these sensory-like discrepancies, he concludes the actions are not truly theirs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Read the principle that every person knows they are a possible agent, aware of their choice and preference.
This self-knowledge is compared to direct perception.
2
List the three discrepancies
Identify the three ways actions defy intention: they occur contrary to one's judgment of good/bad, contrary to one's estimation of place/time, and involve unintended toil and pain despite no lack of power.
These aspects almost become sensory evidence.
3
Draw the conclusion
Conclude that actions from these aspects are not the human's; whoever attributes them to the human in these respects contradicts intellect and sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكرم والجود والغنى عن تعذيبهم، وبما هو عفو غفور، وبما ليس له في طاعتهم نفع ولا عليه في معصيتهم ضرر، فإذا لم يكن الاحتجاج بشيء من ذلك لم يكن في الأول. فإن قيل : كيف لا دل ذا على أن ليس من الله ما تذكرون؟ قلنا : ليما مضت الأدلة في تحقيق جميع ما بينا من الله عز وجل. ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل في هذا أن كلا يعلم أنه فاعل ممكن مما يفعله، مؤثر له غيره، مما لو منع عنه لعظم ذلك [عليه] واشتد، وأنه اختار على ضده؛ فلا سبيل إلى دفع حقيقة ذلك، إذ يعلم كل ذلك نفسه، ولما صار ذلك لأهله كالعيان والحس الذي لا يتخيل إليه على الغلط من فيه . ثم يجد كل واحد فعله خارجا على غير الذي يقدّره عقله من الحسن والقبح، وعلى غير الذي يبلغه علمه من التقدير بالمكان والزمان، وعلى ما لا تقصده نفسه من التعب والألم ولا تستعمل قدرته في مثله، على ما ليس عنده في قدرته نقصان فثبت أن أفعالهم من هذه الوجوه التي كادت تصير حسية عيانية ليست لهم، فمن رام تحقيقها عنهم من هذه الوجوه أو نفيها عنهم من الوجوه المتقدمة فهو يكابر عقله ويعاند حسه . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Generosity, bounty, self-sufficiency from punishing them, and that He is Pardoning, Forgiving, and that He has no benefit in their obedience nor harm in their disobedience, so if there is no argument by any of that, then it is not in the first. If it is said: How does this not indicate that what you mention is not from Allah? We say: Because the proofs have already passed in establishing all that we have clarified from Allah Almighty. And there is no power except with Allah. The principle in this is that everyone knows that he is a possible agent of what he does, choosing it over other than it, so that if he were prevented from it, that would be momentous for him and severe, and that he chose it over its opposite; so there is no way to repudiate the reality of that, since he knows all that in himself, and because that has become for its people like direct perception and sense which cannot be imagined to be subject to error by anyone. Then each person finds his action occurring contrary to what his intellect appraises of good and evil, and contrary to what his knowledge reaches of estimation in place and time, and in a way that his soul does not intend of toil and pain, and he does not use his power in its like, in a way that there is no deficiency in his power. So it is established that their actions from these aspects which almost become sensible and perceptual are not theirs. Whoever attempts to attribute them to them from these aspects or to deny them from them from the preceding aspects is defying his intellect and opposing his sense. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage begins with a statement: the divine attributes of generosity, bounty, self-sufficiency from punishment, forgiveness, and the fact that He gains nothing from obedience and loses nothing from disobedience cannot be used as an argument to deny punishment or affirm reward. The text then presents an objection from a hypothetical questioner: 'How does this not indicate that what you mention (i.e., that actions are from Allah) is not from Allah?' The answer is that the proofs have already established that those actions are indeed from Allah. The author then lays out a foundational principle: every person knows intuitively that he is a possible agent of his actions, choosing one alternative over another. This self-knowledge is as clear as sensory perception and cannot be mistaken. Then he observes that each person finds his actions turning out differently from what his own intellect judges as good or evil, and differently from his estimation of place and time, and often involves unintended toil and pain—despite his power not being deficient. Therefore, from these sensory-like aspects, the actions are not truly his. Anyone who tries to attribute them to the human agent from these aspects, or to deny them from the preceding aspects (i.e., the aspect of divine attribution), is contradicting his own intellect and sense.

Key Terms
فاعل ممكن
الحسن والقبح
قدرة
حس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: My actions are entirely my own because I choose them.
Correction: Maturidi shows that actions often contradict one's own intentions and judgments, so they cannot be fully one's own.

Checkpoint
In the second part of the passage, what does each person find about their own actions?
A) They always match their intention
B) They occur contrary to their intellect's appraisal of good and evil
C) They are completely under their control
D) They never experience pain

Key Takeaway
The multitude of discrepancies between intention and actual outcome proves that our actions are not under our complete control.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a time when you planned an action carefully—intending a good outcome, estimating the right time and place—yet the result was completely different. Were you the true author of that action? How does that moment reshape your understanding of your own power and reliance on Allah?
?
You often attribute successes to your own skill and failures to external circumstances. But this lesson forces you to see that even your intended actions are not fully yours. Can you honestly say you have ever truly created a single act? If not, what does that mean for your pride and your sense of control?
?
The passage says your actions often involve toil and pain you did not intend, despite no deficiency in your power. Look at the last difficult task you performed. Was the hardship really in your plan? If not, who brought it about? How should this awareness change your prayers and your trust in Allah's decree?
