Good and Evil Are from God: The Correct Understanding
Refutation of a misrepresented Muslim creed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of the Muslim statement 'good and evil are from God' and why it does not imply God is the source of evil.

Core Idea
Muslims affirm predestination of good and evil from God, not evil itself.

Some opponents claimed that Muslims attribute evil directly to God by saying 'good and evil are from God.' In this passage, the author refutes this misrepresentation. He clarifies that Muslims affirm that the predestination (qadr) of good and evil is from God, but the predestination of evil is not the evil itself. This distinction safeguards divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Note the strong refutational tone.
2
Identify the false claim and the correction
Find the claim that the author says is a lie. Then find what Muslims actually say according to the faqih.
The key phrase: 'قدر الخير والشر من الله' (predestination of good and evil is from God).
3
Reflect on the argument
Why would attributing evil directly to God be repugnant? The author says the doer of evil is evil; God is not evil because He does not do evil.
This protects God's justice and wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: قول المسلمين الخير والشر من الله، إنما أرادوا به مخالفة قول الزنادقة. وأما فعل العباد لم يخطر ببالهم، بل قال الله: من عمل الشيطان. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: فما ذكر من قول المسلمين فهو كذب، بل يقولون: قدر الخير والشر من الله، وقدر الشر ليس هو الشر. ولو كان القول في شأن الزنادقة لكان إذا قبيحا إضافة الشر إلى الحكيم العليم، بل من فعله الشر فهو شرير، ومن فعله الإفساد فهو مفسد. وقوله: «لم يخطر ببالهم كذب بل لا يخطر خصوص الذي / ذكر. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he said: The saying of the Muslims, 'Good and evil are from God' – they only intended by it to contradict the saying of the zanadiqah. And as for the actions of the servants, it did not occur to them. Rather, God said: 'From the work of Satan.' The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: What he mentioned from the saying of the Muslims is a lie. Rather, they say: 'The predestination of good and evil is from God,' and the predestination of evil is not the evil itself. And if the saying were about the matter of the zanadiqah, then it would be ugly to attribute evil to the Wise, the All-Knowing. But rather, the one who does evil is evil, and the one who does corruption is corrupt. And his statement: 'It did not occur to them' is a lie; rather, what he specifically mentioned did not occur. And there is no power except in God.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a misrepresentation of the Muslim creed. Someone (likely a heretic or opponent) claimed that Muslims say 'good and evil are from God' in the sense that God directly creates and therefore is responsible for evil. The faqih (the jurist) counters: this claim is false. What Muslims actually say is that the predestination (qadr) of good and evil is from God, but predestination of evil is not the evil itself. The faqih argues that if the statement were about refuting the zanadiqah (dualists or Manichaeans who believed in two principles), then attributing evil directly to the All-Wise God would be repugnant. Rather, the doer of evil is evil, not God. He also dismisses the claim that Muslims never thought about the actions of servants; they do, but not in the way alleged. The passage safeguards divine transcendence by distinguishing between predestination and direct causal attribution.

Key Terms
الخير والشر من الله
قدر الخير والشر
قول الزنادقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Muslims say 'good and evil are from God' meaning that God directly creates evil and is responsible for it.
Correction: Muslims mean that the predestination (qadr) of good and evil is from God, but the predestination of evil is not evil itself. Evil acts are committed by the doer, not by God.

Checkpoint
What does the faqih say Muslims actually mean by 'good and evil from God'?
A) God directly creates good and evil
B) Predestination of good and evil is from God
C) Evil is from Satan, not God
D) Good is from God, evil from humans

Key Takeaway
The Muslim creed holds that predestination of good and evil is from God, but evil itself is not attributed to God; it is the doer who is evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a personal calamity or see injustice, do you find yourself silently accusing God of wronging you? How can the distinction between God's predestination and the evil act itself change the posture of your heart from resentment to trust? This week, when a difficult situation arises, consciously say inside yourself: 'The predestination of this trial is from God by His wisdom, but any evil in it is from my own or others' actions—I will not blame my Lord.'
?
Have you ever used the phrase 'everything is from God' to excuse your own sinful choices, as if to say God caused you to sin? After learning that predestination of evil is not evil itself and that the doer is evil, will you take full responsibility for your sins from now on? Begin your next prayer by sincerely repenting of any such misattribution.
?
If you truly believe that the Wise and All-Knowing God does not do evil, how must you reinterpret the painful events of your past that you have held against Him? Write down one painful memory and, in its place, affirm that God's decree was good in its essence, and the evil was from the doers. Ask Him to heal your heart from blaming Him.

Why Evil Deeds Are Not Ascribed to Allah
Maturidi explains the ruling on ascribing evil to Allah and sets the foundational principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons why ascribing disbelief and sins to Allah is disliked, and the general rule for what can be properly ascribed to Him.

Core Idea
Ascription to Allah must convey glorification, not censure.

Maturidi addresses a subtle theological point: although Allah creates all actions, not every action should be directly ascribed to Him. This lesson explains the rationale behind the etiquettes of ascription, distinguishing between what is fitting for divine glorification and what is not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two reasons
Read the Arabic text and its translation, noting the two distinct reasons Maturidi gives for disliking ascription of evil.
The reasons are 'الوجهان' — one regarding ugliness, the other regarding excuse-making.
2
Examine the analogy and the foundational principle
Maturidi uses the example of not calling Allah 'Creator of filth' to illustrate the principle. Understand the four categories of permissible ascription: glorification, gratitude, blessings, and command.
3
Reflect on the summary and the attributes of justice and grace
Maturidi concludes that Allah's actions are always either just or gracious. Consider how this rule applies to everyday speech about divine decree.
This connects to the broader doctrine of qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ وعلى هذا يكره القول في الكفر والمعاصي : إنها بقضاء الله وقدره وإرادته لوجهين. أحدهما ما ذكر من القبح، أو هي لا تذكر إلا على الاستقباح والاستهانة والذي ذلك وصفه لا يضاف إلى الله تعالى على ما أخبرت، وإن كان في التحقيق من قول ووجه آخر أنه يتكلم به على الاعتذار والاحتجاج، ذلك المفهوم منه ، وقد بيّنا أن لا عذر لهم في ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وكذلك عند الناس لا يقال : يا خالق الخبائث والأنجاس ونحو ذلك، وإن كان هو في الحقيقة لكل شيء خالقاً، فمثله الذي ذكرنا . وأصل ذلك أنه يضاف إلى الله تعالى كل ما كانت الإضافة إليه تخرج مخرج التعظيم، أو مخرج الشكر ، أو مخرج ذكر نعمه أو أمره؛ وما خرج على غير ذلك لا يضاف اليه ، وإن كان في الحقيقة خلقه . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وجملة ذلك أن الله يوصف بفعله، وهو خارج على معنى العدل أو الفضل في الحقيقة. وربما يضاف إليه ما ليس في الحقيقة فعله أو صفته ؛ فإن كان يقتضي معنى محمودا يجوز ذلك، لما نيل ذلك بإنعامه وإفضاله، وإن لم يكن لم يُضف، لما ليس ذلك في الحقيقة فعله فيوصف به؛ وهو من حيث فعله حكيم عدل، وذلك الشيء فيما عند الخلق بغير هذا الوصف. والله تعالى يجل ويتعالى عن غير هذين الوصفين، إذ في أفعاله صفة عدل وحكمة أو فضل وإحسان. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
6. And upon this, it is disliked to speak about disbelief and sins: that they are by Allah's decree (qada'), predestination (qadr), and will (irada) for two reasons. One of them is the ugliness mentioned, or they are only mentioned in the context of censure and contempt, and whatever has that description is not ascribed to Allah, the Exalted, as has been informed, although in reality it is from [His] decree; and another aspect is that one speaks of them by way of excuse and argumentation, which is understood from it, and we have shown that there is no excuse for them in that. And there is no power except with Allah. Similarly, among people it is not said: 'O Creator of filth and impurities' and the like, although He is in reality the Creator of everything, so its example is what we have mentioned. And the foundation of that is that everything is ascribed to Allah, the Exalted, when the ascription takes the form of glorification, or the form of gratitude, or the form of mentioning His blessings or His command; and whatever is other than that is not ascribed to Him, even if in reality it is His creation. And there is no power except with Allah. And the summary of that is that Allah is described by His action, and it proceeds upon the meaning of justice or grace in reality. And perhaps what is not in reality His action or attribute is ascribed to Him; if it entails a praiseworthy meaning, that is permissible because that [good] is attained through His beneficence and bounty; but if not, it is not ascribed, because that is not in reality His action so that He be described by it; and He, from the aspect of His action, is Wise and Just, while that thing is, in the estimation of creatures, other than this description. And Allah, the Exalted, is glorious and exalted above other than these two descriptions, since in His actions there is the attribute of justice and wisdom or grace and beneficence. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal explanation of the proper manner of ascribing actions to Allah, particularly regarding evil deeds. The author begins by stating the ruling: it is disliked (makruh) to say that disbelief and sins are by Allah's decree, predestination, and will, giving two reasons. The first reason is the ugliness (qubh) inherent in such acts; they are only mentioned in contexts of censure and contempt, so ascribing them to Allah would be inappropriate. The second reason is that such speech is often used as an excuse or argument, but the author asserts that there is no excuse for sin. He then gives an analogy: people do not address Allah as 'Creator of filth and impurities,' even though He is the Creator of everything. The foundational principle (asl) is then laid out: ascription to Allah is only permissible when the ascription conveys glorification, gratitude, mention of His blessings, or His command; anything else is not ascribed to Him, even if He is its real Creator. The author concludes with a summary (jumla): Allah is described by His action (fi'l), which always falls under justice ('adl) or grace (fadl). Sometimes what is not actually His action is ascribed to Him if it implies a praiseworthy meaning attained through His benevolence; otherwise it is not ascribed. Allah is described only by these two aspects—justice and grace—since His actions contain nothing but these qualities.

Key Terms
قضاء الله وقدره
إرادته
استقباح
استهانة
إضافة
تعظيم
شكر
نعم
أمر
عدل
فضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since Allah creates everything, all acts including evil are directly ascribed to Him.
Correction: Maturidi explains that ascription must be respectful and appropriate, so evil deeds are not ascribed to Allah even though He creates them.

Checkpoint
Why is it disliked to say that sins are by Allah's decree?
A) Because Allah did not create them
B) Because such speech is disrespectful and implies deficiency
C) Because sins are not within Allah's power
D) Because only good deeds are by His decree

Key Takeaway
The ascription of actions to Allah must reflect glorification and gratitude; evil deeds are not ascribed directly, as Allah's actions are always just and gracious.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself slipping into sin, do you ever whisper inside, 'It was Allah's decree,' as a way to let yourself off the hook? How does this lesson expose the dishonesty of that thought? Will you commit to stopping that excuse today?
?
Think of a recent trial or hardship you faced. When you described it to others, did you attribute it to Allah's will with a sense of complaint? How can you rephrase your speech so that it honors His justice and grace, even when you don't understand?
?
If you truly believed that every ascription to Allah must be for glorification or gratitude, what would change about how you talk about your daily life—your successes, your failures, your frustrations? Are you ready to make that change starting now?

The Qadariyya Reinterpretation and Its Logical Inconsistency
Maturidi reports the Qadariyya position and refutes it by showing logical inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya's claim that divine misguidance is merely trial and deprivation, using the principle of consistency in ascription.

Core Idea
If the cause for ascribing good applies, the same logic forces ascribing evil.

The Qadariyya attempted to reinterpret verses that clearly attribute misguidance to Allah as merely trial and deprivation, avoiding direct creation of evil. Imam Maturidi begins his refutation by pointing out a fatal inconsistency: if the cause of ascription of good is command and strengthening, then the same logic would require ascribing evil to Him by the same cause. He then cites Quranic verses to show that misguidance is indeed attributed to Allah in a direct sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qadariyya position
Read the first part of the passage where the Qadariyya interpret divine misguidance as trial and deprivation, and good things as command and strengthening.
Notice how they separate good and evil in ascription.
2
Identify the logical argument
The author argues: if the cause for ascribing good is command and strengthening, then by the same token, the cause for ascribing evil would be trial and deprivation. Yet the Qadariyya don't ascribe evil directly.
This is the core inconsistency.
3
Examine the Quranic citations
Read the three verses (Ibrahim 14:4, As-Saff 61:5, At-Tawbah 9:127) that clearly attribute misguidance and turning of hearts to Allah.
These verses show that misguidance is directly from Allah, not just trial.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that if this logic of ascription were consistent, it would work for both good and evil, but since it doesn't, there must be a different meaning in ascribing misguidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : قالت القدرية فيما أضيف إلى الله الإضلال والإزاغة وصرف القلوب فيما قال: صرف الله قلوبهم ونحو ذلك : إن ذلك كان بالمحنة والتخلية ونحو ذلك، وفي الخيرات بالأمر والتقوية ونحو ذلك . ولو كان بالذي قالوا يضاف إليه الجاز أن يضاف إليه الإخراج من النور إلى الظلمات كما أضيف إليه الإخراج من الظلمات إلى النور عندهم بالأمر والتقوية، إذ صارت علة الإضافة في الخير إليه الأمر والتقوية؛ وكذلك ذكر في [؟] الحقية. قال الله تعالى: ﴿وما أرسلنا من رسول إلا بلسان قومه ليبين لهم فيضل الله من يشاء ويهدي من يشاء وهو العزيز الحكيم﴾ [إبراهيم، ٤/١٤] . قال الله تعالى : ﴿فلما زاغوا أزاغ الله قلوبهم والله لا يهدي القوم الفاسقين﴾ [الصف، ٥/٦١]. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وإذا ما أنزلت سورة نظر بعضهم إلى بعض هل يراكم من أحد ثم انصرفوا صرف الله قلوبهم بأنهم قوم لا يفقهون ﴾ [التوبة، ١٢٧/٩]. أي جعلهم خاليا عن المعونة الإلهية وتقوية الله. الهداية ، بل كل شيء يقابل ما ذكر؛ إذ الأمر والتقوية كلاهما للمحنة وفيهما التخلية، فإذا استقام ذا، ولم يستقم الآخر بان أن في ذا معنى ليس في الآخر. مع ما زعمت القدرية أن الشرور لا تضاف إليه، لأنه نهى عنها، فقد نهى عن الضلال والغواية والزيغ، فلم أضيفت إليه ؟ والله الموفق. وقالوا في الإضلال بالتسمية، وذلك فاسد؛ لما وجد من غيره ولم يضف إليه، ولما ليس في التسمية فضل حكمة يذكر في موضع الوصف بالغنى والسلطان كقوله تعالى: ﴿ومن يشأ الله يضلله ومن يشأ يجعله على صراط مستقيم﴾ [الأنعام ، ٣٩/٦]، وذلك في موضع القوة والسلطان. وبالله نستعين.
The jurist, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The Qadariyya said regarding what is ascribed to Allah of misguidance, deviation, and turning of hearts, as in His statement: 'Allah turned their hearts' and the like: that that was by trial and deprivation, and the like, and regarding good things, by command and strengthening, and the like. And if it were as they say that [evil] is ascribed to Him, then it would be permissible to ascribe to Him the taking out from light to darkness, just as the taking out from darkness to light is ascribed to Him according to them by command and strengthening, since the cause of ascription in good to Him is command and strengthening; and likewise is mentioned in reality? Allah said: 'And We did not send any messenger except with the language of his people to make clear to them, and Allah leads astray whom He wills and guides whom He wills, and He is the Almighty, the Wise' [Ibrahim 14:4]. Allah said: 'So when they deviated, Allah caused their hearts to deviate, and Allah does not guide the defiantly disobedient people' [As-Saff 61:5]. Allah said: 'And when a surah is revealed, they look at each other, saying, Does anyone see you? Then they turn away. Allah has turned their hearts because they are a people who do not understand' [At-Tawbah 9:127]. Meaning: He made them devoid of divine assistance and strengthening of Allah. Guidance, rather everything corresponds to what was mentioned; since command and strengthening are both for trial and in them is deprivation, so if this is consistent and the other is not, it becomes clear that in this there is a meaning not in the other. Moreover, the Qadariyya claim that evils are not ascribed to Him because He forbade them; yet He forbade misguidance, error, and deviation, so why are they ascribed to Him? And Allah is the grantor of success. And they said regarding misguidance through naming, and that is invalid; because it is found from other than Him and not ascribed to Him, and because in naming there is no praiseworthy wisdom to be mentioned in the context of describing [Him] with self-sufficiency and sovereignty, as His saying: 'And whomsoever Allah wills to misguide, He misguides, and whomsoever He wills to guide, He sets him on a straight path' [Al-An'am 6:39], and that is in the context of power and sovereignty. And with Allah we seek help.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya's reinterpretation of verses that ascribe misguidance and similar actions to Allah. The author (Imam Maturidi) first reports the Qadariyya position: they interpret divine 'misguidance' (idalal), 'deviation' (izaghah), and 'turning of hearts' (sarfa al-qulub) as meaning trial (mihnah) and deprivation (takhliyah) of divine aid, not direct creation. For good things, they say it is by command (amr) and strengthening (taqwiyah). The refutation proceeds by pointing out an inconsistency: if the cause for ascription of good to Allah is command and strengthening, then by the same logic, the cause for ascription of evil would be trial and deprivation. But the Qadariyya do not ascribe evil to Allah despite acknowledging that deprivation is from Him. The author then cites three verses (Ibrahim 14:4, As-Saff 61:5, At-Tawbah 9:127) that use language of Allah leading astray and turning hearts. He explains that the verse 'Allah turned their hearts' means He made them devoid of divine assistance. Continuing his argument, he notes that if guidance and misguidance were both due to trial and deprivation or command and strengthening, then there should be consistency. But since they accept ascription of good but not evil, there is a missing element. Next, he addresses the Qadariyya's claim that evils are not ascribed to Allah because He forbade them; yet Allah also forbade misguidance, error, and deviation, yet these are still ascribed to Him in the Quran. This shows the Qadariyya argument is self-defeating. Finally, he refutes their claim about 'misguidance through naming' (i.e., that ascribing misguidance to Allah merely means naming someone misguided). He argues this is invalid because: (1) other things are found from Allah but not ascribed to Him by that logic, and (2) such naming lacks the praiseworthy wisdom that is appropriate when describing Allah with power and sovereignty, as in verse Al-An'am 6:39, which is a declaration of His absolute power.

Key Terms
القدرية
الإضلال
الإزاغة
المحنة
التخلية
الأمر
التقوية
الإخراج من النور إلى الظلمات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya believe that verses about Allah's misguidance only mean trial and deprivation, not direct creation of misguidance.
Correction: The author shows this leads to a logical inconsistency: if the cause for good is command and strengthening, then the same cause for evil (trial and deprivation) would require ascribing evil, but they do not. Moreover, the Quranic verses directly attribute misguidance to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the Qadariyya interpret verses like 'فلمّا زاغوا أزاغ الله قلوبهم'?
A) Direct creation of misguidance
B) Trial and deprivation of divine aid
C) Metaphor for punishment
D) They deny such verses

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya reinterpretation by exposing a logical inconsistency in their ascription theory and citing Quranic verses that directly attribute misguidance to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something bad happens to you, do you ever find yourself subtly blaming Allah or questioning His wisdom, while you readily accept His blessings without a second thought? What does this lesson's exposure of logical inconsistency reveal about the double standard in your heart? Will you repent from that inconsistency today and fully submit to His decree, both good and evil?
?
Are there any areas of your life where you reinterpret clear Quranic verses to fit your own desires or limited understanding, just as the Qadariyya reinterpreted verses on misguidance? What would it cost you to simply accept Allah's words as they are, without trying to explain them away? Will you commit to that surrender now?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is the Creator of all things, including the evil in your life, would you still complain about your circumstances? How does this lesson challenge you to change your response to trials—from resentment to patience and trust in His wisdom? What one practical step will you take to live out that trust this week?

Refutation of the Prohibition and Naming Arguments
Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya's prohibition and naming arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya's claim that evils are not ascribed to Allah because He forbade them, and their argument that misguidance is merely a name.

Core Idea
If forbidding something prevents ascription, then misguidance, which is forbidden, would also not be ascribed.

The Qadariyya had two additional arguments: first, that evils cannot be ascribed to Allah because He forbade them; second, that ascribing misguidance to Allah is merely a matter of naming (tasmia), not real attribution. Imam Maturidi refutes both. He points out that misguidance is also forbidden in the Quran, yet it is ascribed to Allah. Then he argues that the naming argument fails because other things are found from Allah but not named that way, and because such naming lacks the praiseworthy wisdom appropriate when describing Allah's power and sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the prohibition argument
Read the section starting with 'مع ما زعمت القدرية أن الشرور لا تضاف إليه' and understand the Qadariyya claim that evils are not ascribed because He forbade them.
Notice the Qur'anic verses that command avoiding misguidance.
2
See the refutation
The author points out that misguidance, error, and deviation are also forbidden, yet they are ascribed to Allah in the Quran.
This shows the argument is self-defeating.
3
Read the naming argument
Read 'وقالوا في الإضلال بالتسمية' which presents the Qadariyya view that ascribing misguidance is just naming.
They claim it doesn't imply real creation.
4
Understand the refutation of naming
The author gives two reasons: (1) Other things found from Allah are not named that way, and (2) naming lacks the praiseworthy wisdom that suits Allah's self-sufficiency and sovereignty, as seen in verse Al-An'am 6:39.
This verse is about absolute power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : قالت القدرية فيما أضيف إلى الله الإضلال والإزاغة وصرف القلوب فيما قال: صرف الله قلوبهم ونحو ذلك : إن ذلك كان بالمحنة والتخلية ونحو ذلك، وفي الخيرات بالأمر والتقوية ونحو ذلك . ولو كان بالذي قالوا يضاف إليه الجاز أن يضاف إليه الإخراج من النور إلى الظلمات كما أضيف إليه الإخراج من الظلمات إلى النور عندهم بالأمر والتقوية، إذ صارت علة الإضافة في الخير إليه الأمر والتقوية؛ وكذلك ذكر في [؟] الحقية. قال الله تعالى: ﴿وما أرسلنا من رسول إلا بلسان قومه ليبين لهم فيضل الله من يشاء ويهدي من يشاء وهو العزيز الحكيم﴾ [إبراهيم، ٤/١٤] . قال الله تعالى : ﴿فلما زاغوا أزاغ الله قلوبهم والله لا يهدي القوم الفاسقين﴾ [الصف، ٥/٦١]. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وإذا ما أنزلت سورة نظر بعضهم إلى بعض هل يراكم من أحد ثم انصرفوا صرف الله قلوبهم بأنهم قوم لا يفقهون ﴾ [التوبة، ١٢٧/٩]. أي جعلهم خاليا عن المعونة الإلهية وتقوية الله. الهداية ، بل كل شيء يقابل ما ذكر؛ إذ الأمر والتقوية كلاهما للمحنة وفيهما التخلية، فإذا استقام ذا، ولم يستقم الآخر بان أن في ذا معنى ليس في الآخر. مع ما زعمت القدرية أن الشرور لا تضاف إليه، لأنه نهى عنها، فقد نهى عن الضلال والغواية والزيغ، فلم أضيفت إليه ؟ والله الموفق. وقالوا في الإضلال بالتسمية، وذلك فاسد؛ لما وجد من غيره ولم يضف إليه، ولما ليس في التسمية فضل حكمة يذكر في موضع الوصف بالغنى والسلطان كقوله تعالى: ﴿ومن يشأ الله يضلله ومن يشأ يجعله على صراط مستقيم﴾ [الأنعام ، ٣٩/٦]، وذلك في موضع القوة والسلطان. وبالله نستعين.
The jurist, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The Qadariyya said regarding what is ascribed to Allah of misguidance, deviation, and turning of hearts, as in His statement: 'Allah turned their hearts' and the like: that that was by trial and deprivation, and the like, and regarding good things, by command and strengthening, and the like. And if it were as they say that [evil] is ascribed to Him, then it would be permissible to ascribe to Him the taking out from light to darkness, just as the taking out from darkness to light is ascribed to Him according to them by command and strengthening, since the cause of ascription in good to Him is command and strengthening; and likewise is mentioned in reality? Allah said: 'And We did not send any messenger except with the language of his people to make clear to them, and Allah leads astray whom He wills and guides whom He wills, and He is the Almighty, the Wise' [Ibrahim 14:4]. Allah said: 'So when they deviated, Allah caused their hearts to deviate, and Allah does not guide the defiantly disobedient people' [As-Saff 61:5]. Allah said: 'And when a surah is revealed, they look at each other, saying, Does anyone see you? Then they turn away. Allah has turned their hearts because they are a people who do not understand' [At-Tawbah 9:127]. Meaning: He made them devoid of divine assistance and strengthening of Allah. Guidance, rather everything corresponds to what was mentioned; since command and strengthening are both for trial and in them is deprivation, so if this is consistent and the other is not, it becomes clear that in this there is a meaning not in the other. Moreover, the Qadariyya claim that evils are not ascribed to Him because He forbade them; yet He forbade misguidance, error, and deviation, so why are they ascribed to Him? And Allah is the grantor of success. And they said regarding misguidance through naming, and that is invalid; because it is found from other than Him and not ascribed to Him, and because in naming there is no praiseworthy wisdom to be mentioned in the context of describing [Him] with self-sufficiency and sovereignty, as His saying: 'And whomsoever Allah wills to misguide, He misguides, and whomsoever He wills to guide, He sets him on a straight path' [Al-An'am 6:39], and that is in the context of power and sovereignty. And with Allah we seek help.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya's reinterpretation of verses that ascribe misguidance and similar actions to Allah. The author (Imam Maturidi) first reports the Qadariyya position: they interpret divine 'misguidance' (idalal), 'deviation' (izaghah), and 'turning of hearts' (sarfa al-qulub) as meaning trial (mihnah) and deprivation (takhliyah) of divine aid, not direct creation. For good things, they say it is by command (amr) and strengthening (taqwiyah). The refutation proceeds by pointing out an inconsistency: if the cause for ascription of good to Allah is command and strengthening, then by the same logic, the cause for ascription of evil would be trial and deprivation. But the Qadariyya do not ascribe evil to Allah despite acknowledging that deprivation is from Him. The author then cites three verses (Ibrahim 14:4, As-Saff 61:5, At-Tawbah 9:127) that use language of Allah leading astray and turning hearts. He explains that the verse 'Allah turned their hearts' means He made them devoid of divine assistance. Continuing his argument, he notes that if guidance and misguidance were both due to trial and deprivation or command and strengthening, then there should be consistency. But since they accept ascription of good but not evil, there is a missing element. Next, he addresses the Qadariyya's claim that evils are not ascribed to Allah because He forbade them; yet Allah also forbade misguidance, error, and deviation, yet these are still ascribed to Him in the Quran. This shows the Qadariyya argument is self-defeating. Finally, he refutes their claim about 'misguidance through naming' (i.e., that ascribing misguidance to Allah merely means naming someone misguided). He argues this is invalid because: (1) other things are found from Allah but not ascribed to Him by that logic, and (2) such naming lacks the praiseworthy wisdom that is appropriate when describing Allah with power and sovereignty, as in verse Al-An'am 6:39, which is a declaration of His absolute power.

?

?
The Qadariyya tried to protect Allah's justice by denying His involvement in evil. In your own life, do you ever rephrase difficult circumstances—calling them 'bad luck' or 'coincidence'—to avoid acknowledging Allah's direct hand? Will you now consciously attribute every trial to Him and ask for the patience to bear it?
?
The naming argument dismissed divine ascription as mere label. Do you sometimes minimize God's role in your shortcomings by saying 'I just made a mistake' without recognizing His will behind it? How might your repentance deepen if you first affirmed that nothing happens outside His decree?

Why the Term 'Qadariyya' Is Not a Linguistic Accident
Al-Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that Arab naming habits justify calling Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Maturidi rejects al-Ka'bi's claim that the term 'Qadariyya' originates from Arab linguistic habits.

Core Idea
The name 'Qadariyya' is a revealed designation, not a linguistic accident.

Al-Ka'bi attempted to justify the sectarian label 'Qadariyya' by arguing that Arabs habitually nickname those who excessively mention something. Al-Maturidi dismantles this claim, showing that the Prophet's condemnation of the Qadariyya predates any such naming practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that al-Maturidi begins by directly stating that al-Ka'bi erred.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's original claim
Understand that al-Ka'bi claimed the Arabs used to nickname people who frequently mentioned a thing out of place, and thus the term 'Qadariyya' arose from excessive mention of qadar.
This is the foundation of al-Ka'bi's argument that the label is merely linguistic.
3
Note the first error
Al-Maturidi points out that al-Ka'bi's narration about Arab habit is itself inaccurate.
The first error: what he narrated about the Arabs.
4
Note the second error
Even if some Arabs said 'this is the decree of God' for blameworthy acts, it is only the common people, not the elite. The elite avoid it.
The second error: what he narrated about them.
5
Understand the key argument from revelation
The Prophet's condemnation of the Qadariyya came before any sect existed, so the name cannot be derived from later Arab linguistic habit.
This is the decisive refutation: the term is based on divine truth, not custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آراء الكعبي في القدرية وبيان فسادها ] ثم ذكر الكعبي أن من عادة العرب تلقيب من يَلْهَج بشيء فيُكثر ذكره في غير موضعه حتى يجاوز الحد فيه، ونسبة ذلك إليه ؛ وهم يفعلون ذلك حتى قالوا في كل فاحشة وأمر ذميم هذا قدر الله. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } أخطأ في هذا القدر من الدعوى من أوجه. أحدها ما حكى عن العرب. والثاني ما حكى عنهم هم لا يقولون ذلك، وإن كان يقوله فلا يقوله من بهم يُعرف أسماء النحل ؛ إنما يذكره العوام، فأما الخواص فهم لا يذكرون ذلك، بل يكرهون ذكر ذلك خشية أن يذكر على الاعتذار فيما لا عذر لهم والعرب لو عملت الذي قال إنما عملت فيمن ظهر على التلقيب لا للتحقيق؛ ونحن فيما حقه التحقيق لما عن رسول الله جاء ذلك فدم أهله. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضاً إن الذم جاء من عند رسول الله ، ولم يكن في ذلك الوقت من يُعرف بهذا الفعل، ولا كانت النّحلة التي أبدعت العرب لها الاسم، فلا يحتمل الاسم الذي قال لهذا. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
[Views of al-Ka'bi on the Qadariyya and exposition of their corruption] Then al-Ka'bi mentioned that it is from the habit of the Arabs to nickname one who insists on something and frequently mentions it out of place until exceeding the limit, and to attribute that to him; and they do that until they said about every obscenity and blameworthy matter: 'This is the decree of God.' The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: He erred in this amount of claim in several respects. First: what he narrated about the Arabs. Second: what he narrated about them—they do not say that, and if someone says it, he is not from those by whom the names of sects are known; rather, the common people mention it, but the elite do not mention it, rather they dislike mentioning it for fear that it might be mentioned as an excuse in matters where they have no excuse. And if the Arabs did what he said, they only did it regarding one who appeared to be nicknaming, not for the truth; and we, regarding what is due to the truth, came from the Messenger of God that he condemned its people. And there is no power except with God. Also, the condemnation came from the Messenger of God, and at that time there was no one known for this act, nor was there the sect that the Arabs invented the name for, so the name that he mentioned cannot apply to this. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's argument that the term 'Qadariyya' (those who deny predestination) is merely a linguistic habit of Arabs to nickname those who frequently mention something. Al-Maturidi begins by stating that al-Ka'bi erred in several ways. First, his claim about Arab custom is inaccurate. Second, even if some Arabs said 'this is the decree of God' for blameworthy acts, it is not the custom of the learned elite but only the common people. The elite avoid it lest it be used as an excuse. Third, even if Arabs did nickname based on excessive mention, it was not for the purpose of truth. But the Prophet's condemnation of the Qadariyya is based on truth, and at that time there was no one known for that belief, so the name cannot be derived from Arab linguistic habit. Thus, al-Ka'bi's linguistic argument fails to support the labeling of the Qadariyya.

Key Terms
قدرية (Qadariyya)
تلقيب (nicknaming)
نحلة (sect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'Qadariyya' is merely a linguistic accident derived from Arab naming habits.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that the term is based on the Prophet's condemnation, which predates any such naming practice.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of al-Maturidi's refutation in this passage?
A) He agrees with al-Ka'bi that Arab naming habits justify the term Qadariyya.
B) He argues that the name Qadariyya comes from divine revelation, not Arab custom.
C) He claims the Prophet never condemned the Qadariyya.
D) He says the elite Arabs always used 'Qadariyya' correctly.

Key Takeaway
The term 'Qadariyya' is not a product of Arab linguistic habit but a designation rooted in the Prophet's condemnation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a sin or failure, do you ever catch yourself thinking or saying 'it was qadar' to soften the blow? How does al-Maturidi's warning—that the elite dislike even mentioning qadar as an excuse—challenge that habit? What would it take for you to replace that excuse with sincere repentance?
?
The Prophet condemned a group that did not yet exist. How does that reality reshape your understanding of divine foreknowledge and human responsibility? Does it make you more alert to hidden tendencies in your own heart that might lead to blameworthy attitudes toward qadar?
?
Al-Maturidi insists that theological names come from revelation, not custom. Is there a belief or practice in your life that you justify simply because 'everyone does it' or 'people have always said this'? How can you test it against the Prophet's teachings rather than cultural norms?

Why Qadariyya Applies to Affirmers, Not Deniers
Al-Maturidi answers why the term Qadariyya applies to those who affirm human power, not those who deny it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'Qadariyya' is applied to those who affirm human power rather than those who deny divine decree, based on al-Maturidi's reasoning.

Core Idea
Names follow the affirmer, not the denier.

In this passage, al-Maturidi addresses a common confusion: why are the Ahl al-Sunna (who say 'no qadr' in the sense of rejecting human autonomy) not called Qadariyya? The answer lies in a principle of attribution: a name belongs to the one who claims and affirms a thing for themselves, not to the one who denies it. Al-Maturidi also provides two reasons against a hypothetical counter-argument. Understanding this clarifies a key polemical term in early kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Read the passage once. Notice the question: why attribute the name to the denier? Al-Maturidi's answer states a principle: a thing is attributed to the affirmer, not the denier.
The 'denier' here refers to those who say 'no qadr' meaning they reject the idea that humans independently determine actions.
2
Examine the two reasons
Al-Maturidi anticipates a hypothetical: 'You affirm it by saying we determine our actions.' He gives two reasons why this does not make one a Qadari. The first: the name derives from muqaddir (one who determines). The second: otherwise, everyday speech would make everyone a Qadari.
These reasons show that the theological claim of independent determination, not the mere word, defines the sect.
3
Reflect on the polemical significance
Consider how this argument defends the Sunni position. The Qadariyya are those who affirm that humans create their own actions, not those who affirm divine decree. This refutes the accusation that denying human autonomy makes one a Qadari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل عنا سؤالاً دل على حيرته فقال: «نسبتم إليه بقولكم : لا قدر» فأجاب بأن لا ينسب الشيء إلى النافي. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وما قاله صدق، وإنما ينسب إلى المدعي والمثبت لنفسه، وهو حيث يقول : تخرج الأفعال على قدرة التي قدر العبد عليها . ثم قال : لو قيل : أثبتم ذلك بقولكم : «نحن نقدر أعمالنا». قال: لا يجب لوجهين. أحدهما أن الاسم منه مُقدِّر . والثاني أنه لا تمانع له في القول : إنه يقدّر صلاته وثوبه وداره وأمر سفره، فيجب أن يكونوا كلهم قدرية.
Then he asked us a question indicating his confusion, saying: 'You attribute it [the name] to him by your saying: no decree (lā qadr).' He answered that a thing is not attributed to the denier (al-nāfī). The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: What he said is true; it is attributed only to the claimant and affirmer (al-muthbit) for himself, and he is the one who says: 'Actions come forth from the power which the servant has determined.' Then he said: If it were said: 'You affirm that by your saying: we determine our actions.' He said: It is not necessary for two reasons. First: the name [Qadariyya] comes from the one who determines (muqaddir). Second: that there is no obstacle for him to say that he determines his prayer, his garment, his house, and the matter of his journey, so they would all have to be Qadariyya.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector questions why the term Qadariyya is applied to the Ahl al-Sunna (who say 'no decree' in the sense of rejecting human autonomy) rather than to those who affirm human power. The answer clarifies that a name is not attributed to the denier of something but to the one who claims and affirms it for themselves. Al-Maturidi then addresses a further hypothetical: if someone says that by saying 'we determine our actions' they affirm the name, the reply provides two reasons. First, the name 'Qadariyya' derives from the one who determines (muqaddir) i.e., the one who claims to be the determiner, not the one who merely uses the word 'qadr'. Second, if the mere use of the word 'qadr' made one a Qadari, then everyone who says 'I determine my prayer, garment, etc.' would be a Qadari, which is absurd. Thus, the name is reserved for those who theologically affirm that human power determines actions independently of God's decree.

Key Terms
النافي (al-nāfī, the denier)
المثبت (al-muthbit, the affirmer)
مقدّر (muqaddir, one who determines)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term Qadariyya applies to those who deny qadr (divine decree).
Correction: Al-Maturidi explains that the name Qadariyya applies to those who affirm human power as independent, not to those who deny it. The denier does not claim the attribute for themselves, so the name does not attach to them.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, why is the term Qadariyya not applied to those who say 'no qadr'?
A) Because they are not Arabs.
B) Because names are attributed to the affirmer, not the denier.
C) Because they never use the word 'qadr'.
D) Because the Prophet forbade that name.

Key Takeaway
The term Qadariyya applies to those who affirm human power, not those who deny divine decree, because names are attributed to the affirmer, not the denier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your daily choices—what to wear, where to go, how to act—do you ever feel that you are the ultimate decider? How does this lesson’s emphasis on attributing names to affirmers challenge that feeling? What is one tangible way you can remind yourself that your power to act comes from Allah alone, starting today?
?
The passage says the name Qadariyya is reserved for those who theologically claim that humans determine their actions. In your heart, do you ever resent or question Allah’s decree over your circumstances? If so, how can you actively shift from being a ‘denier’ of your reliance on Him to an ‘affirmer’ of His absolute control, even in small frustrations?
?
Imagine someone uses the phrase 'I determined to do this' in everyday speech. According to the two reasons given, why doesn’t that make them a Qadari? Now examine your own speech—do you ever speak as if your actions are independent of Allah’s will? What specific phrase can you replace with one that acknowledges Allah’s power over your actions?

Final Clarification on the Term 'Qadr' and Its Application
Al-Maturidi explains the proper application of the term Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term Qadariyya is applied specifically to those who deny divine decree in favor of human autonomy.

Core Idea
The name Qadariyya comes from a claim of power against God's decree, not from God's attribute.

Al-Maturidi concludes his discussion on the term 'Qadr' by explaining that the label 'Qadari' is not derived from God's attribute but from a specific theological stance. He uses an analogy from religious nomenclature to show that the name indicates a particular belief about human power versus divine decree. This passage clarifies the proper application of the term and distinguishes the position of the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an initial sense of al-Maturidi's argument.
Notice the analogy with Christian and Jewish terms.
2
Understand the analogy
Al-Maturidi compares the formation of 'Qadari' from 'qadr' to 'Christianization' from 'Christian'. Ask yourself why this analogy matters.
It shows that the name is derived from the action attributed to the person.
3
Analyze the key distinction
Al-Maturidi states that God can be called by the attribute (Al-Qadir) but not 'Qadari'. This proves the term refers to a specific matter or meaning in the person, not to God.
This is the logical pivot of the passage.
4
Identify the theological implication
The term Qadariyya applies to those who claim that actions occur against God's decree. Al-Maturidi mentions the Mu'tazila as holding this view. Reflect on why this distinguishes them.
The conflict is about who controls human actions.
5
Summarize in your own words
Write a one-sentence summary of al-Maturidi's conclusion about the term 'Qadari'.
This reinforces understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : فأما الحرف الأول فقدر وقدر واحد. وبعد، فإن الفعل في النصراني واليهودى التنصر والتهود، والاسم على ما يرى فمثله في القدر. والثاني قد يُسمى الله تعالى بذلك، ثم لا يقال «قدرى». فثبت أن ذلك يرجع إلى أمر خاص، أو إلى معنى فيمن إليه . فإن كان إلى أمر خاص فهو في الدين، ومن نسبه إلى نفسه فهو أحق به، وإن كان المرجع فيه إلى المعنى فهو لأنهم على ذلك القول يرون حقيقة الخروج على قدر الله لا على قدر العبد . والمعتزلة تزعم أنه على قدرهم يخرج. والله الموفق.
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: As for the first matter, 'qadr' and 'qadar' are one. Afterwards, the action in the Christian and the Jew is Christianization and Judaization, and the name as you see - similarly is the case with 'qadr'. And the second: God Most High may be called by that [name], yet it is not said 'Qadari'. So it is established that that [name] refers to a specific matter, or to a meaning in the one to whom it is attributed. If it refers to a specific matter, it is in religion, and whoever attributes it to himself is more deserving of it. And if the reference is to the meaning, it is because they, according to that statement, see the reality of going out against God's decree (qadr Allah) not against the servant's decree. And the Mu'tazila claim that it goes out according to their decree. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a concluding analysis by al-Maturidi on the proper application of the term Qadr/Qadariyya. He first clarifies that 'qadr' and 'qadar' are essentially the same word. Then he draws an analogy: just as the actions of Christians and Jews are called 'Christianization' and 'Judaization' and the names are derived from those actions, similarly the term 'Qadariyya' is derived from the concept of qadr. He notes that God may be called by the attribute of Qadr (e.g., al-Qadir), yet no one calls God 'Qadari'. Therefore, the name 'Qadariyya' must refer to something specific, either a specific matter (i.e., a theological belief) or a meaning inherent in the person. If it refers to a specific matter, it is a matter of religion (i.e., belief in predestination or human power). Those who attribute that matter to themselves (i.e., claim power over their actions) are more deserving of the name. If the reference is to the meaning, then it is because the Qadariyya hold that actions come forth against God's decree, not against the servant's decree. The Mu'tazila claim that actions happen according to their own decree. Thus, the name is reserved for those who deny God's decree in favor of human autonomy.

Key Terms
قدر / قدر (qadr/qadar)
قدرى (Qadari)
قدر الله (qadr Allah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term Qadariyya applies to anyone who uses the word 'qadr' or is derived from God's attribute Al-Qadir.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that the term applies specifically to those who claim actions are against God's decree, i.e., those who affirm human autonomy over divine decree.

Checkpoint
What does al-Maturidi conclude about the term 'Qadari'?
A) It applies to anyone who uses the word 'qadr'.
B) It applies to God because He is Al-Qadir.
C) It applies only to those who attribute actions to their own power against God's decree.
D) It applies only to Christians and Jews.

Key Takeaway
The label Qadariyya is reserved for those who assert human power against God's decree, not for those who affirm God's attribute of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something good, do you ever credit your own intelligence or effort more than God's decree? How does this lesson's distinction between divine power and human claim challenge your internal narrative? Will you consciously remind yourself in your next success that all power belongs to God alone?
?
Think of a moment when you felt angry or frustrated because things didn't go your way. In that moment, were you in reality 'going out against God's decree'? This lesson says that claiming autonomy against God's decree is the essence of Qadariyya. Are you willing to repent from any hidden rebellion in your heart and fully submit to whatever God decrees, even when it hurts?
?
If you were to be honest, do you genuinely believe that every event, including your own choices, falls completely under God's decree? Or is there a part of you that reserves a 'small independent power' for yourself? This lesson forces you to decide: will you accept God's complete sovereignty or will you cling to your own imagined autonomy? What will you do today to align your heart with the truth?

The Two Extremes: Murji'a and Qadariyya
Maturidi defines Murji'a and Qadariyya and critiques the Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the defining beliefs of the Murji'a and Qadariyya sects regarding divine management and the implications of the Qadariyya position.

Core Idea
The Murji'a give all to Allah; the Qadariyya give all to creation.

Imam Maturidi begins by identifying two extreme positions on the relationship between divine will and human action. The Murji'a defer all reality of actions to Allah, while the Qadariyya negate Allah's management entirely, attributing all agency to creation. This lesson examines these positions and their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the definitions of both sects and the described outcome of the Qadariyya position.
The passage uses parallel structure to contrast the two sects.
2
Identify the core difference
Compare how Murji'a and Qadariyya attribute actions: one to Allah, the other to creation. This is the central theological divergence.
3
Reflect on the Qadariyya consequence
Consider the logical conclusion Maturidi draws: if creation manages everything, Allah's role is reduced to mere information about the afterlife, and no actions are realized from Him except bringing existence from non-existence.
This shows the problematic nature of the Qadariyya position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في هذا أن المرجئة أرجت حقيقة أفعال الخلق إلى الله، والقدرية هي التي نفت عن الله تدبيرها، وجعلت كل التدبير فيها للخلق، حتى مضى العالم وتم على تدبير الخلق، هم أفنوا وأبقوا ، وبه قام تدبير الله من البعث وأهل الجنة والنار، ليس الله في ذلك إلا الإخبار. وكذا لا يتحقق له في العالم أفعال سوى كونه بعد أن لم يكن. والعدل هو المذهب المتوسط بينهما، وذلك معنى قول الله عز وجل : وكذلك جعلناكم أمة وسطا، والآية، وقول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «خير الأمور أوساطها». ونسب إلى الحشوية الخطأ، ولا أحد سلم عنه . والذي قاله إنما قال قوم منهم.
And the basis in this is that the Murji'a deferred the reality of the actions of creation to Allah, and the Qadariyya are those who negated from Allah their management, and made all the management in them for creation, until the world passed and was completed on the management of creation, they annihilated and preserved, and by it stood the management of Allah of the resurrection and the people of Paradise and the Fire, Allah in that is only information. And likewise there is not realized for Him in the world actions other than His being after He was not. And justice ('adl) is the middle doctrine between them, and that is the meaning of the saying of Allah Almighty: 'Thus We have made you a middle nation' (2:143), the verse, and the saying of the Messenger of Allah peace be upon him: 'The best of affairs are their middles.' And error was attributed to the Hashwiyya, but no one is safe from it. And what he said was only said by some of them.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage defines the two extreme doctrines: the Murji'a defer (ارجأت) the reality of creation's actions to Allah, meaning they attribute all actions ultimately to Allah, while the Qadariyya negate Allah's management and assign all management to creation. Maturidi then describes the consequence of the Qadariyya position: the world is run by creation, and Allah's management is only in matters like resurrection and the afterlife, and He is merely an informant. Then he presents the middle way, 'adl (justice), as the balanced doctrine between these two extremes, citing the Quranic verse calling Muslims a 'middle nation' and the hadith 'the best of affairs are their middles.' He ends by noting that error is attributed to the Hashwiyya, but no one is immune from error, and such claims come only from some of them.

Key Terms
مرجئة
قدرية
تدبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Qadariyya simply emphasize free will, but Maturidi shows they negate Allah's management entirely.
Correction: Qadariyya do not just affirm human agency; they deny divine involvement in managing actions.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the Qadariyya negate from Allah?
A) Allah's existence
B) Allah's management of actions
C) Allah's knowledge
D) Allah's mercy

Key Takeaway
The Murji'a and Qadariyya represent opposite errors: one exaggerates divine control, the other exaggerates human autonomy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you find yourself acting as if Allah only informs you of what will happen, but the management of your affairs is entirely in your hands? Reflect on one decision where you forgot to seek Allah's aid.
?
The Murji'a defer all actions to Allah, potentially leading to passivity. Are there areas where you use 'tawakkul' as an excuse for inaction? What would it look like to truly combine trust in Allah with taking responsibility?
?
This passage contrasts two extremes. Which extreme do you lean towards more often—over-relying on your own planning or neglecting your duty by saying 'Allah will take care of it'? What is one step you can take today to correct that imbalance?

The Middle Way of 'Adl
Maturidi presents 'adl as the middle doctrine with Quranic and hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of 'adl as the balanced doctrine between the extremes of Murji'a and Qadariyya, and its scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
'Adl is the middle path between extremes, confirmed by Quran and hadith.

After presenting the two extreme doctrines, Imam Maturidi introduces the correct position: 'adl (justice) as the middle way. He supports this with the Quranic description of Muslims as a 'middle nation' and the prophetic saying that the best affairs are their middles. This lesson explores this balanced doctrine and a concluding remark about the universality of error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'adl

2
Understand the scriptural basis

The Quran calls Muslims a 'middle nation' (ummatan wasatan) and the hadith praises moderation.
3
Consider the remark on Hashwiyya
Maturidi notes that error is attributed to the Hashwiyya, but no one is immune from error. This serves as a caution that even those claiming the middle way can err.
This shows the need for humility in theological claims.

The Qadariyya's Proof from 'Take It with Strength'
Maturidi presents the Qadariyya's proof from the verse 'Take it with strength'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qadariyya used the Quranic verse 'Take it with strength' to argue that human power precedes action, and the interpretive move they made.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya's proof hinges on interpreting 'strength' as the means enabling action.

This passage introduces a key proof used by the Qadariyya to support their doctrine that humans have power over their actions before performing them. The proof is based on a specific interpretation of the Quranic verse 'Take it with strength' from the story of Prophet Musa. Understanding this argument is important because it shows how theological positions were grounded in Quranic exegesis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the accompanying translation. Allow the theological claim and the interpretive move to become clear.
The passage is short but dense; focus on the chain of reasoning.
2
Identify the Qadariyya's argument
Notice that the Qadariyya cite the verse as evidence for 'precedence of power over action' (تقديم القدرة الفعل). Ask yourself: how does the verse support that?
3
Understand the interpretation of 'strength'
The people of interpretation (أهل التأويل) explain 'strength' as 'seriousness and striving', and the Qadariyya see it as referring to 'the means' (الأسباب) — i.e., the capacity to act. This is the key interpretive link.
Note that different interpretive communities might read 'strength' differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتجت القدرية في تقديم القدرة الفعل بآي من كتاب الله تعالى، منها قوله: فخذها بقوة . وقال أهل التأويل فاعمل بها بجد واجتهاد؛ فكأنهم رأوا القوة ههنا الأسباب
Then the Qadariyya argued for the precedence of power over action by verses from the Book of Allah Almighty, among them His saying: 'Take it with strength' (7:145). And the people of interpretation said: act with it with seriousness and striving; as if they considered strength here to mean the means.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage reports a proof used by the Qadariyya. They cite the Quranic verse 'Take it with strength' (referring to the tablets given to Moses) to argue that power (قدرة) precedes the action. The interpretation by the people of tafsir (أهل التأويل) is that 'strength' means 'seriousness and striving,' as if they considered 'strength' here to refer to the means or capacity that enables action. This implies that human ability exists before the act, supporting the Qadariyya position that humans have power over their actions.

Key Terms
قدرية
قدرة
فعل
قوة
أسباب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya proof is based on a clear literal reading of the verse.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the Qadariyya rely on a specific interpretation of 'strength' as 'means' (الأسباب), not a straightforward literal reading.

Checkpoint
How do the Qadariyya interpret the word 'strength' (قوة) in the verse 'Take it with strength'?
A) As physical strength
B) As the means or capacity enabling action
C) As divine power
D) As the Quran itself

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya used the verse 'Take it with strength' to argue that human power precedes action by interpreting 'strength' as the means or capacity enabling action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a command in the Quran like 'Take it with strength,' do you immediately look for a way to limit your responsibility by reinterpreting 'strength' as something you lack? How often do you use your own reasoning to soften what Allah has commanded, rather than submitting fully to the apparent meaning and seeking His help? Commit to reading one challenging verse this week without trying to explain it away.
?
The Qadariyya turned 'strength' into 'means' to defend their belief in human autonomy. Is there an area of your life where you have subtly reinterpreted Islamic teachings to preserve your own sense of control or independence? Ask yourself: what would it cost you to admit that every action you perform is utterly dependent on Allah's will and power?
?
The 'people of interpretation' in this passage were doing tafsir, but their conclusion served a theological agenda. When you learn about Islam—from scholars, books, or lectures—how carefully do you examine whether the interpretation you are receiving is driven by a prior commitment to a particular worldview? What is one belief you hold that might be more about comfort than truth, and what would it mean for you to subject it to honest scrutiny today?

Hadith on Murji'a and Qadariyya: Definitions and Errors
Maturidi quotes a hadith defining Murji'a and Qadariyya and explains their doctrines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the Murji'a and Qadariyya as presented in a prophetic hadith and Maturidi's critique of their theological errors.

Core Idea
Two groups err by either denying God's control or deferring all actions to God.

Maturidi begins by citing a hadith that excludes two groups from the Prophet's intercession. He then clarifies their defining beliefs: the Murji'a defer the reality of acts to God, while the Qadariyya deny God's involvement entirely. This sets up a critique of both extremes, showing the logical consequences of their views.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the direct statement about intercession and the two groups.
2
Identify definitions of each group
Find where Maturidi defines the Qadariyya (denial of qadar) and the Murji'a (deferral of acts to God). Note how each definition relates to divine and creatorial agency.
3
Reflect on the paradoxical outcome
Maturidi describes a consequence of the Qadariyya view: that the world runs on creation's management, leaving God only as an informer. Consider why this is problematic.
4
Summarize the key error of each group
Based on Maturidi's explanation, articulate the core error of the Qadariyya (denying God's power) and the Murji'a (delaying judgment).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أمتي لا تنالهم شفاعتي: القدرية والمرجئة. وفسرت القدرية بنفيهم القدر عن الله. هي التي أرجت حقيقة أفعال الخلق إلى الله، والقدرية هي التي نفت عن الله تدبيرها، وجعلت كل التدبير فيها للخلق، حتى مضى العالم وتم على تدبير الخلق، هم أفنوا وأبقوا، وبه قام تدبير الله من البعث وأهل الجنة والنار، ليس الله في ذلك إلا الإخبار. وكذا لا يتحقق له في العالم أفعال سوى كونه بعد أن لم يكن.
From my ummah, my intercession will not reach two groups: the Qadariyya and the Murji'a. And the Qadariyya are interpreted as those who deny predestination (qadar) from God. [The Murji'a] are those who defer the reality of the acts of creation to God, and the Qadariyya are those who deny God's management of it, and made all management belong to creation, such that the world passed and was completed upon the management of creation—they annihilated and they preserved, and by it God's management of the resurrection and the people of Paradise and Hell was established; God has no part in that except informing. And likewise, no actions are realized for Him in the world except His coming-to-be after not having been.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a hadith-based classification and definition. Maturidi first cites a hadith stating that two groups from his ummah will not receive his intercession: the Qadariyya and the Murji'a. He then explains the terms. The Qadariyya are those who deny that God has control over events (they attribute power to themselves). The Murji'a are those who defer acts of creation entirely to God, meaning they delay judgment. But his explanation of Murji'a is different: they defer the reality of acts to God, while Qadariyya deny God's involvement, attributing all management to creation. Then he describes a paradoxical outcome: the world runs on creation's management, but God's management of afterlife still stands. He concludes that God has no real actions in the world except that He causes existence after non-existence. This highlights Maturidi's refutation of both extremes.

Key Terms
القدرية
المرجئة
القدر
الأرجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: that the Qadariyya and Murji'a are outside of Islam entirely
Correction: The hadith says they will not receive the Prophet's intercession, not that they are outside the faith. Maturidi uses the hadith to define their theological errors, not to declare them non-Muslims.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the Qadariyya defined?
A) They deny God's power over all things
B) They defer acts of creation to God
C) They deny the intercession of the Prophet
D) They affirm absolute predestination

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses a prophetic hadith to distinguish two theological errors: the Qadariyya deny God's control, and the Murji'a defer all actions to God, both of which misrepresent divine and human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself attributing your successes entirely to your own effort while forgetting God's management? That is the error of the Qadariyya. What is one area of your life where you can consciously acknowledge God's decree today, even in something small?
?
Have you ever used 'tawakkul' (reliance on God) as an excuse to avoid taking action, secretly believing that everything is already fixed so your effort doesn't matter? That is the Murji'a error. What is one step you can take this week to combine reliance on God with active responsibility?
?
The hadith says the Prophet's intercession will not reach these groups. How does it make you feel to know that your theology could place you among those rejected? Are you willing to examine your beliefs about God's control and your own responsibility, and align them with the balanced Sunni position, even if it challenges your habits?

Refutation of Qadariyya's Proof from Jinn and the Woman
Maturidi refutes Qadariyya's use of the jinn's and the woman's statements about strength and trustworthiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Quranic verses about the jinn and the woman do not support the Qadariyya doctrine of independent human ability.

Core Idea
Strength mentioned in the Quran is time-specific, not a permanent inherent ability.

The Qadariyya cite two Quranic verses where the jinn and the woman mention strength and trustworthiness to argue for inherent human capacity. Maturidi refutes this by showing that the strength referred to was specific to a past moment and does not imply a persistent power independent of God. This lesson examines his reasoning and its implications for the doctrine of divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of Maturidi's argument. Notice the two Quranic references and his response.
The passage is dense; take your time to understand each part.
2
Identify the Qadariyya's proof
The Qadariyya use two verses: the jinn's statement (Surah al-Naml) and the woman's statement (Surah al-Qasas). Identify why they think these support human ability.
They see 'strong and trustworthy' as descriptions of inherent qualities.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi argues that the strength of Moses was only known at the time of drawing water and did not persist. Similarly, the jinn's strength was based on a past test. Thus the verses do not prove a permanent ability.
He also offers a second interpretation: strength is for the time of use, not a separate pre-existing power.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi ends with 'There is no power except through God,' affirming that all power is from God, not independently possessed.
This is the core of the refutation: human ability is not self-sustaining.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا أيضا بقول الجنّي: وإني عليه لقوي أمين، وقول المرأة: إن خير من استأجرت القوي الأمين. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: والحرفان مما ليس لهم التعلق به، لما كانت قوة موسى التي علمت بها إنما علمتها وقت النزح، وهي لا تبقى إلى ذلك الوقت؛ وكذلك قوة الجنّي على ما امتحن نفسه فيما سبق. والله الموفق. والثاني على إرادة وقت الاستعمال، بالعادة الجارية بالحدوث في كل وقت لما شاء ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد احتجوا بما في القرآن من ذكر الاستطاعة، وقد بيّنا ذلك الوجه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And they also argued with the saying of the jinn: 'And indeed I am strong and trustworthy,' and the saying of the woman: 'Indeed the best of those you hire is the strong and trustworthy.' The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: And the two phrases are not such that they can derive support from them, because the strength of Moses that was known was only known at the time of drawing water, and it does not remain until that time; and likewise the strength of the jinn in what he tested himself with previously. And God is the grantor of success. And the second [interpretation] is that it intends the time of use, according to the customary habit of coming-to-be in every time as He wills, and there is no power except through God. And they have argued with what is in the Quran regarding the mention of istitā'a (ability), and we have already explained that aspect. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage contains a second refutation of Qadariyya evidence. The Qadariyya cite two Quranic verses: the jinn saying he is strong and trustworthy (Surah al-Naml), and the woman saying hire the strong and trustworthy (Surah al-Qasas). Maturidi argues that these do not support their case because the strength of Moses (in the story) was known only at a specific time (drawing water) and did not persist. Similarly, the jinn's strength was based on a past test. Thus these verses do not prove an inherent permanent ability. He then states that the proper interpretation is that the strength is for the time of use, not a separate pre-existent power. He concludes with 'there is no power except through God,' affirming that all power is from God.

Key Terms
القوة
الأمانة
الاستطاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic praises of Moses and the jinn as 'strong and trustworthy' prove that humans possess inherent, permanent ability (istitā'a) independent of God's will.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the strength mentioned was specific to a past moment (Moses drawing water, the jinn's past test) and does not imply a continuous inherent power. All ability is created by God at the time of action.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the Qadariyya's proof from the story of Moses and the woman?
A) The verses are not authentic
B) The strength mentioned was specific to a past moment
C) The woman was lying
D) The jinn was not strong

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya by showing that Quranic references to strength and trustworthiness refer to context-specific, transient abilities, not inherent permanent power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish a difficult task, do you silently credit your own innate strength, or do you pause to acknowledge that this very strength was created for that moment by God? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized Maturidi's argument that ability is not your permanent possession?
?
Consider a recent moment when you felt especially capable or trustworthy. How would your self-perception shift if you believed that those qualities were granted to you in that instant and could vanish just as quickly? Are you prepared to face a moment when you are not strong or trustworthy?
?
The Qadariyya used Quranic verses to claim independence from God. Have you ever used your own abilities as an excuse to feel self-sufficient, forgetting that every breath and every thought is sustained by Him? What concrete step can you take tomorrow to remind yourself that 'there is no power except through God'?

Refutation of the Jabriyya: The Absurdity of Denying Human Agency
Maturidi critiques the Jabriyya for denying human agency and forcing absurd conclusions about God's commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Jabriyya position leads to absurd contradictions, particularly regarding God's commands and justice.

Core Idea
If humans have no real actions, then God punishes Himself—an absurd and impious consequence.

Maturidi now turns to critique the Jabriyya, who deny all human agency. He shows that their view, if carried to its logical conclusion, forces God into self-contradiction: commanding Himself, forbidding Himself, and then punishing others for His own actions. This reduces divine justice to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a rhetorical question to expose the flaw.
2
Identify the logical consequences
Trace how Maturidi derives absurdities from the Jabriyya's denial of human agency. List the steps: all actions from God → commands directed to God → God commits forbidden acts → God punishes others for them.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Recognize that Maturidi is using a reductio ad absurdum: the Jabriyya's doctrine leads to a conclusion that contradicts God's attributes of wisdom and mercy. This shows the doctrine is false.
The final line 'they must not find pain' reinforces the conclusion that without human agency, punishment is unjust.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجبرية المعروفة عندنا هم الذين تلقبوا بالجبر، وأحالوا القدرة - على ما في الفعل جَعْلُ الله كاذبًا ـ وأرجوا جميع الأفعال إلى الله، ولم يثبتوا للعباد في التحقيق فعلاً. قيل: هل يقول لهم الله: لم فعلتم ذا ولم لا تفعلون ذا، أو يقول: افعلوا ذا ولا تفعلوا ذا في التحقيق؛ بل إن أمر أو نهى فإنما يأمر في التحقيق نفسه وينهى نفسه؟ ثم هو يرتكب المنهي في التحقيق، ويأمر ويطيع هو في الحقيقة، ثم يعاقب غيره فيعذبه ويثيبه، ونسميه مع حكيما رحميا. جل [عن ذلك] من صفته الرحمة والحكمة. وعلى ذلك يجب أن لا يجدوا الألم
Then the well-known Jabriyya among us are those who are nicknamed with jabr (compulsion) and they refer back power—concerning the act making God a liar—and they defer all actions to God, and they do not affirm for the servants any actual deed. It is said: Does God say to them: 'Why did you do this and why did you not do this,' or does He say: 'Do this and do not do this' in reality? Rather, if He commands or forbids, then only does He command Himself and forbid Himself? Then He commits the forbidden in reality, and He commands and obeys Himself in truth, then He punishes others and torments and rewards [them], and we call Him wise and merciful despite this? Exalted is He above that from His attribute of mercy and wisdom. And accordingly, they must not find pain.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a reductio ad absurdum against the Jabriyya, who deny any human agency and attribute all actions to God. Maturidi presents the logical consequences: if humans have no real actions, then God's commands and prohibitions are directed to Himself, and He would be commanding Himself and forbidding Himself, then He would commit the very things He forbids (since He is the only agent), then He punishes others for what He Himself does. This would make God unjust, contradicting His wisdom and mercy. Therefore, the Jabriyya position is untenable. The final sentence 'they must not find pain' likely means that if there is no human action, then humans cannot be responsible for sin, so punishment would be unjust.

Key Terms
الجبرية
الجبر
القدرة
الحكمة
الرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying human agency is a way to glorify God's power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that it actually leads to absurdities that undermine God's justice and mercy.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what absurd consequence follows from the Jabriyya's denial of human agency?
A) God becomes the only agent and ends up commanding and punishing Himself
B) Humans become free from all responsibility
C) God's mercy becomes unlimited
D) The world ceases to exist

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Jabriyya view, which denies human agency, leads to a self-contradictory picture of God that cannot be reconciled with His justice and mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself using 'qadr' (divine decree) to minimize your own responsibility for a sin you committed? How does Maturidi's argument force you to stop hiding behind God's power and own your actions fully starting today?
?
When you read that the Jabriyya view makes God punish Himself for His own actions, does it uncover any hidden assumption in your heart that God might be unjust or cruel? Are you ready to surrender any image of God that does not align with His perfect wisdom and mercy, no matter how comforting that image feels?
?
Maturidi implies that if humans are not real agents, then pain and punishment lose their meaning as just consequences. When you face suffering in your own life, do you ever secretly blame God or feel that your suffering is unmerited? How will this passage change the way you connect your actions to your trials from this moment on?
