Maturidi's Refutation of the Mu'tazila Accusation of Jabr
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that Maturidis are Jabriyya by showing the Mu'tazila themselves are closer to Jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi turns the Mu'tazila accusation of Jabr back against them by showing their own doctrine commits them to a form of fatalism.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila accuse others of Jabr, but their own view of power is closer to true fatalism.

Maturidi now addresses a common polemical charge: that the Sunni stance on divine power leads to fatalism (Jabr). The Mu'tazila claimed that denying human power before the act is Jabr. Maturidi responds by analyzing the concept of power and choice, arguing that the Mu'tazila themselves fall into a more extreme form of Jabr. This lesson unpacks his clever reversal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
Notice how Maturidi sets up the accusation before turning it around.
2
Identify the core accusation and counter-claim
The Mu'tazila claim that denying power before the act is Jabr. Maturidi counters that they affirm action at a time when there is no power, which is closer to Jabr.
This is the central reversal.
3
Understand the logical argument about timing
Maturidi argues that if action is affirmed without power, that is more Jabr-like than affirming action with power. He uses the concept of 'conceivability' to build his case.
Focus on the comparison between 'with existence' and 'with removal of incapacity'.
4
Consider the analogy with sensory perception
Maturidi gives an analogy: just as perception fails when the sense is absent, so action fails without power. This supports his claim that absence of power is more detrimental to choice.
The analogy illustrates why power must accompany action for genuine choice.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how Maturidi's argument not only defends against the accusation but also exposes an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position.
This lesson establishes that the Maturidi view preserves meaningful human choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نَسب القدرية - وهم الذين تلقبوا بالاعتزال ـ الجبر إلينا، على تبرينا عن عقداً وقولاً، لكن كذبهم في هذا نحو كذبهم علينا في اسم القدرية. ثم نذكر أحقنا بذلك في مقابلة المذهبين ، ليعلموا جرأة المعتزلة وعظيم سفههم كما بيّنا في القدرية. وادعوا علينا اسم الجبر بإنكارنا كون قدرة الفعل قبل وقته . ثم هم حققوا الفعل في وقت لا قدرة فيه؛ وتحقيق الفعل في وقت الوصف بلا قدرة أقرب إلى معنى الجبر من تحقيقها مع الفعل لمن عقل الجبر والاختيار. ومما يوضح ذلك أن الفعل غير متوهم في حال العجز، ومتوهم وجوده في حال ارتفاع العجز ؛ فكان توهمه مع الارتفاع أرفع وأبلغ من توهمه مع الوجود، إذ هو سبب المنع. فكذلك القدرة التي هي سبب الفعل في الحقيقة . ويؤيد ذلك فساد الدرك بالبصر مع ذهابه بما تقدم من البصر، وكذا السمع وعمل كل الحواس. فكذلك كان فساد فعل الاختيار مع العجز، وفقد القدرة أوضح منه مع الوجود . ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the Qadariyya—those who call themselves Mu'tazila—ascribed Jabr to us, while we are innocent of it in belief and statement, but their lie in this is like their lie against us in the name Qadariyya. Then we mention we are more entitled to that in comparison of the two schools, so that they may know the audacity of the Mu'tazila and their great foolishness as we explained in [the discussion of] Qadariyya. They claimed the name Jabr upon us because we deny the existence of power of action before its time. Then they affirm the action at a time when there is no power; and affirming action at a time described without power is closer to the meaning of Jabr than affirming it with action, for one who understands Jabr and choice. And what clarifies that is that action is not conceivable in the state of incapacity, and its existence is conceivable in the state of removal of incapacity; so its conceivability with removal is higher and more complete than its conceivability with existence, since it is the cause of prevention. So likewise is power, which is the cause of action in reality. And this is supported by the corruption of perception by sight when it is lost, as preceded regarding sight, and likewise hearing and the work of all senses. So likewise, the corruption of act of choice with incapacity, and the lack of power, is more obvious than it with existence. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. Maturidi states that the Mu'tazila (who called themselves Qadariyya) accuse the Maturidis of Jabr (fatalism). He argues that the Mu'tazila's own doctrine brings them closer to Jabr because they affirm action at a time when the agent has no power (qudra). According to Maturidi, affirming action without power is more akin to Jabr than affirming action with power. He uses the analogy of sensory perception: just as perception fails when the sense is absent, so too action is defective without power. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's accusation backfires.

Key Terms
القدرية (al-qadariyya): the Qadariyya
الجبر (al-jabr): fatalism
القدرة (al-qudra): power/ability
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyār): choice

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi view denies all human power, leading to fatalism.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny human power; he affirms power with the action, not before it. This preserves meaningful choice while acknowledging divine omnipotence.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the Mu'tazila closer to Jabr?
A) They affirm action without power at the time of action
B) They deny divine decree entirely
C) They claim humans create their own actions
D) They reject all causality

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazila's own position is closer to Jabr because they affirm action without power at the time of action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever use the concept of 'fate' to excuse your own shortcomings or sins? How does Maturidi's exposure of the Mu'tazila's self-deception challenge you to take genuine responsibility for your actions?
?
Have you ever accused someone of a fault that you yourself are guilty of? Maturidi points out the hypocrisy of the Mu'tazila. Reflect on an area in your life where you project blame onto others while avoiding your own accountability—what will you do about it today?
?
The Mu'tazila thought their position on power was rational, but Maturidi shows it leads to a worse error. What belief do you hold that feels 'rational' but may actually be harmful or inconsistent with the truth? How can you humbly re-examine it in light of this lesson?

5 min
Mu'tazila View on Will Reduces to Compulsion
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila on will by showing it implies compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila concept of will actually leads to compulsion (jabr), making them akin to the Jabriyya.

Core Idea
If will is separate from action, action becomes compelled.

Maturidi presents a logical critique of the Mu'tazila position on will (irada). He shows that if will only precedes action and does not accompany it, then the action itself occurs without choice, which is the very definition of compulsion (idṭirār). This leads to the surprising conclusion that the Mu'tazila, who claim to uphold free will, are actually advocating a form of Jabr (fatalism).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear.
Maturidi uses a compressed logical style; note the conditional reasoning.
2
Trace the logical steps
Identify the Mu'tazila claim about will, then see what Maturidi deduces from it. Notice how he moves from premise to conclusion.
Look for the phrase 'thabata anna' which signals the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the accusation
Consider why Maturidi calls the Mu'tazila 'false Jabriyya' and what this means for their doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه آخر، أن قول المعتزلة : إن الإرادة هي اختيار الفعل وإنما تكون متقدمة على الفعل؛ وليست بموجودة، وأنه وجد في وقت الوجود بلا إرادة منه ولا اختيار ؛ وحق اختيار الأول عنه زائل، إذ يجوز ورود الاضطرار في الوقت الثاني، ومحال وروده في الوقت الذي فيه الاختيار، والاختيار قائم ؛ ثبت أن فعله في التحصيل ليس باختيار، وأنه اضطرار؛ وعلامة الجبر هذا. وبهذا الفعل يوجبون العداوة والولاية والخلود في الجنة والنار، الواقع وقت وقوعه بلا اختيار ولا قدرة ولا أمر أيضًا ولا نهى. فمن تأمل ذلك وجده عند التحقيق قول الجبرية في التصريح، لكن هؤلاء جبرية كاذبة، وأولئك جبرية صادقة.
Another aspect: that the statement of the Mu'tazila: 'Will is the choice of action and it must precede the action, and it does not exist [at the time of action], and that the action occurred at the time of existence without will or choice from him; and the right of the first choice is removed from him, for it is possible that compulsion occurs in the second time, and impossible that it occurs at the time in which there is choice, while choice is present'—it is established that his action in attainment is not by choice, and that it is compulsion; and the sign of Jabr is this. And by this action they necessitate enmity and friendship and eternity in Paradise and Hellfire, occurring at the time of its occurrence without choice, power, command, or prohibition. So whoever reflects on that finds it in truth a statement of the Jabriyya explicitly, but these are false Jabriyya, and those are truthful Jabriyya.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument. Maturidi examines the Mu'tazila claim that will (irada) is the choice of an action and must precede it, but does not exist at the time of the action itself. If so, the action occurs without will or choice at the moment of occurrence, which means it is compelled (idṭirār). This is the very sign of Jabr. Furthermore, the Mu'tazila then assign eternal consequences like friendship, enmity, Paradise, and Hell based on such compelled actions, which is unjust and more akin to the doctrine of the Jabriyya. Thus, Maturidi concludes that the Mu'tazila are actually false Jabriyya.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irāda): will
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyār): choice
الاضطرار (al-iḍṭirār): compulsion
الجبرية (al-jabriyya): fatalists

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies all free will and is a Jabrite himself.
Correction: Maturidi only exposes the inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position. He affirms that genuine choice exists at the time of action, which the Mu'tazila deny by separating will from action.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say is the 'sign of Jabr' in the Mu'tazila doctrine?
A) The action occurs without will or choice at the time of occurrence
B) The action is attributed to Allah alone
C) The action occurs with full power
D) The will is continuously present

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes that the Mu'tazila concept of will, if will is absent at the moment of action, leads to compulsion—the very thing they oppose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act without consciously choosing in the moment, only to later claim responsibility? How does Maturidi's argument expose the inconsistency in claiming choice while disowning the action at its occurrence?
?
If your eternal destiny were based on actions done without your choice – as the Mu'tazila inadvertently hold – would you find that just? Where in your life have you demanded justice while unintentionally endorsing a system that undermines it?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to defend God's wisdom but ended up with a view that makes Him punish compelled actions. Are there beliefs you hold about God that, upon deeper reflection, contradict His attributes of justice and mercy? What will you do to correct them today?

Mu'tazila View on Desire and Action Proves Jabr
Maturidi shows that Mu'tazila's claim that the agent's action occurs despite his dislike proves compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Mu'tazila's own statements to demonstrate that their position implies compulsion (jabr), and why their attribution of jabr to others is inconsistent.

Core Idea
If an action occurs despite your hate and inability to stop it, that is compulsion.

Maturidi continues his critique of the Mu'tazila by turning their own logic against them. They claim that a person intends an action at the nearest time, and it occurs even if he hates it and cannot prevent it. Yet they still hold him accountable. Maturidi points out that this is nothing but compulsion (jabr), contradicting their denial of jabr. He also notes their inconsistent use of the term 'qadar'. Let's examine his argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila premise
Find the part where Maturidi quotes the Mu'tazila's view. What do they say about intention and action?
The premise is: action occurs even if the agent hates it and intends to repel it.
3
Understand Maturidi's inference of jabr
Ask yourself why Maturidi concludes that this proves compulsion. What is the logical link?
If the agent cannot prevent the action, then it is compelled.
4
Note the additional point about 'qadar'
Maturidi also points out an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila's use of 'qadar'. What is that inconsistency?
They use 'qadar' incorrectly while accusing others of jabr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من قولهم: إن من أراد الفعل لأقرب الأوقات إليه يقع ذلك الفعل له وإن كرهه وأراد صرفه، ويقع له به العداوة والولاية، وإن صار بحيث لا يمكنه الصرف قبل وقوعه أو معه. ثم يكون ذلك الوقت ليس بوقت محال لفوت ذلك الفعل، إذ قد يجوز عندهم فوته بالمنع والقهر . ثبت بما ذكرت وقوعه بالجبر في التحقيق. وأيضًا على قولهم في كثرة جرى اسم القدر في غير موضعه على ألسنتهم يسمون به ، فهو كذلك عندهم، مع قولهم بنسبة الجبر إلى غيرهم. وبالله المعونة والعصمة.
Then from their statements: that whoever intends an action for the nearest time to him, that action occurs for him even if he hates it and intends to repel it, and he incurs for it enmity and friendship, even though he becomes unable to repel it before its occurrence or with it. Then that time is not an impossible time for the loss of that action, for it is possible in their view that it is lost by prevention and coercion. It is established by what I mentioned that its occurrence is by Jabr in truth. And also according to their statement regarding the frequent usage of the term 'qadar' in other than its place on their tongues, they name by it, so it is so according to them, alongside their statement attributing Jabr to others. And with Allah is aid and protection.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a further argument. Maturidi cites the Mu'tazila's own view: a person intends an action at the nearest possible time, and it occurs even if he hates it and wishes to avoid it, yet he is held accountable for it. This, Maturidi argues, is clear compulsion (jabr) — the person cannot prevent it. Additionally, the Mu'tazila frequently misuse the term 'qadar' (divine decree) while accusing others of Jabr, which is inconsistent.

Key Terms
الجبر (al-jabr): compulsion
الإرادة (al-irāda): intention/will
القهر (al-qahr): coercion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage argues that humans have no free will at all.
Correction: The passage does not deny human will; it criticizes the Mu'tazila for holding people accountable for unavoidable acts, exposing their inconsistency.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what proves that the Mu'tazila view leads to Jabr?
A) The action occurs even if the agent hates it and cannot repel it
B) The agent always has full power over the action
C) The agent's will is always effective
D) The action is caused by external factors alone

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazila's own theory of intention and action implies compulsion, contradicting their denial of jabr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there actions in your life that you do not want to do, yet you do them? Is that compulsion (jabr) or a weakness of resolve? How does Maturidi's argument force you to reconsider whether you truly have free will in those moments? This week, identify one such action and consciously examine whether you could have chosen otherwise.
?
The Mu'tazila believed their view gave humans freedom, but Maturidi demonstrated that it actually implies compulsion. Are you holding onto any belief that, when followed through, leads to a conclusion you reject? Pause and name one area of your life where your reasoning might be inconsistent with your fundamental beliefs about free will and responsibility.
?
If an action occurs despite your hatred and inability to stop it, Maturidi calls it 'jabr'. In your own experience, where do you feel powerless over your own choices? Do you use that feeling as an excuse to avoid accountability? What would it mean to take full ownership of even those actions? Commit to one concrete step this week to assert your agency in an area where you usually feel helpless.

Divine Decree
intermediate

The Husayniyya and the True Jabriyya Are the Mu'tazila
Maturidi defends the Husayniyya and rebuts the Mu'tazila's accusation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazila, not the Husayniyya, are the true Jabriyya.

Core Idea
The accuser may be the one truly deserving of the label.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a classic polemic: the Mu'tazila accused the Husayniyya (followers of Husayn al-Najjār) of being Jabriyya (proponents of compulsion). However, Maturidi argues that the accusation reflects poorly on the accusers. By examining the actual positions of both groups, he shows that the Mu'tazila's own doctrine is closer to Jabr. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and its literal translation to familiarize yourself with the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question near the end.
2
Understand the Husayniyya's position
Identify what the Husayniyya affirm: the servant has power over his current act but not its opposite. They also mention divine grace (luṭf) that Allah withheld.
Contrast with the common misconception of them as pure Jabriyya.
3
Analyze Maturidi's comparison
Maturidi compares the Husayniyya and Mu'tazila on the amount of power and choice they grant to the servant. He finds the Husayniyya give more, so the Mu'tazila's label is baseless.
He also points out the Mu'tazila's own statement about the servant's action occurring whether he wills or not.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the Mu'tazila are more deserving of the label 'Jabriyya' because they deny the servant any power at the time of action, while the Husayniyya affirm some power and choice.
Consider the implications for theodicy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سمّت المعتزلة الحسينية مجبرة بما قالت الحسينية للعبد قدرة ما هو فيه من الفعل، وليست له قدرة ضده وقت الفعل وقبل ذلك الوقت. فالاختلاف بينهم وبين المعتزلة إنما هو في الاسم خاصة، لأن الحسينية تقول: «هو قادر على ما هو فيه، فعند الله لطف لم يعطه»، والمعتزلة يقولون: «لم يبق عند الله شيء فيه صلاحه إلا وقد أعطي». فقد اتفقا على قدر ما أعطاه؛ ولا قوة له وقت الفعل عند المعتزلة، وعند الحسينية له قدرة ما هو فيه، وله اختيار ما هو فيه. فكان الذي معه من القدرة والاختيار أكثر من الذي عند المعتزلة. فكيف سمتهم المعتزلة مجبرة لولا قلة الحياء ؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل عند الحسين أنه عند الفعل مضيّع أحد القدرتين ولا عذر له في التضييع ؛ وعند المعتزلة لا قدرة له لا بالتضييع ولا غيره. فأي الوصفين أشبه بالجبر لو كان ثمة إنصاف ؟ ثم الذي يحقق أن المعتزلة هي المجبرة قولهم : للعبد الفعل شاء العبد أو أبى. ومن زالت عنه المشيئة في فعل فهو ساءٍ أو جاهل أو عاجز لا يخلو عن ذلك. مع ما قد جعلوا للعبد أن يريد في سلطان الله ما لا يريده ويشاء في ملكه ما لا يشاؤه، وهو يشاء خلافه ويريد غيره، وذلك
Then the Mu'tazila called the Husayniyya 'Jabriyya' because the Husayniyya said: 'The servant has power over what he is doing of action, but he does not have power over its opposite at the time of action and before that time.' So the difference between them and the Mu'tazila is only in the name, because the Husayniyya say: 'He is able over what he is doing, but Allah has a grace (luṭf) that He has not given him.' And the Mu'tazila say: 'There remains nothing with Allah that contains his well-being except that it has been given.' So they agreed on the amount He gave; and he has no power at the time of action according to the Mu'tazila, while according to the Husayniyya he has power over what he is doing and has choice over what he is doing. So what he possesses of power and choice is more than what the Mu'tazila have. So how did the Mu'tazila call them Jabriyya, were it not for lack of shame? And there is no power except with Allah. The principle according to the Husayniyya is that at the time of action he is negligent of one of the two powers and has no excuse for the negligence; but according to the Mu'tazila he has no power, neither by negligence nor otherwise. So which of the two descriptions is more similar to Jabr, if there were fairness? Then what confirms that the Mu'tazila are the Jabriyya is their statement: 'The action is for the servant whether he wills or not.' And whoever's will is removed from an action is either evil, ignorant, or incapable, not free from that. Along with what they have assigned to the servant: that he may will in the dominion of Allah what He does not will, and desire in His kingdom what He does not desire, while He wills contrary and desires other than that, and that
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a comparison and final argument. Maturidi explains that the Mu'tazila call the Husayniyya (followers of Husayn al-Najjār) 'Jabriyya' because the Husayniyya hold that the servant has power over the specific act he is doing but not over its opposite. However, Maturidi argues that this accusation is baseless: the Husayniyya actually affirm some power and choice, while the Mu'tazila deny any power at the time of action. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's position is closer to Jabr. He then points out that the Mu'tazila's own statement that the action occurs whether the servant wills or not is a clear admission of compulsion. Thus, the Mu'tazila are the true Jabriyya.

Key Terms
الحسينية (al-ḥusayniyya): the Husayniyya sect
المجبرة (al-mujbira): Jabriyya
اللطف (al-luṭf): divine grace
القدرة (al-qudra): power

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Husayniyya are Jabriyya because they deny all human power.
Correction: Actually, the Husayniyya affirm some power and choice over the current act, while the Mu'tazila deny any power at the moment of action, making the Mu'tazila closer to Jabr.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the Mu'tazila are more deserving of the label 'Jabriyya' than the Husayniyya?
A) The Mu'tazila deny all power to the servant at the time of action
B) The Husayniyya deny all human choice
C) The Mu'tazila affirm complete free will
D) The Husayniyya claim the servant has no power at all

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazila's own position aligns more with Jabr than the Husayniyya's, reversing the accusation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accuse someone else of a fault—whether in belief or action—do you first examine yourself for the same shortcoming? Like the Mu'tazila who called others Jabriyya while holding a more extreme position, where in your life are you quick to judge while ignoring your own greater error? Resolve to check your heart before you speak next time.
?
The Mu'tazila claimed the servant has no power at the moment of action, yet still held him accountable. Do you ever find yourself making excuses for your sins by feeling 'I had no choice,' while secretly knowing you did? Own your choices today—acknowledge both your power and your responsibility before Allah.
?
Maturidi reverses the accusation using the Mu'tazila's own words. Is there a belief you hold dearly that, if examined carefully, might actually contradict itself? What if your defense of free will actually leads to compulsion? Commit to studying the arguments, not just the labels, in your pursuit of truth.

Divine Decree
intermediate

The Sign of Jabr and the Two Types of Jabriyya
Maturidi defines the sign of jabr and distinguishes false from true Jabriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi identifies the sign of compulsion (jabr) and distinguishes between true and false Jabriyya.

Core Idea
Compulsion is defined by the absence of human choice, power, command, and prohibition.

Maturidi begins by defining the sign of jabr, showing that any system of thought that attributes actions to God without genuine human choice is a form of compulsion. He then reveals that some who claim to reject jabr actually implicitly affirm it, while others openly embrace it. This passage sharpens our ability to detect determinism in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the definition of jabr at the start.
2
Identify the two groups
Look for where Maturidi mentions 'false Jabriyya' and 'true Jabriyya'. Note how he distinguishes them.
The false Jabriyya deny being Jabriyya but their statements imply jabr.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for Islamic theology. Think about how one might unintentionally fall into jabr by negating human choice.
Maturidi shows that the sign of jabr is not just explicit denial of free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلامة الجبر هذا. وبهذا الفعل يوجبون العداوة والولاية والخلود في الجنة والنار، الواقع وقت وقوعه بلا اختيار ولا قدرة ولا أمر أيضًا ولا نهى. فمن تأمل ذلك وجده عند التحقيق قول الجبرية في التصريح، لكن هؤلاء جبرية كاذبة، وأولئك جبرية صادقة. ثم من قولهم: إن من أراد الفعل لأقرب الأوقات إليه يقع ذلك الفعل [له] وإن كرهه وأراد صرفه، ويقع له به العداوة والولاية، وإن صار بحيث لا يمكنه الصرف قبل وقوعه أو معه. ثم يكون ذلك الوقت ليس بوقت محال لفوت ذلك الفعل، إذ قد يجوز عندهم فوته بالمنع والقهر. ثبت بما ذكرتُ وقوعه بالجبر في التحقيق. وأيضًا على قولهم في كثرة جرى اسم القدر في غير موضعه على ألسنتهم يسمون به، فهو كذلك عندهم، مع قولهم بنسبة الجبر إلى غيرهم. وبالله المعونة والعصمة.
And the sign of jabr (compulsion) is this. By this act they necessitate enmity and love and eternal abiding in Paradise and Hellfire, occurring at the time of its occurrence without choice, without power, without command, and without prohibition. Whoever reflects on that finds it, upon investigation, to be the statement of the Jabriyya explicitly, but these are false Jabriyya, and those are true Jabriyya. Then from their statements: 'Whoever wills an action for the nearest of times to him, that action occurs for him even if he dislikes it and wills to turn it away, and enmity and love occur for him by it, even if he becomes such that he cannot turn it away before its occurrence or with it.' Then that time is not a time of impossibility due to the loss of that action, for it is possible in their view to lose it by prevention and coercion. It is established by what I have mentioned that its occurrence is by jabr in reality. And also according to their statement, in the frequency of the name 'qadar' (decree) running in other than its proper place on their tongues, they are named by it, and so it is with them, along with their statement attributing jabr to others. And with Allah is the aid and protection.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage presents a definition and classification of jabr (compulsion). Maturidi begins by stating the sign of jabr: the attribution of actions to God without any human choice, power, command, or prohibition. He argues that this leads to a deterministic understanding of reward and punishment. He then distinguishes between two groups: false Jabriyya (who claim not to be Jabriyya but implicitly hold to jabr) and true Jabriyya (who openly affirm it). He supports this by citing the claims of the false group, who assert that an action occurs for a person even if he dislikes it and tries to prevent it. Maturidi concludes that this is a real admission of jabr, as the person has no effective power to prevent the action. He also notes that these people frequently misuse the term 'qadar' (decree) and falsely accuse others of jabr. The passage ends with a plea for divine assistance.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr)
جبرية (Jabriyya)
قدر (qadar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only those who explicitly deny free will are Jabriyya.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even those who claim some power can be Jabriyya if they ultimately negate effective choice.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the sign of jabr?
A) Affirming human power at the time of action
B) Attributing actions to God without human choice, power, command, or prohibition
C) Denying divine predestination
D) Claiming that actions occur by human choice alone

Key Takeaway
The sign of jabr is the negation of human choice and power in the occurrence of actions, and both explicit and implicit forms of this doctrine must be recognized.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself explaining your own sin by saying 'It was written' or 'It was my fate' in a way that relieves you of personal responsibility? How does Maturidi's sign of jabr expose that tendency?
?
If you truly believe that your actions are your own choice, then why do you sometimes attribute outcomes to 'qadar' when they are unpleasant, as if you had no power to change them? Could this be a form of false Jabriyya in your own heart?
?
Maturidi warns that false Jabriyya attribute jabr to others while themselves falling into it. Are there any theological positions you hold that implicitly deny human choice while outwardly affirming it? What would it cost you to examine them honestly?
